THIS Company is being formed to acquire gold minin leases Nos. 1981 e, 2394 e, and 489 e compris-
ing 30 acres and situated near the Red Lake, 13 m es EiN. E. of White: Feather north of and
almost adjoining the well-known General Gordon Mine, block of 12 acres intervening. The latter
block is being privately worked by an English Syndicate; its recent purchasers.
The property has been inspected by Messrs. G. R. F ,rby, M.E. ; N. W. Harper, Manager White
Feather Main Reefs and Robinson Gold Mines, Limited Leslie A. Norman, M.A. I.M.E. ; and C.
Tregear, M.E., whose reports are appended.
It will be seen by reference to these reports that the lode formation is identical with that of the
General Gordon and outcrops on the property. The forn i,tion is a highly ferruginous chlorite schist,
containing vugs of gossany iron, and with numerous quai r. veins running through it carrying very rich
gold. In the General Gordon the formation is 35 feet kvi e ; in the Extended it lias been proved at
over 20 feet wide, with no trace of a second wall, and the rold is equally distributed throughout.
Running along the lode on the east side is a dyke'of granite porphyry, which has apparently eon-
dls, and which slpuld take the lode down with it. This may
centrated the lode within well-defined walls,
be taken as an indication of its permanency in depth.
The Red Lake is reported to have a permanent suppl of water in the driest seasons, suitable for
milling and reachable in easy sinking. There is abundaf eof timber for mining purposes, and as the
railway is now nearing Kalgoorlie the mine is within 24 n [es of the temporary terminus.
.£7500 will be placed to the credit of working capital, jss brokerage of 3d. per share.
An agreement dated 17th day of June, 1896, has 1 en. signed between Randolph Bedford, of
Adelaide, and William Arthur Kingsborough, on beha of the proposed Company, and can be
inspected at the Solicitor’s offices, Pirie-street, Adelaide
Upon the formation of the Company a meeting of he Allottees of Shares will be held for the
purpose of passing the Memorandum and Articles of Ai ociation, and the election of Directors, &c.
Dated at Adelaide, this 20th day of June, 1896.

LITERARY NOTES .
Recent Verse.
Everybody with a nice sense of words and
a jealous regard for standards draws two
distinct lines between
POETRY
“ . . there is a great gulf fixed.”—
Luke xvi., 26.
VERSE.
Yerse is the kind of thing that anyone may
write with a certain amount of culture and a
certain amount of pains : Poetry is the other
kind of thing. And the gulf is always there —
wide, narrow, deep, shallow —still, a gulf.
Poeta nascitur, poetilla fit. Perhaps at su-
preme crises of existence—first love, father-
hood, motherhood, the death of dear ones—the
poetilla may overpass the gulf, distribute sis
obnoxious middle, and for a moment gain
foothold on the farther verge as true Poeta
But this is rare ; and even so one seems to
discern the artificial stimulus, to note the
lack of native spontaneity. It is the differ-
ence between the bubbling spring and the
artesian bore, between beauty unadorned and
beauty manicured, cosmetiqued, and dressed
by Worth. Of the verser’s highest efforts
you can say at once, “This is fine! ” —of the
Poet’s you cannot say it, because you feel it
so. Certainly, after the nuptials, when
satiety has dulled the fragrance of passion, it
becomes possible for the critic to look a
poet in the feet, and point out the Ungraces.
But during the honeymoon, at all events, a
Poet drowns criticism in sympathy.
* * * *
Nevertheless, the proud name of Poet is
arrogated by the meanest maligner of the
rural Muse—the person who desires to know
whether The Bulletin pays for poems in
praise of Miss Jones, of Budgereeboola,
commencing—
To the homes of the sick and the feeble she went;
Undaunted and firm on her purp -se was bent;
The dogs Wert- all glad, they left their old bones
To give a reception to our heroine, Miss .Jones—
and winding up with
The ark wm-ti prepared foretold a great sea;
The angels at odom told Lot for to flee ;
The sea and tin- tempest, that dangerous gale,
Commanded old Jonah, and so did the whale.
So it is pleasant, in the push of pretentious
personalities, to meet a modest little pamph-
let of “Yerse: by James Hebblethwaite,
Hobart,” which yet includes lines whose
melody, and simplicity, and flavour, might
well claim the higher title. Could Heine,
for ewimple, wish a better ballad-begmning
than this :
Once as I lay a-sleeping
Beneath an ancient oak,
There slipt to me a maiden —
One of the sylvan folk.
Pale-sweet her face as moonbeam;
Her hair’s clear dewy tips
Fell on each side in darkness;
She kissed my dreaming lips.
Or this :
Faint winds are sadly sighing
Within the ruined shrine,
The rose of day is dying
To twilight in'the vine.
On bluest ether glowing,
Above a cold greeD steep,
A trembling star is sowing
The seeds of folding sleep.
And is there no Shakespenan grace in this
slight song :
Like the scent of violet,
Subtly sweet with all regret,
Love and Spring they pass away—
Ah me, weli-a-day !
Vesper-bell knells dying beam,
Form and feature fall to dream,
Gone the voice, the love of May—
Ah me, well-a-day !
Sigh we for the sunlight fled,
Sigh wr for the love that’s dead,
Love and violet decay—
Ah me, well-a-day !
* * * *
“A languid bard” Mr. Hebb waite
calls himself in lines addressed “To A. B.
Paterson,” of whom :
None out a poet could have tuned the vague far
overword
That haunts the lines that tell the tale of Conroy’s
travelling herd—
O phantom mob of men and sheep that drift the
north-west way !
My heart still follows in your track along the
Castlereagh.
“The vague far overword”—it is a phrase
which kernels the charm of Mr. Hebble-
thwaite’s own verses. There is a note of
weakness in them ; a sadness as of chill
twilight airs when one camps, solitary, on
some great Western plain : the writer’s pinion
is neither very strong nor very steady, and it
is rare to find eight lines without a flaw :
yet there is somefliing in the sentiment and
cadences which justifies the opinion here
set down—that they are some of the most
essentially poetic verses ever printed in our
country. All carry echoes from the elder
sons of song, yet even in their likeness Mr.
Hebblethwaite’s individuality and variety
are apparent. Already he has brought a
suggestion of Heine, of Shakespeare : is
there not a quaint, decadent, Verlainish
touch in this
The word “ sansculotte,” which the French
Revolution applied in an altered sense, was first
coined by the Abbe Maury, who used it of some
ladies in the gallery of the Assembly. (The
reader will note that the joke about the New
Woman does not follow here as se anticipated.)
“ A Castaway of the Barrier,” by David
J. Falk, of Adelaide, is a fine sample ®f worth-
less wordspinning. A New Caledonian escapee
drifts ashore at Cairns and makes love to a local
girl, who decides she will run away with him, and
then decides she won’t run away with him. Those
are the book’s ideas : the rest is fustian verbiage,
c.fA
It came to her she knew not how or when, in the sough
of the wind, in the breathing of the sea, in the'glamour
of the moonlight, in the radiance of the tropic sun. Her
w arm nature during that week unconsciously imbibed a
new life, quivered with new emolions, trembled to a new
influence; and before she, in her ignorance, knew what
I his freshly-awakened feeling was, she loved, and loved
with all the undisciplined force of her nature.
Blow the undisciplined force of her nature !
Sampson Low, Marston and Co. publish-at
6s. an English edition of J. D. Hennessey’s
“The Dis-Honourable, ” a local novel which con-
tains remarkably plain references to the swindles
of a former Q. governing ring. Mr. Hennessey, a
busy little journalist who was formerly a Wesleyan
clergyman, has three or four other stories in
print or preparing.
;f * * * *
“When I first began journalism,” said
John Morley recently, “ a London morning paper
reviewed a book casually—once in half a year;
now one of its most popular features is a careful
suiyey of all the work done in literature, art, and
music.” And can it be said that the new culture-
width implies loss of depth ?
THE CHILDREN’S MINUET.
Ah ! ah ! this is our holiday ;
Trip it with laughter again ;
Then gracefully bending in inode that is olden
We’ll dance to the minuet’s strain.
Slow ! slow ! silent and courteous —
White satin and powder and rose ;
And sad the refrain that floats dreamily over
Sweet maidens and stately young beaux.
See ! see ! just for a moment
The old world is with you again;
And now they have vanished and we are but
children
That dance to the minuet’s strain.
To quote all the good things would be almost
to quote the booklet: to show Mr. Hebble-
thwaite’s stronger manner take some stanzas
of which, with small alteration, Matthew
Arnold (another comparison !) would not be
ashamed :
ULYSSES.
The wise Ulysses stands upon the stair,
His wave-worn raft sways idly at his feet;
To lift the drooping sail there blows no air,
And all around is stillness, save the beat
Of faintest ripples that make cool the heat
With cool lipped sound, and on the ocean floor
Of weed-grown rock, flit golden shadows fleet,
Uid in the surgy murmurs evermore
Ulysses hears a dirge from happy days of yore.
He sees the grey and windless olive woods
Upon the smooth green shoulder of the hill;
He hears the tumbling of the rough green floods
That smite the shore and all the pebbles thrill
With glad stern motion in the seething mill;
But salt-green surge, green sward, and olive tree
Grow dreamlike, and his eyelids slowly fill,
And fainter falls the rumor of the sea,
For memory’s lingering voice now names Penelope.
In silent sunshine lies eaoh winding street,
Nor is there movement either high or low;
He hears no voice, no sound of any feet,
And all the soft blue air is in a glow
From somedivinest wellspring’s overflow
Of tender bliss, and in the rainbow dew,
And on the statue’s limbs of carved snow,
And on the columned marble’s peachy hue,
The glad young morning light trembles and blooms
anew.
O hush ! speak not! but list that silvery sound
Of flutes, and pipes, and merry clashing din,
And cry of sweet clear voices, floating round
The temple porch—-O, can it be a sin
To think Apollo and liis shining kin
Have left the glittering mount? In colored weeds
They slowly loiter past where keen and thin
The hot light cleaves the ebon shade—who leads
This train of tender youth fresh from the flowery
meads.
The scattered buds a dainty pathway leave,;
From arks and lapfuls that the children bring
Faint haunting gales of sylvan sweetness breathe—
As shyly sweet is one young blue-eyed thing !
The wise Ulysses yearns to feel her cling
About his childless neck, and with them roam
Beneath the rain of garlands, hear them sing
In ravished harmony : “Spring ! Spring ! is come,
Ah ! leave the house of stone, the sweet earth is
your home.”

T. o O K 1
BEFORE YOU
T. E A P t
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout ,a list as long as
(xeorge-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A TELL-TALE TRYST.
Oh 1 who was it saddled White Star, 1 st night,
And who was it saddled White Star
You can read his track to the rails and back
And down the creek ever se far ;
Oh ! moonlight is lovers’ light, somebody knows,
And witch-time the season to woo,
And down in the bend where the kurrajong grows
The tracks have been trodden by two.
Oh ! who was it galloped White Star, last night,
When gold stars jewelled the sky ?
You can see the brand of saddle and band
In sweat that is clotted and dry ;
Oh ! somebody raced, with the world asleep,
To a tryst that somebody knew,
And over the blue-grass fetlock-deep
The white hoofs scattered the dew.
Oh ! who was it fastened White Star, last night,
To a bough of the kurrajong-tree ?
The deep-set grooves of his restless hooves
Are there for the world to see ;
Oh ! somebody left him for true love’s sake,
And somebody left him long,
For horses may hunger and bridles break
When true love fashions her song !
Oh ! who was it fondled White Star last night
When somebody whispered adieu,
And plaited the gray of his mane in a way
That never those gray locks grew ?
And who was it bent from his saddle-bow
To the plea of an upturned face,
While down in the bend where the kurrajongs
grow
The world stood still for a space ?
Oh ! the lover who saddled White Star last night
It is very easy to guess,
For his face is bright with a new-found light
And a joy that his eyes confess.
Oh ! somebody met in the moonlight snow,
Someone that cared to be kissed,
I And the veriest dolt in the world may know
Who rode to the moonlight tryst!
GLE[?]ROWAN.
WHERE THE FLINDERS FLOWS.
(A NORTHERN PASTORAL.)
“ Our magnificent pasture lands on the Flinders .”
—Queenslander.
There’s a land of beef and bovines
(Where the Flinders flows),
There are flocks of fleecy ovines
(Where the Flinders flows);
There’s a land where drought eternal
Only yields to floods infernal—
’Tis that peaceful vale and vernal
(Where the Flinders flows).
There are lands that melt in money
(So the legend goes),
There are lands of milk and honey
(Where they lie —God knows !)
There’s a land where tramping sinner,
Workless trudges, waxing thinner,
Till he makes the crows a dinner
(Where the Flinders flows).
There’s a land of crimson curses
(Where the Flinders flows),
Quite unquotable in verses
(Where the Flinders flows);
There’s a land where summer breaking
Makes you think that hell’s awaking,
And your soul’s commenced a-baking
(Where the Flinders flows).
There’s a land of lies gigantic
(Where the Flinders flows),
There are flies that drive you frantic
(Where the Flinders flows),
There’s a land of dam and dingo,
Beery bushmen, fiery stingo
Bleary blight and lurid lingo,
(Where the Flinders flows).
Upper Flinders.
P. LUFTIG.
JACK CARDUE.
There’s a big soul sails through the dark to-night,
Tried and solid and true ;
A soul that was never afraid to fight—
The soul of Jack Cardue.
His style was never the fancy style,
His creed might not fit you,
And you’d find that you’d struck a raspy file
If you argued with Jack Cardue.
His talk would make clergymen die of shock, (
Though his folks were for “ Church and Light,
And he didn’t invest in teetotal stock,
But, oh ! his heart was white.
He stuck to his folks from birth to grave,
And when burst banks hit him bad
He didn’t go cursing the world or rave
Or otherwise play the cad ;
He didn’t throw up the sponge and weep ;
He didn’t a moment shirk ;
He simply remarked —“Well, boys, less sleep,
And a big bit more of work ! ”
Yet the only epitaph they gave him
When his soul went West by Bast—
Oh ! my arm got hard and my eyes went dim—
Was “ a drunken, selfish beast ! ”•
Jack would always take another s turn,
And he always felt for you,
When the whole wide world seemed to crush and
burn,
And the future looked Prussian-blue,
You had only to say,_“ Here’s a big J pen,
A blotter, a pot of ink,
A cosy fire and a quiet den,
And a bottle of beer to drink!
Ah. I know when his wisp of life was spun,
There flashed through the winter night—
“ St. Peter, Heaven. Heart 1 Lloyd s Ai;
With care; for it’s, oh, so white / ”
And I’ll bet when, after life’s ups and downs,
Jack reaches heavenly things,
He’ll growl if they offer him sapphire crowns,
And haloes and robes and wings,
“ I’ve got no use for those damned fal-lals 5
All I want to give is a spurt
To any of my old earth-time.pals,
And I’ll do it in pants and shirt!
And he’ll turn to the Angel of Records then,
And his ink-marked hand enjoy,
And he’ll sav, “ Here’s at least one brother pen !
Can I give you a lift, old boy ?

SATURDAY, JULY 4, 1896.
The Newcastle Strike.
The Newcastle (N.S.W.) coal-strike has
dragged along for over three months, and, up
to date, there are no prospects save the
prospect that things, which are now bad
enough, may shortly be a great deal
worse. As for the merits of the case,
they were very difficult to sort out, even
at the beginning, and they have grown no
less complex as time went on. Also the
original merits or demerits of the quarrel
have been complicated by the addition of
new factors to the old problem, which problem
was sufficiently aggravated without them.
The first cause of the trouble was undoubtedly
the miserably inadequate wages paid to the
miners, whose condition has been steadily
deteriorating for several years past, until it
has become so wretched as to be almost past
endurance. The cause of the low wages was
partly the greed of the mine-owners and their
hunger for large dividends, hut this consti-
tuted only a small part of the difficulty ; the
owners are doubtless as hungry for big divi-
dends as they ever were, but of late cir-
cumstances have been against them, and their
profits have been comparatively moderate.
The other cause, and by far the more im-
portant one, has been the absurdly low price
of coal, which lias gone down of late to a
figure never before known on the Hunter
Itiver coalfields. The reasons of this exces-
sive cheapness are many and widespread, but
they seem to resolve themselves mainly into
over-production, brought about by a variety
of circumstances.
* * * *
New South Wales turns out annually more
than twice as much coal as the province
requires for local consumption. Therefore it
depends mostly on its export trade. In the
good old days, when coal realised about twice
as much per ton as it does at present, New
South Wales had almost a monopoly of the
Victorian, Californian and Chilian trade, as
well as that of many other countries. There-
fore a stupendous effort was made to open
mines in any and every place where coal was
procurable, and to tear out all the interior of
the province at once and sell it by the ton to
all-comers. In those days it w r as assumed,
as it always is, that the monopoly would last
for ever. No one foresaw that coal-fields
would be developed in Japan, British
Columbia and Victoria, or that Maoriland
would begin to bid for the South American
trade, or that the Jap. would annex Formosa
and bring its coal-measures into the general
competition, or that any of the things which
have happened would ever come to pass.
These results were brought about partly by
mismanagement and partly by the ordinary
course of events. During the good times
that are gone wages and dividends were both
higher than they now are, and both miners
and owners kicked on smaller provocation.
The owners tried to reduce wages on the
ground that they were not getting sufficient
profits at a time when their profits were so
large that the shareholder of to-day would
regard them as gorgeous affluence ; and
disastrous strikes were the result. The
miners demanded more wages when their
pay was higher than the highest figure they
now hope for, and more disastrous strikes
were the consequence. The owners certainly
were the chief offenders, but there was mis-
management and bad judgment on both
sides.
The natural consequence of all this was
that prices were high. Freights both by sea
and rail were also high in those days. The
former were high from various causes ; the
latter because the railways had to charge
heavy rates to make interest on the exorbi-
tant cost of construction (mainly brought
about by land-resumptions at absurd rates)
and to make up for extravagant management.
Therefore, coal was enormously expensive to
the distant consumer, and this led to the
eager search for coal-measures in Gippsland
and elsewhere. The Victorian discoveries
wrecked New South Wales’s best foreign
market, for, although large quantities of coal
still go to Victoria, it is at a very different
price from that realised before southern com-
petition set in. The Gippsland fields would,
doubtless, have been opened up sooner or
later in any case—but the high prices charged
by the New South Wales owners certainly
hurried on their development. The same
causes helped along the competition of Japan
and Maoriland and British Columbia. Now
the great and profitable monopoly is practi-
cally at an end, and nothing that can be done
is.ever likely to re-establish it. Also, China
may have to be reckoned with in the future
as another element of disaster. China lias
deposits of coal that are, by all report, prac-
tically illimitable, and has cheaper labor than
even Japan ; and if the European gets a firm
footing in that country, which he seems cer-
tain to do sooner or later, and opens up its
coal-fields, which also seems certain sooner
or later, the competition, which is sufficiently
active now, will grow even more severe.
* * * *
Cheapness, according to the Foreign-trade
doctrine, is the great desideratum—the chief
purpose and end of man—the cause of all
prosperity—the thing to be aimed at above
all others. Yet in this case the benefit is
not at all apparent. The cheapness of New
South Wales coal has reduced the profits on
the capital engaged in the industry, and has
caused a lot of capital—invested in mines
which were not well-adapted for cheap work-
ing—to yield no interest at all, while the
principal is abandoned to rust and decay.
It has thrown many coal-miners out of work,
and has starved the rest, along with their
wives and families. Through them it has
helped to starve the shopkeepers and others
who live by supplying the miners with neces-
saries, and their families, and the people
who are dependent on them, and the families
of these dependents. And, though coal lias
become cheaper to the consumer, this cheap-
ness has not led to the creation of new in-
dustries on any scale worth mentioning, nor
to any perceptible alleviation of the general
poverty, nor has it conferred much visible
benefit on anybody. The Foreign-trade
policy certainly did not create the cheapness
of coal—it arrived through circumstances
with which no fiscal system was actively con-
cerned ; but the fact that its arrival has done
harm rather than good casts a serious doubt
on the beautiful doctrine of cheapness in all
its branches.
* * * *
The present situation looks about as hope-
less as it very well can he. The coal in-
dustry, apparently, can’t pay living wages at
the prices now prevailing. It certainly could
pay higher wages than it does, but even at the
best it couldn’t at present pay such wages as
would enable the miner and his family to
maintain a decent standard of existence.
If the prices are raised to any extent
worth mentioning, in the face of the
foreign competition which now exists, and
the lower wage paid in many of the compet-
ing countries, and the fact that many of
them are more conveniently situated in the
matter of markets, it would seem that the
foreign trade, which constitutes more than
half of the business, would vanish altogether.
In one sense, of course, there is no visible
reason why it should not be allowed to
vanish. There is no special cause why New
South Wales should break its heart and
spend its immortal soul in tearing all its
visible coal-supply out of the ground imme-
diately, and selling it abroad at ruinous
prices at this particular juncture. The coal
will be as good 100 or 200 or 500 years
hence ; it will all be wanted in course of
time to supply the wants of Australia’s
groiving population, with its possibilities of
almost unlimited increase ; and by dragging
it out of the earth in such red-hot haste
just now Australia is only hastening the ad-
vent of a possible period when it may have
to buy coal abroad to make up for the d ;ple-
tion of its own resources. If the price was
put up to a figure which would cover decent
wages to the- workers and moderate interest
on the capital invested, the foreign trade
might go, but the local demand ought to
supply a respectable rate of pay to perhaps
half the miners now employed and leave the
aggiegate owner no worse off than lie is
at present. Unfortunately, the half of the
mining community which would thus he
thrown out would he driven by abso-
lute want to under-cut the other half. There
is no other industry which can absorb them
and pay even the wretched wages they now
earn. There is no prospect, in this era when
foreign imports are making the local worker
more and more superfluous, of any such in-
dustry arising. There is no prospect, either,
of any manufactures starting to increase the
local coal-demand, because the tendency of
the Foreign-trade policy is to wipe out. local
manufactures for the encouragement of pro-
tected German imports brought here cheaply
per subsidised European steamers. More-
over, if the price was raised to such a rate as
would yield decent profits and decent wages,
not only would the foreign trade he lost, but
it remains to he seen whether, under the
new.conditions, and by virtue of the great
Free-raid policy, some subsidised foreigner
might not begin to compete even in the
Sydney market.
* * * *
To sum the situation up in a few sentences :
There are more coal-mines and miners in New
South Wales than the industry, under present
conditions, cm support. There is no pro-
spect of a diminution in the number of miners,
for there is no other employment for them,
and even the meanest wage is a shade better
than absolute starvation. There is no pro-
spect of a diminution in the number of mines,
because the capitalist regards even 2 per
cent, profit, gained by further overcrowding
an already glutted m irket and further starv-
ing his already starving employes, as better
than no profit at all. There is no immediate
prospect of a rise in prices which will alle-
viate the distress, because foreign competi-
tion is apparently becoming more instead of
less aggravated, and the great importing
policy does nothing to encourage a local
demand. The Newcastle miners struck for
higher wages, which course was fully justified
by their truly miserable situation. The
owners refused, which course was partly jus-
tified in some cases by the smallness of their
present profits. They offered at first to let
the miners resume work at the old, abject
rate of pay, and the offer was rejected ; now
that the employes are willing to accept it the
owners demand the practical abolition of the
unions, which is wholly unjustifiable, and a
further reduction of wages on the ground
that the strike has driven away much of what
trade remains, and made the previously bad
state of things even worse. What truth
there may he in this assertion no one really
knows, but it certainly looks like a brutaliy-
unfeeling attempt to take advantage of an
opponent’s distresses to the very utmost.
The depression which prevailed before the
strike is an advantage to the owners and a
tremendous handicap to the men. It practi-
cally beggared the unions, so that there are
no funds to keep the strikers from absolute
starvation. And whereas the owner, who
was making 50 per cent, on his capital in the
old days, was in red-hot haste to get his
mine at work again because the loss of 50
per cent, profit was a serious matter, the
owner who now makes perhaps 5 per cent,
doesn’t care so much because the loss is a
much smaller affair.
There is no prospect of bringing about a
satisfactory settlement by State interference,
because no amount of State interference can
alter the question of Japanese and other
competition abroad, and it is on the foreign
trade that the coal industry has principally
depended. Also, no interference which is
compatible with the principles of a Foreign-
trade Government would improve the local
demand, or find other employment for the
superfluous miners. There is no apparent
solution in the idea of making the coal-mines
State property. Even if the State took them
over at a reasonable valuation, and only
required them to provide, over and above
working expenses, 5 per cent, interest and
sinking-fund on the amount invested, it
could not apparently pay a white man’s wage
to the workers in the present circumstances,
unless it was content to make coal-mining a
subsidised industry, and charge the shortage
to the revenue. The problem moves in a
vicious circle, and brings up against a dead
wall everywhere. It had its beginning away
back further than most people now alive can
remember, and its end, apparently, is in the
future further away than anyone can foresee.
The Freetrade theory in such cases is t hat,
when an industry doesn’t pay, the people—
or, at least, the superfluous people—engaged
in it should resort to “something else.”
Unfortunately, there is nothing else, and no
prospect of it. In all the industrial history
of New South Wales there is nothing more
piteous than the present situation of the
Newcastle miners—and nothing more hope-
less.
The Black Sheep of Australia.
In a recent pamphlet on the subject of Federa-
tion, J. C. Drake, a Q. politician who, with
apparently many of a leader’s qualities, has
hitherto somehow failed to become more than a
nominal leader, emphasises The Bulletin’s
expectation that the existing Queensland Govt,
ring would never consent to federate upon a
Democratic basis. That expectation is now
realised. In terms of the resolution of last
year’s Premiers’-eonference, the Parliaments of
New South Wales, Vic! >ria, S.A. and Tasmania
have passed Acts providing that the whole
body of provincial electors shall choose repre-
sentatives to a Convention for drafting anew a
Federal Constitution. Queensland, the black
sheep of Australia, the tainted wether of the
provincial flock, proposes that its delegates to
the task of building the Australian nation
shall have a unique distinction—they shall be
elected, not by the people, not by the electors,
but by its corrupt and Govt.-ridden House of
Assembly.
* * * *
It is true that, at the Premiers’-couference at
Hobart, on Jan. 31, ’95, Queensland Premier
Nelson voted for the carried resolution pro-
viding that representatives of each province
should be “ directly chosen by the electors.” It
is true that, on his return to Sydney, Premier
Nelson told the Sydney Daily Telegraph that
We were all agreed that the Convention should be
elected by popular vote. ... I don’t think we’re
likely to get a better Convention than by popular vote.
It is true that, on his return to Brisbane,
Premier Nelson told the Brisbane Courier
that he
had agreed to the election of delegates to the Federation
Convention, all present rights and privileges being
reserved to the electors,
and that, as that journal states, he gave it no
reason to suppose that he “ favoured the elec-
tion of delegates otherwise than by a national
vote.” By Premier Nelson, however, and by
the people who manage Premier Nelson, these
little discrepancies between past and present
are considered of no importance.
* » * +
Yet the conditions of the question have not
changed meanwhile. The necessity for popular
suffrage is obvious. No Australian House of
Assembly has been elected primarily or chiefly
to deal with Federation. Probably Federation
had no influence whatever upon the majority of
electors when choosing between candidates. In
Queensland, certainly, not one member of
Assembly was elected at the recent contest
because of his supposed fitness to deal with the
Federal subject. That contest hinged entirely
on local issues—Govt. v. Labor, party v. prin-
ciple, bribery v. no-bribery ; and so on. It is
absurd to say that persons chosen to deal with
purely local questions should become supreme
arbiters of the people’s wishes on national ques-
tions. With more reason Premier Nelson
might argue that Parliament should be elected
by the municipal councils.
* * * *
But the necessity for the change of front is
also obvious. Nobody supposes that Premier
Nelson is personally dishonest, but he works
in a dishonest political groove. At Hobart he
was out of his groove, and naturally saw the
right and chose it. When he returned to Bris-
bane, and got back in his groove, his managers
showed him that he had blundered. “ Our
attitude towards the people,” they reminded
him, “ is distrust tempered by fear. By popu-
lar election of Federal-Convention representa-
tives we gain nothing, yet stand to lose
everything. Quite conceivably the people may
return a majority of representatives to pledge
Queensland for the white man. We are pledged
to Queensland for the black man. National
union, popular progress, a white Australia, a
homogeneous race—these are figments. Cheap
colored labor is a reality. What asses we should
be to throw away the substance for the
shadow ! ” And Premier Nelson looked, and,
behold, he saw that it was good.
* * * *
It may seem strange that a Govt, which has
just returned triumphantly from a general elec-
tion should dread another reference to the people.
But the Govt, knows that the election was won
literally by the smell of an overdraft. It was
won, as The Bulletin has pointed out, by elec-
toral fraud, by electoral cajolery, by electoral
intimidation, by the employment of every dis-
honorable electoral artifice. Fraud is an un-
canny tool—you can never tell when it will
turn in your hand—and the Government
knows it. The existing Q. Parliament is
thoroughly misrepresentative of the people,
and the Government knows that too. No-
where has the gentle art of “ weeding the
rolls ” reached higher perfection than in Queens-
land. Here is a little table based on figures in-
judiciously supplied by Brisbane Courier, the
Govt, organ, with the approximate increase of
population credited monthly instead of yearly :
Date. Adult On Dis- Percent- Percent-
Males.* Rolls, fran- ajre on age Dis-
chised. Rolls, franchised
Dec., 1892 114,423 98,065 16,358 86 14
Election, 1893 115,386 86,983 28,403 75 25
Election, 1896 126,963 86,878 40,085 69 31
*Capable of voting— i.e., excluding Chinese, Poly-
nesians, and other Asiatic races ; prisoners, lunatics, and
members of charitable institutions.
An attractive little table, truly. The “purifi-
cation of the rolls ” entered on by Govt, at the
close of ’92 resulted in disfranchising 11,082
democratic voters in time for the election in
April, ’93, and the Govt, returned victorious.
By strict attention to business, Govt, actually
succeeded in reducing the number of voters
still further by the election of ’96, though the
adult male population increased by 11,577 in
the interim ! The nett result was that nearly
one-third of the adult male population was dis-
franchised, and Govt, returned to power again,
as it boasts, “stronger than ever.” It certainly
smells stronger than ever.
* * ♦ *
To a certainty, eight out of ten of the dis-
franchised electors are democrats; and the
wonder, under the circumstances, is not that
the Labor party did not do more, but that it
did so much. Here is a table of votes polled
at last election, based on Brisbane Courier
figures:—
82,298 votes in all elected 66 members in all (6 seats un-
contested).
41,836 Ministerial votes elected 37 members —average,
1130 votes per member.
29,268 Labor votes elected 19 members—average, 1540
votes per member.
7,062 Opposition votes elected 6 members—average, 1177
votes per member.
4,132 Independent votes elected 4 members—average,
1033 votes per member.
Thus the average Labor member represents 410
votes, or nearly half as many again, more than
the average Ministerial member ! If the Labor
party had its due share of representation on
the Ministerial basis, it would hold 26 seats in


stead of the 19 won at the polls. The Govt,
majority of seats represents a minority of votes,
and the Govt, knows it.
* * * *
For it must be remembered that the Courier
has cooked these figures by the inclusion
of contingent votes, which did not influence
a sinr/le election. And why cooked ? Because
nearly all the contingent votes given were given
for Govt, candidates : the Labor-electors did
not use them. Also, the Govt, tally is swollen
probably 25 per cent, by property-votes : the
Labor tally represents men. The actual number
of men who voted for the Govt, at last election
was probably under 30,000, out of an adult male
population of 127,000, whose will it professes
to represent ; and this is why Q. Govt, will
not trust the people to elect delegates to the
Federal Convention.
» * * *
The duty of Australia is clear. On the ques-
tion of the exclusion of aliens there must be
no compromise; and to the Federal Govt, must
be reserved absolute control. If Queensland
wishes to emulate the slave States of America,
we will reckon with Queensland as an enemy of
the nation. Let her stay without the Common-
wealth until she can elect to speak for her a
Government which is white inside.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Fiscal Referendum.
That, in the opinion of this House, the commercial and
financial unrest consequent upon the uncertainty of the
tenure of any fiscal policy calls for the immediate taking
of a plebiscite vote upon the advisability of the present
policy remaining unaltered for a period of five years ; that
in the event of a majority declaring themselves against
the present policy, and in favor of a protective tariff, im-
mediate action be taken to adjust the fiscal policy in
accordance with the wishes of the majority of the people.
—Motion by M‘Go wen, M.L.A., in N.S.W. Assembly.
The above motion, which, at time of writing,
was in a comatose condition on N.S.W.
Assembly notice-paper, suggests a lot of dis-
jointed reflections. No one knows what the
sentiments of New South Wales on the
fiscal issue, or any other single issue, really
are; political questions have been so mixed
up with each other, and with Parkes’s
hair, Dibbs’s <£looo and his K.C.M.G.-ship,
Lyne’s friend Waller, the Broken Hill strike,
the sectarian row, the 1893 crisis, and other
tilings, that there never has been a straight vote
on any tiling. The S.A. Referendum laid the
ghost of sectarian education, for the time at
least; it would be worth trying if a similar
device might nob, for a time, lay the liscal
ghost in N.S.W. AH the same there is no
special reason why this particular question
should be introduced now more than at any
other time, unless M‘Gowen, M.L.A., begins
to have qualms about his conduct in helping to
bring in Foreign-trade, and wants to lift the
load of responsibility off his shoulders on to
those of the people. And there is no reason
why there should be a fiscal referendum only ;
what the country wants is a general power
of referring important questions to a direct
popular vote. And there is also no reason
why the Referendum should settle the fiscal
question for five years more than for any
other period ; the vote of the male adult
population of to-day can’t bind the 60,000
or so of voters who will come of age
within the next five years, and the population
can’t prohibit itself from changing its mind
between now and 1901. All that can be done
is to enact that, when a question is once
decided by popular vote, no further referendum
shall be taken upon it for a certain period, and
five years is probably as good a period as any
other. Further, when Parliament starts con-
sidering M'Gowen’s motion it should at the
same time seriously ponder the question of the
election of Ministers by the Legislature. Ob-
viously, if the popular vote went for Protection,
Reid, the Freebrade apostle, is not the best
man to carry out the will of the nation in this
matter with any thoroughness or energy, or
to do his best to make the protective policy a
success. Yet the people don’t necessarily want
to exchange Reid for the other alternative,
Lyne, because they want a generally democra-
tic policy, and they strongly suspect Lyne
of being anything but democratic. The
spectacle of the people having a popular vote
and being only able to use it for the purpose of
ordering Reid to enact a Protectionist tariff, or
Lyne to establish a Single-tax regime, would
be a vast absurdity. If the people are to defi-
nitely dictate the items of the national policy,
they should also have a more distinct voice in
deciding who should carry out their policy.
At present, if the nation happens to want
Sleath and John Haynes and Nicholas
Willis as the principal parts of the Ministry,
it has no direct or even indirect means of say-
ing so. Moreover, if the fiscal ghost is referred
directly to the people, which is certainly the
best tiling to do with it, the question is not
merely one of “ the present fiscal policy ” on
one side and Protection on the other. It is the
triangular question of absolute Freetrade, which
New South Wales has never had ; and absolute
Protection, which it can hardly be said to have
ever had ; or a Revenue Tariff, which it has had
practically all the time and which the Reid
Government proposes to maintain with varia-
tions. The man who tries to reduce the fiscal
question to tw o issues doesn’t even begin to
understand what that question is.
Tne Melbourne Banking Prosecutions.
It is announced that the Victorian Government
intends to criminally prosecute certain of the
people who ran the late lamented City of Mel-
bourne Bank on the rocks. Undoubtedly, con-
sidering the awful smellsomeness of the defumt
institution, this is the right thing to do, if only
for the purpose of extracting more information
as to what became of the capital and oilier
assets which weren’t there when they were
wanted. Also it is the right thing to do, if
only to impress upon the public the awful need
of that new Companies Act which the many
directors of the Leg. Council so enthusiastically
rejected last session. But it is worth while
noting—so that the public won’t feel any more
disappointed than is absolutely necessary if the
acquittal comes along as usual, and so that no
>ne need profess to regard that acquittal, if
it comes, as a triumphant vindication of every-
body’s character —that in the present awful
state of Victorian and other law, quite a lot of
the things which wrecked the C.O.M. Bank
seem to be perfectly legal. The bank burst
partly because the late manager was allowed by
the board to have a huge overdraft without
security, and there is no law against that. It
got into difficulties partly also because the
directors, or some of them, lent the money of the
shareholders and depositors to each other on
insufficient security, and there is no law against
that either. It fooled away some more of its
customers’ money in buying its own shares to
prevent a panic on the market, and thereby
diminished its customers’ security by reducing
the callable capital, and that doesn’t seem to be
illegal as the law now stands —and as the Leg.
Council keeps it standing. The balance-sheet
made the dreadful bank appear, at a superficial
glance, to be perfectly sound ; it said that the
coin on hand, the capital, the stock of deben-
tures, and the amounts owing were so much,
which they were ; and it put down the biggest
asset —the one on which everything depended
as “ Advances £4,222,648,” which they were.
It didn’t mention that between <£1,000,000 and
.£2,000,000 of the advances were bad, nor did
it say definitely that they were good, and
apparently the law doesn’t clearly state that
this kind of concealment is unlawful at least,
it isn’t stated with such clearness as to be abso-
lutely definite. The auditors gave their certifi-
cate that the balance-sheet was correct, and so
apparently it was in the strictly legal sense ; it
said that the advances were £4,222,648, which
was a sad and dreadful fact, and it doesn t
appear at all certain that anything more is
demanded of an auditor. In a general way
it is understood by law that the directors
and the auditors and the balance-sheet
are to show the people interested the affairs of
the co., but whether the affairs of the co. were
or were not sufficiently shown by the true
statement that the advances were £4,222,648
doesn’t seem to be clearly specified by any
statute. If the balance-sheet had alleged that
the profit or loss shown was realised “ after
providing for bad and doubtful debts ” there
might have been a direct misrepresentation,
but the two last sheets, at all events, wisely
left out this statement, which is also left out of
a good many other documents of the same kind.
There is no law against leaving out this essential
statement. In a large, general, promiscuous way
there undoubtedly was gross misrepresentation
or gross negligence or gross fraud by somebody
—whether by the persons accused or by some
other persons—but it is a painful fact that
quite a number of the doings which ruined the
City of Melbourne Bank, and yet kept the
dreadful deficit from appearing in the balance-
sheet, are not specifically illegal—or not nearly
so much so as they should be. The Companies
Law is about the loosest and most rotten thing
in Victoria just now, and the Leg. Council is
keeping it that way.
How tlie Sharks Stick Together.
The Victorian inferior courts are just now all
at sea by reason of a recent decision by Chief
Justice Madden. Formerly there was a law in
Victoria by which, when a common or lay
citizen laid an information against anybody,
only the informant or his attorney or his ex-
pensive barrister could conduct the case before
the court of petty sessions. This was a
pleasant and profitable arrangement for the
attorney and the barrister, as the informant, in
most cases, lacked the legal knowledge to run
the case himself, and had to hire a costly limb
of the law to assist him. Then a custom arose
by which constables and police-inspectors, if
the case was a matter of public interest,
used to take it out of the hands of
the informant, provided he was agree-
able, and run the prosecution themselves.
This answered sufficiently well, as the ordinary
“cop” knows about enough law for petty
sessions purposes, and it saved much money,
and prevented many cases being dropped for
want of legal assistance. Also, it aggravated
the legal brigade considerably. Next, an Act
was passed authorising any member of the
police force to prosecute in the case of any
breach of the Police Offences Act, which seems
sufficiently comprehensive for all purposes.
Recently a number of tote-men were informed
upon by a private person ; the police prose-
cuted according to custom ; and the result was
a conviction of the entire batch. And now
Chief Justice Madden has turned the
whole lot loose on appeal, his reason
being that though the Act says police-
men may prosecute in any breach of a
certain law it doesn’t really mean that; it only
means that he may prosecute if he is also the
informant. There was no necessity, of course,
for such a law as Mr. Madden alleges this to
be, for the policeman had the power to serve
as informant and prosecutor without it, there-
fore it would have been just as reasonable to
pass an Act authorising the “trap” not to
interfere with the sun rising at the usual hour
in the morning. Also the limitation which
Mr. Madden put into the Act isn’t there ; the
clause is as comprehensive as any Legislature
could well make it, and about as clear as the
Australian language allows. But the C.J. is a
lawyer. The Vic. lawyers have long protested
against the police prosecution system which
deprives them of so much business and so many
fees. Every lawyer, from long habit of cursing
about his lost fees, has come to regard the
police interference as a lawless outrage, and
when Mr. Madden came to look at the matter
from a new point of view, lie seemingly found
the old point of view too strong for him. The
C.-J’s jerry-built decision will probably be law
in Victoria until overset by Act of Parliament,
and private informants who can’t hire lawyers
to run their cases are hereby warned off the
grass.
Agent-General Saul Samuel deserves cordial
thanks for having administered a long-deserved
snub—a smack with the flat of his tongue
to that utterly irresponsible clique of self-ad-
advertising Bigheads, self-styled the “ Colonial
Party ” in the British Commons. Australia
knows of no “ Colonial Party in the British
Legislature. The ’Ogans and the ’Enniker
’Eatons and the ’Oward Vincents have not a
feather’s weigh* of influence with, nor a
feather’s value of interest in, Australia as Aus-
tralia. They merely regard Australia as an
appanage of J. Bull. They are not a “Colo-
nial Party,” but a “Bull-Barnacle Party.”
Their attempted interference with Austra-
lian affairs is an impertinence, and would
be an insult if it ever produced any
result but wind. The “Three Tailors of
Tooley Street ” were much more reason-
ably “the Citizens of London” than is
the “ Colonial Party ” any shadow of Australia.
It is self-appointed and self-delegated. It holds
no pretence of warrant from Australia. It
would he just as unreasonable and not more im-
pudent for a clique of N.S.W. Parliamentarians
to squad themselves into “ the British Imperial
Party,” and attempt to advise or coerce the
British Commons according to the clique’s
ideas of the necessity for new self-advt. It
would be far less unreasonable, and not half so
impudent, for the writer to advertise himself as
“Baron of London,” and promulgate from the
Woolloomooloo Fislnnarket a “ Code of Rules
for the Guidance of the London County
Council.” London could be splendidly governed
from Woolloomooloo, provided that somebody
woiJ.d furnish the banquets and liquors without
which all good Government is impossible.
The collapse of the motion of censure in N.S. W.
Parliament last week was a foregone conclusion.
The cause of the trouble was the glaringly ex-
travagant payments in connection with the
Dean Commission, and probably three-fourths
of the House would gladly have voted that
these payments were utterly disgraceful. But,
though this was the question ostensibly before
the House, the question that hung on the vote
was whether it would have Reid or Lyne for
Premier. And the majority had to vote against
its conscience that the payments in question
were perfectly right, as the only way of voting
that it didn’t want Lyne as Premier—which is
just as logical as if it had to vote that Fer-
guson’s bull-dog was a retriever as the only
way of declaring that it didn’t think John See
was a success as Treasurer. Party Government
is built like that, somehow.
Any legislation on Employers’ Liability for
negligence is imperfect (writes a democratic
Victorian lawyer to The Bulletin) without the
extension of the time during which notices of
action, &c., may be given in order to enable an
injured person to sue. As a matter of right, no
such notices should be exacted at all. They
are not required in ordinary actions for negli-
gence, and it was a deliberate piece of class
legislation to insert them when this Act was
passed. Morally, the employer should be liable
for injuries caused to a workman through the
negligence of a fellow-workman in the same
way as he would for injuries similarly caused
to a member of the public. But a silly decision
of a bench which couldn’t conceive the
possibility of its being just that a butler
should have the right to recover for in-
juries arising from the negligence of the
cook, has settled the matter otherwise. Par-
liament has made timid reforms in a few
cases, but has spoiled even this patchwork by
the provisions as to notice. It often happens,
on account ot the care, anxiety, and confusion
inseparable from the accident which may
have deprived the bread-winner of his
limbs, to say nothing of the common ignor-
ance of the technical details of the law,
that a lawyer is not even instructed till
after the time for giving notice has expired.
The plausibility of the employer, studiously
careful not to commit any admission to writing,
often helps also to cause delay in inquiry. In
fact, the technicalities of the present Victorian
law on this subject are so many that a cunning
employer caii, and often does, trap his maimed
workmen by palaver or apparent charity into a
false position. The whole subject needs
thorough revision, and will never be on a satis-
factory footing until it is provided in one simple
clause, without notices, limit of damages, or
anything else, that workmen shall have the
same rights against the negligent employer as
members of the public. At present workmen
are treated as if they were a serf class. Again
Vic. Labor members are warned, if that’s any
use, that a sudden dissolution of the present
Parliament would leave them without even the
present Act.
Sydney Telegraph of June 8 :
To instance the United States as an example of what a
thorough and general application of the Protectionist
principle to national production can do is to err through
design or through want of knowledge. The whole foreign
trade of the Republic, including specie, amounts, accord-
ing to most recent figures, to £207,000,000 per annum.
The internal trade, as represented by incomes, is
£2,500,000,000. It will thus be seen that the trade done
in the home market represents more than 91
per cent, of the whole. Of the remainder, only a
small part, less than half, according to one authority,
is subject to any influence from the protective duties.
The United States can fairly be regarded as one of the
greatest freetrade countries the world has ever seen.
From north to south, from coast to coast, there is not a
bar to the free exchange of products between States
whose characteristics and natural adaptabilities differ
widely. Great Britain has no such free market to
buy and sell in as the seventy millions of the people
who constitute the population of the United States.
It would be extraordinary indeed if with such varied and
magnificent natural resources, and with such freedom,
the people there could not stand for a long time ag-ainsb
the evil effects of artificial scarcity and dearness produced
by a monopolistic tariff, which can only influence about
five per cent, of its trade.
Query : Why has the Teleguaph Been wailing
off and on for years past about the evils of
Protection in U.S. since, after all, the U.S. is
“one of the greatest Freetrade countries the
world has ever seen ” ?
Melb. Argus touches the bedrock of crawlsome
and wicked imbecility in raising a subscription
for hard-up Chow market-gardeners, the unfor-
tunate washerwomen forced out of employment
by the compatriots of these cabbage-uoercers
being meanwhile left to the kindly operation
of the Vagrancy Act, or, if they happen to be
young, to the pleasing occupation of prostitu-
tion. Nobody ever sees a starving Chow, for
the simple reason that every sane and “ deserv-
ing ” Celestial is seen through his period of
tribulation by his clan or secret society, to which
he subscribes during good times. The clan is
no benevolent asylum ; it is conducted on hard
and fast lines, standing no nonsense, and firing
out skulkers and malingerers with much velie-
mence; still, apart from the insane, no Chow
willing and able to work is ever without a meal
or bed. This worship of colored races is essen-
tially an English weakness, and the Argus is
English, not Australian. Nigger-butclier Stan-
ley’s black-boy, brought from Uganda, com-
plained bitterly on his arrival in Amer ica. “In
England,” he pleaded, “ I was kissed by duch-
esses all day ; here they say, ‘ Git out, you gaw-
dam little nigger. ’ ”
Melville, the alleged radical of Vic. fat-man
House, deals out statistics in the Address in
Reply debate: —
Since he entered the Council it had passed 719 hills, and
had absolutely rejected only 25 ! If they had passed
those 25 as well, the country would have good reason for
saying that there was no need for an Upper House at
all. (“ Hear, hear,” and laughter.)
Accepting Melville’s figures, the inference is
right enough. If a non-representative Council
obstructs a representative Assembly, it should
be abolished. If it merely acquiesces to every-
thing, it should be abolished. Also, if it emas-
culates every Assembly measure, and farcically
“ passes” a maimed Bill —it should be abolished.
It isn’t necessary, by the way, to “ absolutely
reject” a bill, and that is very seldom done;
the measure is simply shelved, or dropped, or
postponed, or ordered to be read that day
six months. The Council has no need to
“ absolutely reject ” bills. Moreover, the Vic.
Legislative Council is an adept at emascula-
tion. The Factories Bill wrecked last session
was technically “passed” by the Council, and
rejected by the Assembly. But the “ permit
clause ” for out-workers was so tinkered by the
Council as to be ridiculous, and the punishment
ot offending employers was reduced to a mini-
mum. Nevertheless, the Bill was “ passed
and defeated. The Council will get the Bill
again, and will probably make some slight
“ concession ” with the haughty air of the
superior person —which the Assembly will ac-
cept if its present mood continue.
Thirty constituents of James Balfour have
held a meeting to encourage the re-election of
that undesirable saint to Vic. Leg. Council.
The Bulletin would like to feel sure that
those 30 earnest Balfourians are related by
blood or marriage to the 49 abandoned crea-
tures who voted for Tom Bent down at South
Melbourne the other week. The possibility of
Bent and Balfour, and such like public per-
sons, having free and independent supporters
scattered all over the surface of Victoria is very
disquieting. Balfour’s past record and his
terriblereverence for the Sabbath are sufficiently
strong reasons why no constituency should
send him to Parliament to assist in blocking
the Companies Act Amendment Bill any fur-
ther. But an even stronger reason for abolish-
ing the sour-faced saint lies in James Service’s
“ unqualified praise of Mr. Balfour’s piiblic
acts and private character.” Service presided
at tlie meeting aforesaid, and had the magnifi-
cent assurance to state that if, by any mis-
chance, Balfour happens to gets left out of the
Vic. Leg. Council at the ensuing batch of elec-
tions, the country will suffer a heavy loss. The
country which would feel the loss of Balfour
must lie in a bad way.
Premier Turner engineered the opening scenes
of the new Vic. session of Parliament in
characteristic fashion. Governor’s address,
address in reply, the mover’s speech—were all
as mild as milk. Turner goes warily, like a
wily solicitor. Last session ended with
sulphurous threats at the Council. Turner
doesn’t know anything about that. He doesn’t
know that this session must go far to settle the
position of the Council. It he were the
teacher of a ladies’ college he could not
know less. The Opposition looked for an
opening, but could find nothing. The Labor
Corner, aching for a fight, and listening for
trumpet-blasts, rub their eyes in amazement at
Turner’s harmlessness. All the recess there
havebeen mass-meetings denouncing the Council,
the Labor members have breathed blood and
slaughter, members of the Opposition have
denounced the denouncers of the Council.
Turner blinks amiably through his spectacles
and knows nothing about any row. The As-
sembly has its work to do. Turner never
heard of the Council. But if the Council
assaults inoffensive citizens’ bills .
The identification of James Butler, the Maori-
land recediviste, by two Melbourne citizens who
allege robbery-under-arms, was a disgraceful
farce. Instead of being placed among 10 or 20
others, Butler was simply taken from his cell
and confronted with each “ victim ” in succes-
sion. Even then neither of the men was able
to say more than that he was the same sort of
a ruffian, and, “ to the best of their belief,” lie
was the man. The value of this identification
may be gauged by reference to the stories re-
lated by the injured parties at the dates of the
crimes. The first then told the police that he
did not get a good view of his assailant’s face,
but he would “know his voice anywhere”;
yet at the “ identification ” Butler’s voice was
left severely alone. The second stated that his
footpad wore a false beard, and that it was
quite dark at- the time, also that the interview
lasted only a few seconds. And yet the much-
qualified recognitions of these men are trum-
peted forth to the world as “ identifications ! ”
Employers’ liability reform is on the board
in S.A., according to the Governor’s speech. Par-
liament should take care that any sections dealing
with the rights of seamen under this Act are
within the powers of the legislature. The present
sections of the S.A. Act on this subject are, almost
certainly, ultra vires as to the majority of the ships
trading with Adelaide, and this may vitiate the
sections when they come to be applied to the rest.
From a survey of all the Australasian Acts, one is
forced to the conclusion that nothing but the legis-
lation of a federated Australasia, or the provisions
of an Imperial Enabling Act, will do much to
protect sailors outside the three miles limit. And
even within this limit the provisions would have
to be very carefully worded to affect foreign or
English ships, if they could affect them at all.
Perhaps the best present practical course would be
for the Australian Premiers to confer and arrange
for a united “crawl ” to the Colonial Office and the
Admiralty on the subject.

SYDNEY SHOWS FOB COMING WEEK.

The new American co. opened on Saturday
at Sydney Lyceum to a crowded audience, which,
from the noise it made, seemed immensely pleased
all through—though why it should have been
quite so pleased the present scribe knows not.
lor the “Trip to Chinatown” is reminis-
cent of the lamented “Morocco Bound,” which
froze some time ago, inasmuch as it consists of a
collection of songs and a number of variety turns
suspended on a thin streak of alleged plot, like the
family armor hung out to dry on a thread. A
considerable quantity of people walk around con-
siderably and say they are going somewhere as an
excuse for saying they will sing one more song
before they go there, and after they have sung the
song they don’t go—they stay to sing some more
songs. When at last they do go they stop on the
way to have supper, and don’t have supper—they
sln K j us t one more little song before the supper
a l on £> kept on doing it indefinitely.
When they aren’t singing because they are
just going somewhere they are singing
because they have just arrived from some-
where. Finally, the last act shows them after
they have been at the place they have so long
been going to, and then they do another song
or two before they come away from it. The
result is long and shadowy and hazy and unsatisfac-
tory. Many of the turns are good enough to make
an exceedingly smart variety entertainment, and
many of the sayings are clever enough for an Oscar
Wilde comedy, but the attempt to string them
together into one narrative (which only consists in
eight people intending to go to a ball) by tying a
thm streak of conversation from the tail of the
last joke to the head of the next one is not wholly
successful. In the second act the stage is often left
entirely deserted save for an old man sitting on a
hot brick in one corner, and this act drags more
than usual, and gets lost in the mist even more
frequently than the others.
* * * *
The co. is good in places and respectably
second-rate in other places. Harry Conor vaguely
suggests himself as a most capable comedian who
might appear to better purpose in a show which
wasn t quite so much like a spectre straying in a
fog, and he has the additional advantage of being
the only man in the co. who talks exactly like
Mark Twain. The rest talk like somebody else—
possibly the late Josh Billings. Frank Lawton,
as the waiter, is a comedian whom The Bulletin
swears by without any reservation whatever.
Moreover, he is a heavenly whistler, with more
music in his whistle than all the rest of the show
has in its soul. Miss Bessie Clayton’s skirt-dance
is a marvel of contortion, and whether Bessie
travels as a dancer or as a contortionist, she
is equaHy good either way. Also, Miss Geraldine
•j ann 13 a P rett y an d exceedingly nice
widow. The other three ladies at the tail-end of
the bill have no special points about them, and
with the exception of George Beane—the man
who sits wearily in a corner for sixty minutes—
there is a lot of dust and ashes about the rest of
the gathering. The big tenor is only noteworthy
because he is the walking duplicate of the big
baritone—they got measured for each other, appa-
rently, and this similarity is the result; and
Patrice is an unconvincing little woman in boy’s
clothes. Some of the songs are'good and some are
moderate, and the people come back and sing it all
over again on too little provocation. Probably, a
great want of tangibleness is what is really the
matter with the “Trip to Chinatown.”
** * *
On Saturday night George Darrell arose
once more at Her Majesty’s and raged afresh in a
new, bounceable, india-rubber drama of terrible
intensity called the “Crimson Thread.” Said
drama is G.D.’s own latest literary effort, and
it represents the author, as a British detective in
Chicago, and Inman as a ’Murkan tough of bowel-
less ferocity, prowling after each other’s scalps
through four acts. Darrell is in search of a friend
who vanished 25 years ago, or his heirs, executors,
or assigns, or the blood of the man who killed him
—he doesn’t mind which. Consequently he and
Inman disguise themselves in vast masses of long
hair apiece and go round stealthily in search of
each other. Each one swears to have the other’s
ber-lud the first time they meet, therefore they
come together in Act I. and Darrell escapes
through the window, and in Act 11. they meet and
pull each other’s hair somewhat, and in Act 111.
they foregather again and throw each other over-
board very considerably, and in Act IV. they mutu-
ally prance in a vegetable garden, and in between
all these meetings they make dreadful resolves as
to what will happen if they keep on meeting. At
last it begins to appear that one of them will cer-
tainly catch rheumatism through exposure if they
meet many dozen times more without any active
shooting, but providentially a common Chicago
policeman plugs Inman as he is rushes over the
fence after omitting to kill Darrell for the fifth
time (it is understood that the blood-stained
desperado is still alleging as he rushes over the
fence that he will butcher his enemy at sight), and
he is carried back to where Darrell isn’t actively
running after him in the garden, though he is still
stating that there will be a dreadful reckoning for
Inman whenever they meet; and their mutual
animosity is thereafter buried in the bad man’s
grave.
The manner in which these two men of
iron resolve that something dreadful will happen
whenever they meet, and it never does happen
except through the wild, casual gunning of a police-
man, is affecting. The sensation of the “ Crimson
Thread ” is a realistic and business-like train
going over a very creditable bridge, and three
people falling from various heights into a capable
and brilliant tank. In addition to the two men
who don’t shoot each other as above-mentioned,
the play is ably propped up by Miss Gladys Leigh,
Miss Lilia Wiide—the latter as a stormy girl in
the newspaper and peanut business; and a number
of other people, most of whom are Harry Leston.
The co., in fact, is very good all round, and the
objection is mainly the drama, which is written
with a bucket of red paint and a spade. How
Inmftn gets up his reputation as a desperado of the
most awful description, seeing that he allows
Darrell to call him a liar with hardly a protest,
and how Darrell ever became a brilliant detective,
considering that his prey meets him and shakes
him and disarranges his pretty hair and goes away
uncaptured, are two mysteries that defy solution.
** * *
Woods and Marshall, the lessees of
Her Majesty’s, leave Sydney for a time at
least, and a complimentary benefit to Alfred
Woods is being organised as a send-off.
The Bulletin trusts he will get a good
one, if only because his nigger in “ Bobbery
Under Arms ” is the best aboriginal this paper re-
members on the Australian stage. The firm at
Her Majesty’s consists of one very young man and
one very young girl (for the pretty Mrs. Marshall
is no more than that), and it tackled the biggest
theatre in Sydney with small resources and
unlimited pluck. It had a spell of remarkable and
well-deserved success, and then, by mischance, it
got mixed up in ruinous law proceedings, and the
great, damp hand of costs fell upon it, and the
curse which is always waiting about this theatre
came home to roost. The Bulletin trusts that
the benefit will pan-out for enough to enable Alf.
Woods—wherever he and the co. are' going—to
take the biggest theatre in that place—whatever
place it may he—and prosper for ever afterwards.
** * *
The last nights of “ Derby Winner ” are
announced at Sydney Royal. Despite the many
weeks during which Baker has repurchased his
horse for ,£25,000 every night, the present drama
is still going well, and apparently, if Bland Holt
cared to stretch his luck as far as it would go
without breaking, he might have put up a record
run. And this isn’t a good time for records, either.
* * *
At time of writing, Sydney Tivoli an-
nounces the last four nights of Harry Rickards,
and the last three nights of Tennyson and
O’Gorman, and the last night of ventriloquist
Winton, so, presumably, a vast mass of new
talent will he turned on next Saturday. A big
benefit is announced for the widow and children
of the unfortunate person who was fatally dropped
upon in the Tivoli stalls some time ago, but as
said benefit will be over before this appears in
print The Bulletin can only wish it luck in a
retrospective sort; of way.
* * *
The luckless Sydney Music Hall is open
again with the American Prolique Minstrel and
Burlesque Co. on the stage, and the ghost of the
various defunct managements on the front door-
step. Things are not quite so bad with the present
co. as with some that went before ; still, it seems
as if the star of the Music Hall had set. In fact,
it never rose. In fact, it is questionable if the un-
happy place ever had any star at all.
** * *
Mrs. Potter’s re-appearance in “Camille,”
at Melb. Princess’s, was bound to suffer from the
fact that Sarah Bernhardt had been there between
the ’Murkan lady’s visits. After seeing Bern-
hardt identify herself with the character of the
amorous consumptive, one finds it harder than
ever to believe in Mrs. Potter’s imitation ; and to
see “Camille,” without a strong faith in the
heroine, is to be wearied by a wire-drawn piece of
sickly sentimentalism. Dumas’ story has nothing
but naughtiness to recommend it. The two male
competitors for Camille’s favors are equally unin-
teresting, and the more manly and respectable of
the pair is the Count who pays the piper whilst
the other fellow calls the tune. Camille at her best
is only a harlot who commits herself to an act of
unwomanly self-sacrifice on the altar of a tin god.
She attempts to cure her lover’s passion by
actually “ taking up ” with the Count de Yarville
whereas she might have answered the same pur-
pose by pretending to run away with an imaginary
protector. In which case, however, the plav
would be robbed of its chief vice.
* * * *
Mils. Potter as Camille gives an oppor-
tunity for comparisons between her histrionic
powers of these and former days. She is much
less affected in the lighter scenes than she used to
be, her consumptive cough has greatly improved
and her sobs are now almost heartrending. On the
other hand, Mrs. Potter’s efforts at tragic acting
have ; weakened somewhat, and her death scene
didn t deserve the applause heaped upon it on
Saturday by a section of the audience. Indeed
the fury of first-night demonstrations at the
Princess sis becoming farcical. Bellew’s perform-
ance of Armand Duval seems much the same
stagey, showy, skilfully exaggerated article as
before; Scot.lnglis is a natural and unassuming
Count, the style of J. P. Cathcart is utterly un-
suited to Duval, pire, and Hans Phillips pops up
pleasantly among the minor people, none of whom
are as important as Mrs. Potter’s costumes. On
Saturday they change to “La Tosca.”
** * *
Belle w s Marat is essentially a creature of
the stage, and only to be criticised as such. The
real Marat was an accomplished physician, suffer-
ing from a horrible disease contracted through
circumstances which, in a measure, excused his
remorseless savagery. This is not suggested by
Bellew. His Marat seems to be a man-monkey,
a naturally dirty, sordid informer, who limps
through the revolutionary period crying “ Arrest
that man ! ” in the spirit of a beggar-on-horsebaclt.
Surely the foreigner who assumed the position
of the People’s Friend, despite his repulsive ap-
pearance, must have been a man of commanding
intellect and will power, not merely a cunning,
craven monkey.
** * *
The “Trilby ” burlesque, at Melb. Royal,
turns out to be humorous in spots, and not par-
ticularly sad elsewhere. Also, it possesses brevity,
which is the soul of wit. The entertainment on
Saturday worked itself out by ten o’clock, and as
the vast audience didn’t stop to clamor for more,
it went away satisfied, The Bulletin supposes.
All the same, this journal would tremble for the
financial prospects of a travesty that terminated at
10 p.m., even if the management had staged it
regardless of expense. Maggie Moore’s ver-
sion of “ Trilby ’ ’ is not a costly experi-
ment, neither is the company strong in
the particular sort of talent and experience required
for doing much with little material. The first
Gaiety Co. might have elaborated the poor little
Royal “skit” into a full-sized joke, with Fred
Leslie, Marion Hood, and Nellie Farren as Sven-
gali, Trilby, and Little Billee —but no matter.
Some of the points in Du Maurier’s play admit
of very easy parody, and, in some cases,
these are made the most of by Maggie Moore,
Roberts, and Walter Rivers. The leading
lady’s pride in her bare tootsies is
frank and fearless. Svengali and Gecko
making music upon dummy instruments are neces-
sarily funny ; likewise the idea of destroying the
mesmerist’s ungodly influence with bad liquor.
There are hearty laughs, here and there, in the
course of the show. If the laughs were more fre-
quent, and if many other matters were different to
what they are, “Trilby ” might run for a month
at Melb. Royal.

At Poverty Point .
The Distressed Actors’ Fund, although drawn upon by
mummers from all parts of Australia, seems to be a
Purely Vic. institution. One experience of a Theatrical
Carnival in Sydney didn’t encourage the Firm to identify
itself with further attempts in the same direction. Some
nasty remarks by a Melb. weekly, re last year’s voting of
douceurs to certain busy workers for the Fund, were not
called for. No other charity in the country is so econo-
mically managed, taking one year with another, as the
D.A.F.

tion of W. R. Wilson, who— The Bulletin regrets to hear
is in bad health again. Nor that Brownlow’s journey
West has anything to do with the health of W.R.W.
A delay of 20 minutes in raising the curtain, and subse-
quent wearisome intervals between acts on the first night
of “ Camille,” at Melb. Princess’s, made it appear that La
Belle Potter was playing Sarah Bernhardt as well as per-
forming Sarah’s favorite character. Possibly the first
long wait was a concession to Mrs. P.’s proUgee, Miss Ida
Hamilton, who had to appear in the opening scene as
Camille’s maid, and was so unready with her lines that
she needed prompting when the time came for telling De
Varville the story of the old Duke’s fatherly affection for
the courtesan. The embarrassed damsel got stuck at the
word “duke” until De Varville coughed for assistance
from the man with the book.
The first (and wettest) night of “ Charlotte Corday,” at
Melb. Princess’s, found all parts of the house crowded,
with the exception of the amphitheatre. On the five fol-
lowing nights the order of things was reversed. Sassiety
seems to have lost interest in Mrs. Potter, and the mis-
take of starting their season with “As You Like It” is
now admitted. It was really George Musgrove’s mistake,
for the campaign was planned under his advice. G.M.,
remembering Bellew’s former success as a professional
beauty, reckoned tbit his Orlando would be an equal
attraction with the m w Rosalind. But the Melbourne
girls don’t seem to bib ll,is time.
At a performance o i “Dick Whittington,” by Burton’s
Rockhampton Liliputi n Opera Co., a pleasant incident
happened. The cat, in making a spring upon an imagin-
ary mouse, left his tail under the toe of a chorus-girl.
She, in no way abasia I, picked up the caudal appendage
and pinned it where it would be most useful.
The church and the concert platform are getting to be
on familiar terms, anyhow. Edward Branscombe, the
new tenor imported by Stevens and Howells, is a lay vicar
of Westminster Abbey, whatever that may mean in point
of holiness. Douglas Powell, the baritone member of the
same co., graduated in a church choir.
F. : Musical criticisms in the Melb. morning dailies
often have some snap in them—unlike the dramatic
crude-icisms, which are mostly written in the advertising
manager’s office. The musical criticisms are written by
professionals—and the harmony that always doesn’t exist
among musicians is responsible for the most snappy
remarks. A musician will always tell the truth about a
rival, unless it is flattering. Pianists who propose to
settle in Melbourne (though all Melb. musicians don’t
settle) always have a bad time. Transient contraltos, on
the other hand, are permitted to get their notices cut
according to advt. The way to get readable dramatic
criticisms, instead of pump-water, would be to employ
pros, who are resting. If we must have lies, let us have
readable lies.

S.S.: “It is not the Frantz family that draws the
females to the miscellaneous show at Melb. Opera House :
it is Frank Yorke. Yorke comes on in an insane arrange-
ment of primitive kilts and a tar-brush. The kilts are a
mere wisp and the rest is legs, horrible legs in fleshings
with carbuncles on them as big as gourds, and warped
like stale bolognas, but a man’s legs, nevertheless • and
when Yorke is jerking those gnarled extremities in a
lunatic dance how the women laugh—how they toss up
their hands and rock on their seats, reckless of hair and
stay-busks, and shriek ! To the average man, Yorke’s
turn seems coarse and humorless, a mere suggestion of
hideous nudity and wind-galls ; to the woman, it is evi-
dently the most unctuous comedy. In 10 years I have
never seen the women of an audience so tickled, and it
fetches them all at once. A man never sees humor in a
woman’s legs—they are either exquisite or pathetic.”
