Chief Justice Madden’s illogical judgment in the
Richardson v. Richardson divorce-case, where he refused
to cut the knot on the ground that the husband had not
sufficiently guarded the wife from temptation, has been
rendered more ridiculous than ever by his Honor’s dis-
covery of collusion on the lady’s part. This is an addi-
tional reason, says Madden, C.J., why a divorce should be
withheld. As a matter of fact, it was the only legal
reason for his judgment, but he knew nothing about it at
the time.
Mrs. 8.-Pot. threatens to play Lady Macbeth to K.
B’s Macbeth. Can anyone imagine Cora as the terrible
centrepiece in the Scotch tragedy? When Ellen Terry
played the part, someone said : “It is not Ellen Terry as
Lady Macbeth, but Lady Macbeth as Ellen Terry.”
Alice Rebottaro has made a welcome reappearance, in
Melb., with the Jewish amateurs. She came with Mine.
Simonsen’s Italian Opera troupe, and made a good im-
pression in “Lucia.” However, her father has the same
hatred of the stage which possesses Stefani, the husband
of Madame Boema, and she has been quietly teaching.
Mrs. Brown Potter, who has realised that Worth does
not make the actress, probably tries harder than any
other woman prominent on the stage to be an artiste, but
play-goers can say with the Vicar of Bray : “ You do, you
do ; we have observed your efforts,” and that is the devil
of it. Mrs. B. P. struggling with a bit requiring intensity
of feeling reminds one of the strong man putting up great
weights, and suggests the advisability of a severe course
of training with light diet. Instead of being carried away
by the genius of the actress, one. sits considering at her
physical powers and comparing her with the little doll of
a woman who carries the Frantz family on her snowy
shoulders, much to the advantage of the acrobat. Gush-
ing young critics of the “ nocent waterspout” order
declare that Potter equals Bernhardt, but is the victim of
blind prejudices. Can it be that the average man,
absorbed in the physical attractions of the tawny Cora,
has no eyes for her spiritual beauties ?
“ He is the archetype of the anarchists who would wreck
the whole fabric of society.” Thus does Dr. Armont
Corday play to the circle, referring to Marat in the Melb.
Princess show. How can any man refer to a contem-
porary as an archetype of something to come ? “I.
Montesquieu,” the author of “ Charlotte Corday,” gravely
suspected of being Potter and Bellew in disguise, should
certainly try and make his characters remember the
century they live in. The time has not yet arrived for
Charlotte to go to execution on a bicycle.
One of the first actions of the French Republic was to
pass an act repealing God, and giving the clergy 24 hours
to clear out of France. Yet in Charlotte Corday at Melb.
Princess, an abbe is depicted mingling freely with the
public at a period three years later. Historical inac-
curacies like this must rankle in the bosom of Cora
Urquhart 8.P., whose worship of truth led her to envelop
her calves in hideous bandages in “ As You Like It.”
The death of Augustus Harris will probably upset a
number of operatic (Italian and English) arrangements for
the present London season. Harris’s theatrical business
was so large and risky, and so much under his personal
management, that it can hardly pass into other hands
without being slowed down for awhile.
Augustus Harris got his managerial inspiration from
his father, who used to run promenade concerts at Covent
Garden, but Harris p&re hadn’t half the shrewdness,
energy, and all-round cleverness of his boy, Gus. The late
“ Knight of Drury,” as some of the London weeklies
called him, took over Drury Lane Theatre from a bank-
rupt lessee (Chatterton), and made money right from the
start of his career. He collaborated with the late Henry
Pettittand Paul Merritt in writing “The World,” “Youth,”
and numerous other gaudy dramas, and, until he grew
fat, he played the hero in a brisk, unconventional manner,
thereby saving himself a “star’s” salary. He always
drew up the scheme of his gorgeous Xmas pantomime,
and never failed to beat his own previous record in that
direction. No doubt Augustus Harris had sound business
reasons for pushing himself into the London Corporation
and City Council, although most men in his position would
have kept clear of aldermen and Lord Mayor’s shows.
Judging from his appearance of late years, the busy man
fell a victim to civic banquets.
A London paper says of Letty Lind that she “ acts with
the daintiness by which she has gradually come to be
recognised as an artist.” Seven years ago The Bulletin
called Letty Lind an artist in contradistinction to the
other Gaiety dancer, Sylvia Grey, whose acting was
abominably careless.
Bellew’s diffieulty has always been not to imitate Irving,
and now, with his Chas. Clarkly silver hair, he is quite the
younger brother.

S.G. : “ Apropos the Potter-Bellew ‘ As You Like It,’
why do all mummers insist on talking Shakespeare’s lines
at about the pace of a spavined hearse-hauler ? I have
never heard a really decent Jaques yet. Jaques has
been laughing cynically all over the forest, and says so
himself. Yet every exponent of the part delivers the big
speech as if he were intoning a burial-service. Rosalind
is plainly girlish, lively, enticing. Yet her lines are usually
spoken like family morning-prayer. That old Adam
should speak slowly is understandable in a man of his age.
The two bright, redeeming attempts to escape tongue-
fettered funereality are those of Billy Elton and Polly
Emery as might be expected in people who bear sug-
gestively lively names like Billy and Polly. A fellow with
a name like Orlando naturally must be thinking all the
time whether his hat is on straight and his tights have no
mud on them; so, perhaps, Bellew’s not so much to
blame as he seems. Why can’t a company remember that
Shakespeare was a jolly, devil-may-care house-author who
died from over-grogsomeness?”

The Wild-Cat Column.
The doings of the Ward Farmers’ Association—
the concern which has bankrupted and politically
wrecked the M.L. Treasurer—grow more remarkable as
the revelations go on. One astounding circumstance is
that when the grain-buying transactions of the Associa-
tion turned out well the profits went to the Association ,
when they went wrong, which they generally did, the
loss was debited to Ward. Why this was done Ward
doesn’t say ;he simply alleges that it was done. There-
fore, the Association kept paying away its profits in divi-
dends and debiting the losses to Ward until the amount
debited was more than Ward could pay. Then he
collapsed, and the Association, which had been reckoning
him as an asset for any amount, collapsed, too. It
is difficult to understand how either Ward or the Associ a
tion could expect anything else. it is also alleged that
the Association gave the Colonial Bank stdre warrants fni
£30,000 worth of oats, and induced the Bank, on tln<
strength of this, to credit it with a draft for £30,000 to
make its overdraft look good. There were really no civ ,
so that the warrants were bogus and the draft worth It n'i
The man who gave the bogus warrants wasn’t prosecuted
neither was he sacked. At the time the Colonial Bank
made out its last balance-sheet the draft, it is said, was
supposed to be good ; but it is also alleged that before the
balance-sheet was presented to the shareholders the
£30,000 security was found to be waste paper. vet,
apparently, the balance-sheet wasn’t altered, although
this transaction threw quite a new light on the bank s
finances. The bank, so far, seems to view the matter in a
perfectly unembarrassed spirit. Altogether the business
appears to have been managed by everybody concerned
on purely aboriginal principles, and no one seems really
to know the reason of anything.
* * * *
The A. J.S. Bank issues a circular to its British
depositors, offering to convert £1,000,000 of the 4i per
cent, reconstructed deposits falling due between now and
1901 on the following terms £500,000 to be immediately
repaid in cash, and £500,000 to be converted into 3 per
cent, deposits not falling due until 1906. It is expressly
stipulated, however, that the A.J.S. doesn’t repay more
than £500,000 in cash ; therefore the above half-and-half
arrangement only applies if the rush of depositors doesn t
represent more than £1,000,000. If it represents
£2,000,000, then they are to receive one-quarter cash, one-
quarter 3 per cent, deposit-receipts, and one-half in 41 per
cent, deposit-receipts, as at present. According to latest
advices, however, the conversion will reach about
£900,000. This means about £20,250 interest saved
annually by payment of one-half, and £6750 saved
by reduction of interest on the other half total,
£27,000 a year. The A.J.S. has made comparatively
little progress at reducing its reconstructed depo-
sits ; at the end of 1893 they stood at £7,692,894, and
at the end of 1895 they had only been reduced by about
£1,000,000. Considering that over £6,000,000 of them fall
due by regular half-yearly instalments in 1897-1901, the
present offer to pay off £500,000 (less than one half-year’s
instalment), on condition that another £500,000 is dated
10 years ahead at a big reduction of interest, looks like a
very feeble way of tackling the difficulty.
**• * *
It is reported that the Westralian Govt, has a
scheme on hand for a £5,000,000 loan. Half the money is
wanted for water-supply purposes ; £1,500,000 for railway
building ; and £1,000,000 for the purchase of the “private
enterprise ” Great Southern Line, it having been decided
that syndicate management is a failure. The Great
Southern is 243 miles long, so at the price mentioned it
will cost the State about £4115 per mile—only about £3OO
per mile more than the average cost of construction by
the State —and the land-grant of 12,000 acres per mile,
and the amounts which have been paid for interest on the
co.’s debentures to keep the line out of the insolvency
court. So, reckoning all things, the Government won't
Eay more than 1J times as much as it would have cost to
uild the line as a public enterprise at the beginning. A
£5,000,000 loan represents something like £4O a head
added to the debt at one fell swoop—in proportion to
population almost as much, in one dreadful act, as the
whole public debt which Victoria has accumulated in forty
years. If this event comes off, Westralia will have, in
proportion to population, the biggest debt in Australasia,
and the biggest boom, with the biggest burst looming in
the distance.
* * * *
Considering Westralia’s experience with its two
land-grant railways, both of which, after they had been
subsidised at the rate of 12,000 acres per mile of line, had
to be propped up by State guarantees for enormous
amounts ; and Maoriland’s experience with its land-grant
line, which burst because the State wouldn’t prop it up in
the same way—where would Queensland be now if the
great gridiron railway-scheme had been carried out ?
Judging by these other experiences, after most of the public
lands had gone in subsidies Queensland would have had
to guarantee about £20,000,000 worth of debentures, or
else have about twenty half-finished scraps of railroad left
on its hands to be finished and worked by the State, while
about twenty British shindykits would sue the Govern-
ment for £1,000,000 damages apiece. The land-grant
railway job almost always comes to the same disastrous
ending.
* » * *
The awful Freehold Investment and Banking
Co. (Melb.) has passed away. Its assets were so unrealisable
that it simply handed over the whole lot—nominally worth
£1,330,000—t0 the creditors. The creditors now call
themselves the Freehold Assets Realisation Co. They
have issued shares to themselves for the amount of their
claims, and the new co. collects the rents, when there
are any, and watches the alleged securities, and hopes to
pay a div. —some day.

* * * *
The National Bank of Tasmania shows a profit
for the half-year ended 31st May of £4299, againsi £1136
last half-year and £3989 and £4695 for two previous ones.
Therefore, though things are improving, they are still dis-
tinctly worse than they were in Nov., 1894. A 5 per cent,
div. and income tax thereon absorb £3991, leaving £3OB
to add to the funds. Business has improved a little in the
half-year. The reserve-fund (which hasn’t increased in
the last two years) still stands at £22,500 ; but the cash
reserve is large and solid, and equals 30 per cent, of the
liabilities to the public. The National has now been in
existence for 10 years, and it seems to have good pro-
spects if it keeps out of the mining boom now raging in
the serious little island. The last mining boom down
there, by the way, was what killed the V.D.L. Bank.
* * * *
The awful drop m the Vic. railway takings still
continues, and that regular weekly statement, “ the
revenue showed a decline of £2916 as compared with the
corresponding week of last year,” has become stereotyped.
Obviously, however, things must improve ; if the revenue
ultimately gets down to Id., then nothing on earth can
prevent it rising to 2d., and it will then have gone up 100
per cent, quite suddenly. The total, at latest, was
down to £33,430 per week.
* * * *
The National Bank of Maori!and, a concern
with its head office in London, reports a profit for the
year ended 31st March of £18,090, which is a distinct
improvement on the £16,431 of last year, and £16,998 of
the one previous. A 5 per cent, dividend uses up £12,500,
and the remaining £5590 goes to profit and loss, making
it £24,696. Under these circumstances, £20,000 is devoted
to starting a reserve, the last one having vanished when
the National Bank struck its big snag a few years ago.
Business has improved greatly with the National of late,
and it looks like having a run of prosperity.
* * * *
St. James’Building Society (Melbourne), a big
concern with £214,000 capital, announces that it has lost
£62,556 during the half-year. Last half-year the loss was
£7942, and the total deficiency in the assets is now
£72,285, which means one-third of the capital gone. The
St. James, like most other Melbourne societies, is loaded
up with a great dead-weight of foreclosed freehold pro-
perties, and as it sells them the difference between the
price realised and the book-value is added to the bloated
shortage, which promises to eat up everything before the
process is finished. During the past six months some
£30,000 worth (book-values) of leasehold property have
been thrown overboard to lighten the ship. _ Lease-
hold property is hardly worth calling an asset in Mel-
bourne these days.
• * * *
The day of small things. The Haymarket Land,
Building and Investment Co. (Sydney) states that it has
stopped the deficits of the previous 12 months, and that
the net profit for last half-year is £4 Is. 6d. And the
Argus Building Society (Melbourne) proudly reports a
profit of £7 for the year—equal to about 9d. per cent, per
annum.

The Universal Building Society (Melbourne) has
property to the nominal value of £137,000, and the net
rental for last half-year was £533 —something like sixteen
shillings per cent, per annum.
° * * * *
The Manly (Sydney) ferry difficulty gets more
complicated. 'The directors of the new co. (‘ on its
uppers ”) proposed amalgamation, with the old one, but
the shareholders repudiated the idea —mainly, it would
seem, because the Brown firm of ship-owners offered to
finance them. Now the Browns have withdrawn, appa-
rently tired of waiting, and the new co. is left with no
amalgamation—no Brown —nothing. Also it owes the
old co. £550, and various other sums to other people, and
it has no ready cash, and the hank won’t honor its cheques,
and all is chaos. Therefore, amalgamation is again being
attempted, but’the Port Jackson Co., now that its hated
rival has no Brown to fall back upon, will probably be
hard to deal with.
» * * *
The Imperial Marine Insurance Co. of Japan—
in the language of the Chrysanthemum Land, Ihe
“Teikoku Kaisho Hoken Kabushiki Kaisha”—with a
capital of 3,000,000 yen (about £400,000) has started busi-
ness in Sydney. Burns, Philp & Co. are the local agents.
Considering that the Australian fire and marine insurance
business is mostly done by foreigners already, and the
local offices are fast being smothered by the stupendous
mass of foreign competition, this big Jap. office may as
well add itself to the list as not. When every other nation
is scrambling for a share of the business, a Jap. company
more or less hardly signifies.

• * * *
The affairs of the Union Milling Co., Horsham,
Vic., provide interesting information for the farmers of
that province. It appears that the co. went bung through
inability to satisfy the claims of the Commercial Bank,
that institution having pretty well everything with the
co. under mortgage. Included in the assets seized were
several thousand bags of wheat, stored at the mill by
local farmers, and the latter are kicking vigorously at the
action of the hank in seizing property that was simply
held by the co. in trust. The bank, however, sticks to
the grain, and the only party to whom the farmers can
look for redress is the co., which hasn’t anything left to
grant redress with, and which, it seems, did a perfectly
legal thing, as the law now stands, in mortgaging that
which it didn’t own. A similar case occurred at Numur-
kah (Vic.) last year, and the question of erecting Govern-
ment storage sheds was seriously thought over by the
Turner Ministry, but nothing eventuated. Possibly
something will eventuate this time. .If the farmers
throughout the province were to combine in a sort of
vigilance society they might perhaps discover that a lot
of the so-called'mills in the country districts are simply
living upon the hypothecation of the grain entrusted to
their custody.
* * * *
The liquidator of the Union Finance Co. in Melb.
has announced a final div. of Is., making 20s. in the £
paid to creditors. The Argus commercial editor thus
comments : “ This is a very satisfactory result as times
go”! What! Which times? When our forefathers
clothed themselves in woad and were financially holiest?
Or the recent dark ages when so many of the biggest of
the boomsters laid the foundations of fortunes for tlieir
posterity ? Or perhaps the Argus man means the 21st
century, when his paper will consist of 20 cols, and sell at
Is. a copy ? Twenty shillings in the £ good as times go !
Of course it isn’t! That liquidator should have paid 40s.
on the quid and given every creditor an annuity and a
K.C.M.G.’s patent.
* * * *
A side-splitting illustration of The Bulletin’s
recent remarks on the absurdity of three-ha’penny muni-
cipalities, gingerbread town-halls, and tin aldermen is the
corporative cataclysm that has overtaken the lilliputian
municipality of Five Dock (Sydney). This gorgeous little
state couldn’t meet the account of a creditor who dunned
it for a few pounds ; a bailiff took possession of its palatial
Town Hall, and the office sticks were sold up and realised
£2O 10s., or 7s. more than the debt plus the cost of bailiff.
The buyer, being the father of an alderman, has let the
municipality the furniture at a rental of 3d. a week, so
that the worst of the crisis is over. Five Dock is munici-
pally a quarter of a century old ! though the present cor-
poration is only a splinter of the old one. The latter was
proclaimed in ’7l ;it was divided into two in ’B9. Under
the rotten condition of N.S.W. municipal law, any area
whatever may be separated from an existing municipality
and proclaimed a municipality “ on its own” upon the
petition of two-thirds of “ the electors ”in that area. So
that it would not be impossible to have a N.S.W. munici-
pality composed of three persons and a vacant block of
land.
* * * *
Since the Vic. telephone system has been under
State control, an aggregate loss of £70,000 has been made ;
yet the private enterprise which previously ran the con-
cern paid a steady five per cent. Which fact is trium-
phantly pointed to' by the Fat press as a horrible example
of the fallacy of State Socialism, and another proof that
the railways should be auctioned at once. But the Tele-
phone Co. reaped the advantages of the seven years of
plenty, while the period of the lean kine from ’B9 to the pre-
sent day fell to the lot of the State. How long would the
private concern have maintained its divs. ? The Tram Co.
with its 25 per cent, and 10 per cent, bonuses dropped
pretty quick on the collapse of the boom.
* * * *
In Adelaide, the other day, the Yuba S.M. Co.
was floated in an hour or two to acquire a marvellous pro-
perty “in the Broken Hill district.” The co. (£75,000
paper-capital) had been in existence some time before any
information about it could be got in Broken Hill. The
locality, it appears, is Mt. Rohe—the district upon which
Bendigo Lansell spent some thousands, and which has re-
turned less for the money spent in it than any other in
the Barrier region. The particular mine is supposed to
be one held and abandoned in the days when silver stood
50 per cent, higher than it does to-day.
C * * * *
Junction North makes a start at serious busi-
ness with the sale of 2000 tons of first-grade sulphides to
be delivered within three weeks from date of signing the
contract. There ought to be a Is. div. for August; one
would have been declared by this, but the timbering bill
has been heavy—the ore body is very troublesome. The
co. sends on its own account an experimental lot of 600
tons of ore to Europe.
* * * *
Block 14 adds to its Port Adelaide plant the
largest furnace yet erected in
B.H. North stops its mill—for alterations.
Ominous. The irregularity of the ore body in this mine
must always give trouble while a mechanical process like
jigging is relied upon.

Shareholders in the South ought to make them-
selves quite satisfied that the mine has honestly been

duced figure. To say the least, things in connec-

* * * *
B.H. Consols has resumed work, the English co.
—a plucky one—having reconstructed.
Small parcels of ore rich in silver are sent from
the Old Billagoe mine, in far Western N.S.W., to Alder-
shot, Q., and to Wallarroo, S.A., for treatment at a cost
of from £6 to £8 a ton. It is a free-milling ore that
might be treated on the ground for less than £1 a ton ;
nor would the plant involve any considerable cost. As in
the case of Mt. Drysdale, which is near it, far more money
has been spent in the carriage of ore than would erect
efficient machinery.

Mt. Gahan, Pambula. It now appears the
hoped-for sale to an English-co. Was fallen through, that
the payable milling-ore has cut out, and that, to prospect
for the Victory lode, calls must be made.
* * * *
The very worst has befallen Cobar as a mining
camp. A few weeks ago it was doing well, and seemed to
have a good, solid time ahead. Now the great Sunday
mining tipster has started to boom it. Cobar really
deserves a better fate.
* * * *
Big things are predicted from the alluvial wash
on the Macquarie River, N.S.W., when a co. starts under
the distinguished patronage of Warnford Lock, the Czar
of Lucknow.” Harry Newman having been appointed
manager of the extension mines at Lucknow, he will
probably be re-crowned king of that locality. Neither is
he too modest to enjoy the appellation.
* * * *
Sensational wire from Cobar, N.S. W.: —“ Fresh
shoes have been put on the Mount Pleasant stampers.
So far, the news lacks confirmation.
* * * *
The English co. that recently bought some
leases at Galley Swamp (N.S.W.) have, it seems, changed
the name to Gallymont; probably to get away from the
idea that the mines were in the middle of a swamp, which
they are not. They have been swamped with £300,000
capital instead. * * *
The Sunday “ tipster” advises all and sundry to
buy Rainbow and Mountain Maids—a mine at Copeland—
and refers to the very favorable report from the new
mining manager. Is this it: “ Cannot find anything to
doubt the bona-fides of the report issued. Everything is
full of water.”
* * * *
Cobar is determined to have a mining-boom or
burst. It has been proved easier to float a Cobar mine in
Cobar than in Sydney. No other N.S.W. mining camp
can say that. Cobar has now an open mining exchange.
* * * *
Pambula (N.S.W.): “The Great Victory has
cleaned up a nice cake, but the weight has not been dis-
closed.” Ten oz. is a nice cake, so is 10,000 oz. —now which
is it ? Great things have for many months past been ex-
pected from this show, which has been regarded as the
most permanent thing in a will-of-the-wisp field.
“Gold at the back door of Melbourne.” At
Black Flat, six miles from Oakleigh—among the market-
gardens— about 80yds. from Moylan’s hotel, a local
market-gardener named Holland and his two sons (all ex-
miners) are now sinking a shaft right on the footpath of
the Spring Vale road. They have already got several
specimens on the surface—one weighing 16dwt.—and are
hopeful of striking the reef. Meanwhile, all the neighbor-
ing property-owners, including Movlan aforesaid, have
applied for leases. Holland informed the writer that he
had found payable alluvial gold on the easterly flat 34
years ago. ,
At a recent Ballarat (Vic.) land-sale £75 an
acre was refused for II acres of lucerne land, £9O being
the reserve. The land was subsequently sold privately at
nearly the reserve price.
* * * *
Down South a Govt, official had to report upon
the bona-tides of a friendly society about to be registered,
and recommended registration because the office was
handsomely furnished. Three months afterwards the
concern went bung.
* * * *
Melbourne tobacco-factories are offering prizes,
bicycles, &c., to the individual who collects the greatest
number of tags or labels of certain brands of tobacco.
The gentle public is, as usual, out of it, as retail tobac-
conists carefully remove the tags and labels, cut up the
tobacco instead-of buying it ready cut) and thus collar the
swag.

Catherine Beef, Bendigo, bobs up again with a
6d. div., the first for nearly two years. Last week’s
milling, 430 tons for 2200 z., with 60oz. from pyrites and
plates. The Catherine appears to have a much better
time ahead.

crowd.
* * * *
Kyneton (Vic.) went delirious the other day over
the alleged discovery of a “ mountain of gold and silver,
estimated to go 400 oz. to the ton.” The whole hamlet
formed itself into a syndicate right away, but the joy
speedily turned into wails. A Footscray assay gave ldwt.
20gr. gold and 18gr. silver per ton. At latest, a posse of
inhabitants was preparing a new hell for the prospector.
North Woah Hawp, Ballarat, on 7idwt. stone
last half-year, just met ex.’s, but the prospects for the
coming term are better. The Indicator lode in No. 5 level
is tossing out much better gold, and the stone is making
strong underfoot, so that the deeper levels should speak
more cheeringly. The North is one of the mines on which
the royalty-battener weighs heavily.

slumped last half 44650 z. lldwts., showing none too well
against the previous term’s 51980 z. 12dwt., especially as
the latter had the worst of the fathom-average by lldwts.
Ditto divs., £4500—£7500. Important developments in
the north end are expected to give the yields a “push”
in a couple of months.
* * * *
S.A. Govt. Geologist Brown is cautious in his
report on the Westralian mines. The only mine he ven-
tures a definite opinion upon is the Great Boulder, thus :
“The formation altogether appears to be the decomposed
and oxidised portion of what will most likely be a solid
lode at a greater depth.” His remarks on the other mines
are non-committal.
* * * *
One important question will probably have to
be settled before the case of the Alliance Syndicate and
the Liberty Consolidated Gold Mines, Ltd., of Coolgardie,
is finally disposed of by Vie. Supreme Court—viz., whether
a contract for pooling shares is legally enforceable.
Warnings are given by most authorities on company-law
against the practice, on account of the danger of “ over-
stepping the mark.” There appear to be varieties of
pooling, from the kind that “ rigs the market ” and is in-
dictable as a conspiracy to defraud, to a perfectly legiti-
mate variety. In this particular case the statement of
claim reveals a carefully-worded agreement to pool cer-
tain allotted shares.
Another interesting feature of this case is a
letter alluding to the existence in London of a special
newspaper press devoted to Westralian mining matters.
These papers, according to this letter, wrote according to
the amount of payment received—it was a perfectly-
organised blackmaii. “I have established communica-
tion with two of the best financial papers,” significantly
concludes this remarkable missive.
* » * *
When John Howell, ex-B.H. Prop, manager,
went to England last year, he took for flotation the Bush-
man’s, a Murchison (W.A.) mine, discovered by Broken
Hill prospectors. Howell transferred the job to White,
M.L.C., of M.L., and a co. was formed. An expert was
sent out, and he reports that there has been gross mis-
representation. The English co. refuses to. go on,, and
the vending B.H. syndicate finds itself left without either
mine or promised cash.
* * * *
The cheerful market - aspect of the General
Gordon (White Feather, W.A.) has led to the inevitable
results rather earlier than usual. The G.G. scrip is
scarcely dry, yet already the G.G. Extended has been
floated easily, and a North Extended promises to meet the
same fate. There seems to be local faith in the parent
mine.

* * * *
Coolgardie is going to find half-a-dozen Boulders
right in the town. Two were discovered last week, and
when it settles down to the business that number, should
be found every day in the year. The last is in Lindsay’s
mine, North Bayley-street. Of course it is a “ composite
lode,” and assays 9oz. This lode is only 30ft. wide, but
the other is 60ft. Unfortunately, Coolgardie can’t rid
itself from its great curse —“ too much white quartz.”
Immortal phrase.
* » * *
Golconda G.M. Co.: —“The chairman regretted
that the operations of the co. had not been as successful
as was anticipated.” A few rich specimens in a streak of
decomposed iron were found in cleaning out a tank.
Some men secured it under offer, and also secured the
tank under lock and key, till the co. was floated. The
patch soon gave out, but the shareholders are told “ the
prospects are encouraging.”
* * * *
The last wired Coolgardie discovery is said to be
within a mile of the town—a few days before it was out
near Burbank’s, and even now the exact spot is unknown,
“ the owners being very reticent. It is supposed to be
the Great Boulder formation.” There can’t be much
doubt of this, the Boulder being but 30 miles away. The
lode assays 270 z. to the ton, and, as it is only 60ft. wide,
it is child’s-play for the owners to conceal it from the
many thousands of people around Coolgardie. As the
lode was found in sinking a shaft, measuring the width—
60ft. —is another easy matter.

A co. with a capital of .£250,000 is erecting a
80-head stamp-mill near Northam, W.A. It is proposed
to bring ore from the Golden Pig mine, at Southern Cross,
one of the co.’s properties, and to take fresh water from
their lagoon to Coolgardie, over 300 miles. This co. can
have little faith in the Coolgardie fresh-water schemes.
The fact that the Brown Hill mine, Kalgoorlie,
is poor in the bottom is still kept as dark as the lungs of
midnight.

ies.” Truly a noble idea. It is beginning to look as

* * * *
The West Australian Brick Co. publishes its
prospectus in this issue. The affair doesn’t seem likely
to stagger under too big a capital, and the directors have
adopted the excellent plan of making the £1 shares fully
paid. The salient points about the prospectus are (1)
the co. reckons that 100,000 bricks a week can be sold at
an estimated minimum profit of 10s. a thousand—£so a
week, or £2500 per year net profit. (2) The co. passesses
13 acres of clay, which it is calculated will produce
130,000,000 bricks, and it will take 20 years to exhaust
the deposit. (3) The co. will be the first in Perth to use
machinery for brickmaking. (4) A new loan for public
buildings has been announced by Premier Forrest, and a
big demand is anticipated from public as well as private
sources.

• * * *
One Mt. Lyell furnace is now in blast, and
“there is every promise that the most sanguine expecta-
tions will be fulfilled” —a wheeze inseparable from the
start of everything mining. May the big venture prosper,
for the men chiefly interested are a game lot. After years
of monkeying by others, the mine was tackled by the
Broken Hillionaires, who, having the pick of nearly every-
thing in Australasia, surely ought to know what they are
about. They conspicuously differ from the Mt. Morgan-
aires, who don’t put back into mining a penny in the £ of
the money they have made and are making in the game.
* * * *
News has come to the West that G. A. Stockfeld
and his party, who went on a gold-expedition to Matabele-
land last year, have all been killed by the natives. If true
—and Heaven grant that it is not —Australia has lost one
of the few men who told the truth in examination of
Westralian mines. Physically and morally Stockfeld was
a fine fellow, and made many friends wherever he went.
* * * *
A N.S.W. contractor’s say-so: “There is no
more use for the contractor now. The department fixes
the prices and the men’s wages, and all the contractor has
to do is to take his bag across to the Treasury, get the
money, and hand it out to the men. None of it sticks to
his hand.” Which is probably the curious reason why
would-be contractors are still falling over each other in
the departmental passages of the Tender Department
and rushing every chance job. They don’t want money ;
they hanker after the chance to commit philanthropy.

The National Council of Women—a new
And “ boldly progressive ” association called into
being in Sydney one day last week—elected, as its
first three office-holders, women with the “Lady ”
handle to their names. Booh ! Lovely woman is
a dreadful snob.
» * * *
Pastor Edgar, Melb., is in excelsis. He
has now six Wesleyan nuns in his convent,
Mackenzie-street, connected with the central
Wesleyan church. The Holy Romans smile, but
none of the young quasi-nurses have yet gone off
the hooks. Yet no one mistakes the real nun for
the amateur article, which can never keep its eye
downcast.
* * * *
The question has been raised as to whether
the N.S.W. Railway Commissioners are justified
in putting up placards at stations and platforms,
cautioning passengers against committing a breach
of the by-laws, and citing cases—giving the names
of offenders —where convictions have been sus-
tained. In England and America cautionary
bills are still published, but no names are given, as
some few years ago a substantial verdict was
given against a co. for defamatory libel. Counsel’s
opinion points out that when an individual has
been fined or imprisoned for an offence, the matter
should end there, as the law has been satisfied;
but to publish a delinquent’s name from month to
month and year after year is calculated to provoke
a breach of the peace, and to do the offender
serious injury. It is alleged that several writs
have been, or are about to be, served on the Rail-
way Dept, claiming heavy damages for slander
and libel on account of these notices.

Dear Bulletin, —Re
that Benalla (Vic.) par-
son and his magic-lan-
tern, a word of warning.
In the days of my youth
a man with a lantern full
of holy slides came to
give exhibitions at our
Sunday - School (Me-
thody) in connection with
the anniversary. It was
a tremendous success
until lights were turned
up rather suddenly, and
Brother Jordan, the sup-
erintendent, was disco-
vered hugging the pale,
ascetic young sister who
taught the second-class
girls. The mischief of
turning up the lights
without warning is ob-
vious. In this instance
the efficacy of a very
zealous teacher was lost
ojr ever, and Brother
Jordan has not to this
day recovered from the
effect of the shock to his
wife’s nervous system.
That Benalla pulpiteer
should cause a bell to be
rung ssec. before crying
“let there be light.”
Light is a very good thing
—in its place.—S.S.

F. : “Se, Sis, Sim” for “he or she,”
&c. ? A neighbor (age 6) has settled a common
gender for the personal pronoun, third person
singular. He uses “ ser ” (er as in her) for nomina-
tive, possessive and objective of both sexes of the
pronoun—and no one has ever misunderstood him.
In connection with N.S.W. Govt. Astrono'
mer Russell’s nineteen-year cycle-of-drought theory
—supposed to be new —note the following from
More ion Bay Courier, date June 20, 1846 (half a
century ago)
The Rev. W. B. OlarkS, in a recent communication to
S.M.H., expresses his opinion that “Sturt’s desert,”
Leichhardt’s experience ot little or no rain even in the
trorics, SirS. L. Mitchell’s suffering from want of water,
?,r j the state of the colony generally prove this to have
been a year of drought parallel with the season, of 1826-7,
and certainly adding another link to the chain of facts
for establishing an atmospheric cycle of nineteen years.
Truly, nothing is new under the sun ; and it has
taken our scientists half-a-century since the above
was written to re-discover what Mr. Clarke noticed
and wrote about.

The Central Methodist Mission, a number
of never-touch-it associations, and something call-
ing itself the Social Reform League (meaning
Gimlet and Age-of-Consent mysteries chiefly), with
the aid of a brass band, a banner, and lots of
hideous regalia, made Sunday week noisy by
public meeting and blarey procession in the very
centre of Sydney. Yet doth the echo still vibrate
in the public ear of that hoarse shriek of the
“ unco-guid ” against the recent proposed Sabbath
demonstration of the Friendly Societies for the
benefit of the hospitals. Ah ! beloved brethren,
that was a splurge of Beelzebub ! It is an awfu’
thing to disturb the Savvbath calm for the benefit
of the sick and distressed ; it is a powerful, saintly
deed to blather and procesh on the Sawbath in
order to abolish beer and raise the “age of con-
sent. ”
He * * *
Yic. coroner’s-jury verdicts of suicide
while of unsound mind are mostly returned from
charitable motives. The other day at Williams-
town (Vic.), in the case of a young man who
suicided because he suffered from an incurable
disease, the usual verdict of “ suicide whilst of
unsound mind ” was returned. In keeping with
an old and barbarous law the local authorities
insisted on a burial in the dark, in unconsecrated
ground. This is the fifth interment of the kind in
Victoria ; and three of the five were at Williams-
town.

A leading Sydney daily indicates that the
case of Elizabeth Phillips, whose death some larri-
kins were abortively tried for causing some years
ago, justifies the Mount Rennie judicial slaughter.
The brutal truth about Elizabeth Phillips is
simply this : Whatever the larrikins did they did
when they found her lying drunk, a dead-beat
derelict, semi-moribund, on an ash-heap in
Woolloomooloo. Once she was a beautiful woman
and was “ kept ” by a very rich Sydney merchant!
Then a civil-servant of moderate means took her
in hand ; finally they parted, and she descended
lower and lower in the social scale, finally landing,
dying and destitute, on the ash-heap aforemen-
tioned. High society ruined Elizabeth Phillips
so far as outside agencies could ruin her. Just as
in the bush the blaekfellow throws over his shoul-
der to his gin the bone he has partly picked,
which bone the gin in turn throws over
ker shoulder to the outside circle of expect-
ant dogs so it was with the carc . ase 0 f
the o nce-beautiful Elizabeth Phillips. But it
was old y w fi en the dogs quarrelled over the bones
of the woman that Sydney “ society ” shrieked for
a g or y holocaust, and the virtuous press joined
i n the chorus

The Mount Rennie record : 11 tried, two
acquitted, nine sentenced to death, four hanged,
live to be released next Nov. Is it generally
known that a man, older than any of those tried,
and believed to be the ringleader, was, although
the police held a warrant for him, never captured '!
Fact.
* * * *
A man was found on the verge of starva-
tion in the bush near Taradale, Vic., the other
day, sentenced to a month’s imprisonment at Cas-
tlemaine, and died in the gaol, all within 30 hours.
.That is about the size of things in Victoria just
now—many poor men have the option of living in
gaol or dying there.

In many of the railway-passes given to the
Melbourne unemployed the “ Mr.” on the form is
cut out by the contemptuous pens of the dude
clerks.

One of the pleasantest, coolest, most
central and best-appointed restaurants in Sydney
is the Manhattan Dining Room, situated in the
basement of the great Equitable Building in
George-street, Sydney. It is in the middle of the
busiest portion of Sydney, and is in every respect
a high-class establishment with good cooking, good
attendance, a lavish bill of fare, and luxurious
fittings and furnishings.*

' *■* * * *
The tone of the Gordon anniversary enter-
tainment at the Freemasons’ Hall, Melb., was
cleverly expressed in the cross-headings of the Age
report—“ An Australian Poets’Night ; Remarks
by Lord Brassey.” And yet, strange to say, the
committee forgot to enrich the programme with a
recitation of Fitz-Gribbon’s “Ode to the bun-
beam,” a specimen of Australian poetry which far
surpasses some of the stuff that comes in for ad-
miring comment.
* * * *
At the so-called Australian Poets’ night,
held at Melb. on the Lindsay Gordon anniversary,
“under the patronage and presence” of Guv-
mentouse and a long string of knights and “nice
people,” the programme of songs and recitations
represented Australian poetry by Gordon, Win.
Gay, Kendall, and Marcus Clarke, three of whom
are, with very few exceptions, totally unfit for
recitative purposes, while Marcus Clarke, though
an excellent versifier, has hitherto never been
accused of being a poet by his best friends. But
the songs and recitations were obviously a mere
peg on which to hang the dreary sandwiched
orations of three or four individuals who desired
to pose in the public eye as critics and patrons of
literature. Gov. Brassey spoke a “piece -a
rambling, steeplechasing speech it was evidently
under the impression that the swallow-tailed
collection before him was composed of local Horners
and Sapphos, and he ladled out wholesome advice
on the penning of iambics in the same spirit
as if advising Sunday-school children to keep in
the path of virtue. T. P. MTnerney also took
the floor, and made a. rew inconsequential remarks
which would nave been equally applicable to the
tick,-,pest or the Amended Factories Act. Prof.
Tucker's observations were principally confined to
picking Gordon to pieces. According to the prof.,
Gordon was guilty of “rugged diction, crude
rhythm, and faulty rhyme ” ; certainly a discovery
in the second instance, as, up to now, everybody
has regarded Gordon as a master of rhythm.
Mr. Tucker also held that future editions of
Gordon should include only his racing ballads,
the ambitious efforts (presumably the “Dedica-
tion,” the “Basket of Flowers,”* and ah
those poems his readers have most revelled
in, including the unsurpassable “Where shall
we gather our garlands glad ”) to be dis-
carded ! The lowest, deepest depth, how-
ever, was reached by Henry Guileless T urner,
who fired off a fulsome laudation of Guy
Boothby, Alex. Sutherland, and other such
people, strangely omitting the Age obituary-
man and Purtell, the football bard! Then
the man of balance-sheets turned, with a heart-
broken voice, to Paterson and Lawson, of
whom he spoke with bated breath, as if he was
saying “blanky” before ladies. “The pheno-
menal sale of the books of these men,” said Turner,
“ causes the judicious (Turner?) to grieve. It is
surprising to find men like Brunton Stephens and
Rolf Boldrewood classing them with Gordon,
solely because their work so closely resembles his
as to be a mere imitation.” Fancy any sane man
who knows enough English to read without having
to spell out the long words seeing the least simi-
larity between Gordon and the two live men !
Gordon wrote horse-ballads and ‘ 1 Banjo wrote
horse-ballads, and there the resemblance of the
two stops short. But between Gordon and Law-
son there is not anywhere the faintest suspicion of
a likeness —not even likeness of subject. The man
who would make such a comparison would accuse
Pattie Brown of stealing Irving’s manner of acting
or charge Pinero with plagiarising George Darrell.
By the way, little or nothing seems to have been
said on the occasion about Victor Daley, far and
away the cleverest of Australian songsmiths—as
will be discovered presently, when his book of
verses comes out.
* * * *
Gov. Brassey, cycling down a hill at Cam-
berwell (Vic.), last week, lost his pedals, and,
when the accumulated speed had reached a bat
something like Parsons’s best, the front wheel
struck a snag and spilled H.E. on the road. A
scraped nose and jaw were the sum total of the
injuries to God’s anointed, but the mount, though
only slightly damaged, was utterly unndeable.
“ Uncle Tom ” at once solicited the assistance of a
passing boy, to whom he confided his identity,
and, handing the knickerbockered youth half-a-
crown, bade him take the injured jigger to Guv-
mentouse, where a similar coin would await him.
The Baron then trained to Melb., and has since
been sitting in his study gazing pathetically over
the Domain. For neither the small hoy, nor the
bicycle, nor the half-crown has yet turned up.
* * * *
Northerners fondly imagined they had
settled the tick question by dipping their cattle.
It has, however, just been found that kangaroos,
plain turkeys, and even wild ducks convey the
pest from place to place. Waterfowl acquire the
“ plaguey critters ” from masses of floating vege-
tation, and land birds simply swarm with them.
The only decent remedy shortly will be the soak-
ing of Bananaland in kerosene, turpentine, and
whisky, and allowing the whole country to burn
steadily for a week.
» * * *
In a case tried recently before the
Northern Supreme Court the defendant was the
wife (European) of a Chow who had levanted.
The lady averred she had sought her spouse
in both China and Japan—but all in vam. It was
suggested in Court for the lady’s comfort that her
husband had probably married again—Chinese
custom being by no means averse to bigamy, inas-
much as John believes that in cases of monogamy
“ she fightee he,” while, with a plurality of wives,
“she fightee she.”
Mrs. BROWN POTTER, as “ Charlotte Corday."
(Photo, specially taken for The Bulletin by Falk, Sydney and Melbourne.)
The N.S.W.* system of rent-allowance to
State school head-masters is variegated with cases
of hardship and friendship. In places, residences
have been built adjoining the schools and here
the head-master is compelled to dwell whether he
likes it or not. Some of these “ vested residences
are too small teacher’s family, while others
are too large, and others are pitched m the most
unsavoury localities. The residence to one Syd-
ney first-class school is built in a dirty cul-de-sac,
and commands an uninterrupted view of a large
assortment of municipal night-carts placed directly
opposite the front-door. The teacher, after
giving the place a fair trial, resolutely refused to
live there, and the place has since been let to an

* * * *
The isle of Tristan d’Acunha has a popula-
tion of 15 males and 45 females. From the wreck
of the Allanshaw last January there was washed
ashore a solitary man, who was immediately
rushed by the 30 superfluous women, one of whom
wooed him while he was wet, and married him
before he could wash the salt out of his hair. Ihe
29 others are still watching the horizon.*
It is time somebody asked N.S. W. Colonial
Secretary how much Sydney Benevolent Asylum
made last year out of nurse-diplomas at three
guineas each. Also, whether it is in the public
interest to charge such a useful and frequently so
ill-paid a class of women a three-guinea tax for
an official acknowledgment of their competence ?

ted. Fancy giving your

farm-gat', as security for

* * * *
The well-known Melb. abortionist' now
under sentence of death is a tall and remarkably
handsome woman with a nerve of steel. She took
her sentence quite unconcernedly, and, waving
her hand to a pal in the audience, called lra-
la-la, Billy,” without a tremor. Despite the foot
that she has had two “ lets-off ” already, the Ex-
ecutive is unlikely to carry out the death-sentence,
though the death of her client was the result of
sheer carelessness—the use of an unclean instru-
ment —a blunder worse than a crime.
At the trial of the abortionist aforesaid,
some witnesses swore that accused had success-
fully procured abortion with them. This opens
up some phases of enquiry that should whet the
appetites of the Gimlet and Age-of-Consent party.
Is the procuring of abortion a crime? Or is the
crime only in the killing of a patient while
attempting to procure abortion ? Also, if the
abortioner is guilty of a crime, how about the
abortionee? Further (with the Rapiport, &c.,
“conspiracy” case in mind), if the Melbourne
witnesses who swore as hinted above were abor-
tionees, then they were (in an understood sense)
also charged with the accused, and they need not
have given any evidence on that ground.

No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.

Sydney, June 30, 1896.
Mv Dkar Moorabinda—
The convivial supper given at the Hotel Aus-
tralia by Williamson and Musgrove to farewell the
Trilbyites and welcome the Chinatown co., was a
great success. Of course, the new co. were a little
rocky (having only landed that morning after a
voyage that was rather a nightmare—especially, it
appears, to the plump tenor, who had a misfit
berth all the way) and there was a disposition to
hail the waiter as “steward” on the part of the
new co., which is a very nice co. seen without its
paint. Also, lam led to believe that the morale
of the procession is good. When the genial
J.C.W., in his happily-delivered speech, touched
on the fact of there being few amusements for
actors out of business hours in Australia—no
players’ clubs, for instance—a male voice cheer-
fully chipped in : “No matter ; we’re all married.”
And so all the men are—with one exception, but
which one he is I will not divulge out of mercy to
a lonely stranger who may not know how to pro-
tect himself in foreign parts.
Cigarettes went round for the ladies, but none
of them smoked—not even Trilby ? whom some
people accused of enjoying her “ business ” smokes
in the play. And I was interested to see that the
girl who kicked highest in “ Trilby ” drank lemon-
ade. Everything was orthodox—if you except
that one of the new co. took two arms and a long
time to re-tie the neck-scarf of the jovial “ Trilby ”
stage-manager, although the scarf looked to me to
be all right; but there are some women who hate
to see a man’s tie going awry. The supper-tables
were full of interest, and one caught glimpses of
many well-known faces between the jonquils and
ferns of the decorations. At the “ disciples ” end
J.C.W. sat between Edith Crane, in a dark dress
spotted with blue and a regular garden of natural
crimson roses in her hair and on her corsage, and
Bessie Clayton, premiere danseuse of the new co.,
who is 18 years old and has been married two
years, but who looks like an infant (stage variety)
in spite of it.
Then came Svengali Fax, too tired to make a
speech and wearing a lightning-rod-canvasser’s
costume, and he was balanced on the other side by
Harry Conor, funny-man-in-chief of the new co.,
who made a temperance oration which wasn’t
audible at my end of the festive board. The most
soul-stirring Chinatown youth seen at a private
view is Arthur Pacie, the tenor, whose wife, a
charming little woman, is along too, let me warn
the d.c. The Chinatown girls (who are not so
married as the Chinatown youths) have a refined
style and a tricky way of dressing reminiscent of
the Gee Gees. At the supper all—barring Patrice
—wore high frocks, and they mostly sported ab-
surd little theatre-toques (if you can give such a
matter-of-fact name to airy arrangements of a
sprinkle of aggressive rosebuds and a paste buckle
or so) which gave a novel appearance to the supper-
table. And the vocabulary of the newcomers was
fresh, too. “ I was hypering daown street,” for
example, is a phrase that cannot be cleared at one
mental leap. And when you are suddenly advised
to “ get a hustle ” it takes outside assistance to let
you know that you are only being asked to hurry
up. Among the guests invited to the supper were
Mr. and Mrs. Brough, Mr. and Mrs. Bland Holt,
Mr. George Darrell, dear old Harwood, Mr. and
Mrs. Treasurer Goodman, and others.
The English Concert Co. brought out by Stevens,
Basse, and Howell—the enterprising trio whose
last trump was Mark Hambourg, you remember—
opened at Sydney Centenary Hall in the presence
of Gov. and Lady Hampden and the usual V.R.
semi-scoop which bites a good bit out of the
reserved chairs. Novelties about the English co.
were that the men didn’t wear gloves, and that
the programmes were books of the words a la
Liedertafel bills—a useful help “ when singers
sing in chorus,” but which leads one to surmise
that the artists are prone to chew the libretto when
only soloists are in question. This, however, was
not the case.
The importations are four strong. Mass Thudi-
chum, the soprano, is tall and dark, with a well-
cultivated and expressionful voice and a pro-
nounced taste in dress —geranium-red silk is
rather aggressive. And when Miss T. came on
and stated in high relief that her “ love was like a
flaming sword,” I couldn’t help thinking that her
taste in dress matched her affections. Miss
M'Kenzie, the contralto, is plump and fair, and
do butted in scrumptious white satin brocade
faked with gold and silver, trhs-decollete and
long-tailed, as if most of the bodice material had
melted into the skirt. Her warble is a small,
sweet contralto with a similar range, I should
think, to that possessed by the baritone of the
“Autocrat of the Breakfast'Table,” and is.free
from the masculine quality which distinguishes
other contraltos—Miss Nagel, for example. Of
the men of the party, baritone Douglas Powell
runs away with the public fancy. He is an aide-
de-campish youth with a lovely gloss on his linen
and a curly mo., and he has what I would
describe as a very clever voice. It is not very big
or bouncing, but it is always well-pitched, clear,
direct and unaffected, without a suspicion of exag-
geration or straining unduly after effect, although
he lets himself go and doesn’t spare the agony.
Mr. Powell was popular from the jump—with a
fatal popularity which means double encores and,
alas, double work. But he seems to revel in it.
Mr. Edward Branscombe (a “lay vicar,” whatever
that may mean) is a pale, be-ringed tenor with a
carefully-trained moustache and a leaning for
pianissimo in the upper register—which appeals to
the cultivated few so long as it is understood that
the gentleman can howl like forty dingoes at the
same altitude if needs be. A tenor who always
tapers off to a womanish whisper cannot hope to be
the apple of the Sydney public’s eye, which prefers
a vocalist who appears to be bursting his collar-
stud. Herr Vollmar gave delightful music on his
’cello, and J. Eyebrows Sykes accompanied with
his usual aplomb.
Another concert on the same evening was
“ Daddy ” Hallewell’s pupils’ splash at Sydney
Y.M.C.A. The budding vocalists gave Daddy
the best advt. he ever had in his life, several of the
singers being of the “coming” variety. Haydn,
Meyerbeer, Gounod, and Blumenthal were some
of the “tough” composers tackled by the Halle-
well students.
The new Yankee co. broke loose energetically in
“A Trip to Chinatown,” at Sydney Lyceum, last
Saturday, before a house crammed to the top
shingle—and it was noticeable, too, that the nearer
people got to the shingles the greater success they
considered the performance—though it is as idle
to seriously “ consider ” musical trifles of this sort
as it would be to hold an inquest on a dead butter-
fly. The chief charm of this kind of assault on
common sense is that deep-thinking is fatal to it—
you can spoil a musical trifle by bringing too much
logic to bear on it, just as easily as you can spoil
champagne with sodawater. The dressing of the
“ trifle ” was suitably gay. Geraldine M'Cann, as
the Widow, mostly looked like the girl who gets
sketched on the forefront of smart picture-papers.
Her first dress of black satin, smothered in jet,
and made with jutting skirt and sprouting sleeves,
produced the best effect, for her second garment
was so like No. 1, with the sleeves lopped off, that
it hardly counted. The harlequin masquerade
frock in black and white was Erenchy, but Miss
M‘Cann (weird sort of nom-de-theatre for a girl to
choose) best proved her title to good looks by look-
ing her sonsiest as a grey Quakeress in a demure
little bonnet.
Sadie MacDonald, as the French maid, without
whom no musical trifle is complete, was principally
attired in bows of ribbon—her prettiest turn-out
being cinnamon and cherry-color, and her most
gorgeous a “ mermaid ” costume of sea-green satin
and lace, with hat foaming with feathers. Nellie
Butler’s chiefest garment was a wallpapery-yellow-
red-and-green brocade, and Miss Stone was remi-
niscent of the guest in grand opera in her blue
evening-dress, but her pretty singing-voice atoned
for the absence of ginger in her toilet. Miss Stone
has the prettiest voice in the co. As it was with
the departed Gee-Gees, the alleged prima-donna of
the expedition has the smallest voice of the lot.
The Chinatown ladies believe in lots of make-up,
but I scarcely see the reason of the ’Murkan trick
of painting the outside of the nostrils red. Nellie
Butler was the worst offender in this respect. But,
perhaps, it’s only a fleeting fad, picked up when
influenza had the floor, and a red nose was a sign
of distinction? That’s what it suggests, at any
rate.
Bessie Clayton, the premiere danseuse, is a most
peculiar dancer. Her toe-solo suggests a play of
strong emotions as much as a frantic pas-seul.
When she flopped at the finish in a limp bundle
on the floor she looked to me like the husk of a
maiden after the seven devils of dancing had got
away with her soul. Miss Clayton’s dancing is
most bizarre and unearthly—one might almost say
demoniac—and if her taste in dress was corres-
pondingly fantastic her “turn” would be a very
magnetic item. But her whole appearance was
raw, and her frock abjectly commonplace—a point-
less pink-and-white mixture with black gloves
several inches too short to be effective. A girl
who can dance like that ought to prefer rather to
suggest one of Macbeth’s witches than the belle of
a suburban tea-party—when she’s got ’em all on.
The author’s folks are cheap in “Chinatown.”
Willie Grow (played by Patrice), for instance, an-
nounces himself as an agent for perfumes,
whereat the girls pat him rapturously on the back!
Now, in the Gee-Gees’ farce, “In Town,” the
“ boy ” was a peer—Lord Clanside. Therefore, 1
wasn’t surprised in “A Trip to Chinatown” when
one of the gay young sparks shook hands with the
waiter at the supper-club—it seemed to be quite
in keeping with the general atmosphere. Though
perhaps lam wrong. To shake hands with the
waiter may be the latest diplomacy of the creamiest
men of the world when you come to seriously con-
sider the affair.
The Trebelli concert last Friday evening at
Sydney Town Hall plainly proved that, in the
vocal world, the comet may sometimes have a rag-
tail without disastrous results. Never was there a
concert from which the padding protruded so un-
mistakeably, and yet, to the confusion of Sydney
audiences be it said, all hands got loud recalls.
Madame Trebelli, in pale peach-pink satin
brocade with soft lace and diamonds, sang ex-
quisitely, the large hall being just the right size
for her obedient voice. Everything she sang
gained treble recalls, her last encore being Patti’s
“Laughing Song,” of which Trebelli gives the
finest Australian rendering ujj to date. And as a
reward for her finished and charming vocalisation
somebody handed Madame a wreath of laurel big
enough to fit the late Premier Parkes. These in-
discriminate garlands of honor irritate me beyond
measure. Imagine the cool egotism of a person of
no social or official importance solemnly handing
bay leaves to an artist! They treat the solemn
and sacred wreath of laurel, which can only come
backed by the applause of nations, as if it was so
much sarsaparilla—in Sydney everybody gets
crowned with gum until the circlet loses all signi-
ficance. Edgar ’cello Strauss really assisted at the
Trebelli concert, and J. E. Sykes accompanied—
not forgetting a German youth, Herr Scheinpflug,
who played Trebelli’s flute accompaniments in a
style reminiscent of Lemmone.
From W. H. Paling and Co. I have lately re-
ceived the “Delightful” gavotte, by W. A. Caf-
lisch, conductor of the Brisbane Liedertafel.
“ The Delightful ” is a pleasant composition in the
conventional gavotte style, which is so full of rests
and staccato marks that the person who plays it
always looks Ike a cat on hot bricks. I am also
in receipt of the “Florence, schottische by J.
Holland. A very excellent portrait of Flo Young
livens up the cover, but the music is of the most
tin-pot description. Mr. Holland approaches a
charming actress under the guise of melody, and
gets everything into his programme but the music.
Next Thursday (July 9th) Lady Hampden opens
the N.S.W. Poultry, Pigeon, Canary, and Dog
Society’s annual champion show at Sydney Exhi-
bition Building. I hear that the entries in the
poultry sections are “numerous and handsome,”
and the staging of the birds and 1 beasts will receive
special attention. A. J. Gray, Eldon Chambers,
is still hon, sec. to the society. On July Bth the
Chemists’ Assistants’ annual ball takes' place at
Paddington Town Hall; on the 10th the steam-
ship officials’ invitation-dance occurs at the same
place ; and on 20th the council and committee of
the Institute of Bankers and of the Australasian
Economic Association give a conversazione at the
Equitable Buildings, from 8 to a quarter to 11, at
which Gov. Hampden will be present.
There is, after all, some gratitude in the world.
Mrs. MacDonald (nee Kate Slattery), one of
Sydney’s most philanthropic vocalists, was recently
presented with an illuminated address and enter-
tained at a ball and banquet by the residents of
Thornleigh (Milson’s Point line), as a token of
gratitude for Mrs. MacDonald’s charitable efforts
to raise money for the local poor by a series of con-
certs. By this means she raised £7B, for which the
grateful inhabitants now publicly testimonialise
her. The address is on view in Paling’s window.
A National Council of Women is the newest
women-suffrage spasm in Sydney. The inaugural
meeting was held in the Centennial Hall the other
Friday (the new woman is evidently not super-
stitious), and Lady Hampden, who had been some-
how lassoed into the chair, made far and away the
cleverest speech. Lady H., of course, expressed
her pleasure at being present, though she really
had no idea what sort of object the gathering had
in view—whether it was a scheme for the abolition
of everything, or merely a friendly discussion as
to the psychological reasons why thin-legged
women object to Bloomers. So her Ex. gracefully
and diplomatically confessed that she was there as
a figurehead before the secretary had time to com-
promise her.
When all the chaff is blown away the wheat of
the National Council of Women is that another
grabby association is being violently thrust on the
public in order that the emancipated society old
maids of itinerant tendencies and lots of time both
before and behind ’em may have the chance of
careering from Dan to Beersheba as “delegates”
attending female conferences, where they justify
their existence in waves of jaw-jaw mountains high.
And, although the National Council of Women
sounds an expansive order, embracing, one would
think, the votes of a nation of intelligent females,
it is after all only another frantic attempt of the
old, forlorn suffrage jackdaws—with “Lady Hamp-
den in the chair, and Lady Darley vice-president,”
as the fabled disguising peacock’s feathers.
The complimentary benefit concert tendered by
the musicians of Sydney to organist Wiegand in
the Centennial Hall last Saturday, attracted the
biggest paying crowd ever jammed into that vast
building. Gov. and Lady Hampden and daughters
had dreadful positions in 'the very van of the con-
cert, where they couldn’t see the performers with-
out cricking their necks, and where the noise must
have been deafening. The V.R. dressing was
principally top wrap, the gayest item on the carpet
of patronage being a glorious basket of committee
flowers. In the interval the hero of the evening
M. Wiegand, was presented to Gov. Hampden
at His Ex. ’s special request, and that pleasant-
mannered potentate expressed his joy at the suc-
cess of the whole thing, although, judging by the
desperate manner in which H.E. grasped himself
bv the scruff of the neck, all the evening, it
almost looked as if he had to hold himself back
from es aping into the night.
The weak point of the Wiegand concert was
that there was too much organ - it got to sound as
it the City Council were working a bit of overtime
out. of Mons. W. so as to get ahead of his six
months spell. The programme was long and
effective. Madame Kellerman (who wore a verv
elegant mauve silk and got a great reception oil'
her first appearance after her recent trip to Euro lie)
Miss King, Miss Evelyn, Mrs. Riley, Miss Mylott
Daddy HallevVell, Simonsen, Edgar Strauss
and four clever accompanists, with the Second
Regiment band added, were responsible for a large
and brilliant concert with more fanfare and din
than is usual, and much more money in front too.
•louu Laure was hon. sec. to this most successful
ecu. hr.
A Sydney bazaar lately attended by Lady
Hampden was an amazing turn-out. There were
four stalls, each about the size of a small toilet-
table, whereon were exposed for sale articles of an
inconceivably trumpery kind. No wonder the
aide, who stalked behind the Government House
party, laughed behind his hat as by V.R. orders
ne purchased six-pennyworth of gingerbread. I
shan’t wonder if, after this experience, Lady
Hampden declines to visit any bazaar at which the
show has not previously been inspected by one of
the vice-regal suite.
Writes my Melb. correspondent :
“ The promoters of the Seamen’s Institute Con-
cert, at Port Me 1 bourne, held a grand hand of
trumps in the promise of two songs by a real live
Earl, as many more from the Governor’s son-in-
law, and 10 minutes of ‘ hum-hum, er-er, and
yah-yah ’ oratory from the vice-regal Ancient
Mariner himself. But some people would sing
small if they had at their beck and call for the
charity platform the services of Her Most Gracious
and Albert Edward with his banjo. At all events,
the faint-hearted Port Melbournians parted with
their advantages dirt-cheap—two shillings each for
the very best seats.
“ The thrill of the evening came when Earl
Shaftesbury proceeded to try conclusions with
‘ The Bloom is on the Rye ! ’ Pink facings and
nine beauteous gold buttons, ‘ three down each
side and the same up the middle’ (as audibly
enumerated by a long-shore mermaiden), were all
in his favor, and he was tumultuously encored.
All the same, his wrinkled white vest, as here-
tofore, was palpably in need of judicious
‘ boning.’ Willowy Lord Hopetoun put his
whole trust in whalebone ; and the one Earl of the
Brassey menage, plump though he be, might do
worse than follow his example. To return to the •
programme, the singer’s vocal treatment of his
‘pretty Jane’ was tunefully insinuating. What
with a sweet tenor voice and unaffected utterance,
even male hearers found only one fault—the per-
former’s mannerism of wriggling in his bits of
things as if, in musical ecstasy, he meditated an
upward flight, leaving behind him buttons, weskit
and all.
“For some reason, the entertainment suffered
from a congestion of mayoralty, which, from a
non-municipal point of view, had its drawbacks.
In the end, when the City Fathers grew dazed
with sitting too long in a speechless row near an
unrestrained piano, they apparently conceived the
delusion that a Council-meeting was in progress.
At all events, they moved and seconded like mad,
and proposed votes of thanks to all and sundry,
especially the various mayors. Then the latter
called upon one another to respond for the ladies,
just as if it was a wedding, and everyone declined
the office on the ground that his own private,
particular mayoress was present. Finally, they
shifted the task on to surgeon O’Hara, who had
sung four songs, and now rose from his seat in the
hall to protest that he’d done his duty, though he
offered in compensation to cut off a mayoral leg if
required. Meanwhile the audience, which had
paid to get in, took this display of excessive grati-
tude calmly, and joined in singing ‘ God Save ’ for
the second time that evening without audible
demur. Halfway through the glory of the night
had departed with the Governor, who was due
elsewhere, and carried off both his aides, though
each was down for a second song. After CO, when
a man’s had his own say, what else does he care
about ?
“Signor Buzzi, Melbourne’s most successful
singing-instructor, suffering from severe ill health,
is returning to Italy. The fair sex of Melbourne
adore Buzzi, who is on the concert platform what
the beauteous Ryley was on the stage.
“ Suburban mayors are rampant. They have all
altered the dates of their civic hops several times
to suit vice-regal convenience, and the latest de-
velopment is that the autocratic Gov. has thrown
them all over. Mayor Nathan, of Richmond,
changed his date three times, and finally fixed upon
the evening Lord B. named. Three days after-
wards he received a letter stating that tlie Royal
Figurehead had finally decided to be absent from
Melbourne at the time. It is shrewdly suspected
that some of the other suburban potentates will
have to share Mr. Nathan’s cruel fate.
“ The Caledonian ball, as a social function, is a
thing of the past, and this year’s rally of the braw
Scot was a pronounced frost. Vice-royal tv arr i ved
a few hours late, and was led into the hall by a
party of pipers, preceded by a magnificently meaty
specimen of a Highlander. After some agonised
rushing hither and thither, the first set was made
up, but with the wrong people included, whereat
the right people ground their false teeth and
smiled bitterly. A cosy nook had been contrived
in the ballroom, behind a screen, where the extra
special vice-regal supper was laid. Even this
proved a failure. His Ex. flatly refused to be
privately enclosed by a screen, and insisted upon
patronising the general refreshment-room. Falls,
partial or complete, were a frequent cause of bad
language. The noble Shaftesbury, when waltzing
with a Potteresque damsel, by strenuous exertion,
just managed to save himself from an undignified
cropper, and, at one awful point of the proceed-
ings, Lord B.’s equilibrium trembled in the bal-
ance. However, the great man made a clever
recovery, and the Caledonian ball breathed
again. ”
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

S.M. Herald announces that the total
capacity of its three machines is 72,000 copies per
hour. For many hours? Probably not more than
18, anyhow.
Time deals kindly with the woman who
uses “ Babeskin ” soap. It softly removes the tell-
tale lines of the year, and renews the delicate
bloom of youth. One shilling packages of two
cakes. All chemists.*
The Crimnedah Land Board, after sitting
for five months and taking Ocvvt. of evidence,
decides that the Mercadool dummying charges are
fully proved and that the law has been evaded,
bona-fide settlement impeded, and the country
swindled as usual. And there is the usual appeal.
The widely-known Australasian firm of
Baker and Rouse, photographic manufacturers,
importers and dealers in each of the provinces, has
been formed into a limited company, and continues
in possession of this continent with the mystic
letters “Ltd.'’ added to its name. Its head-
quarters and agencies in each province are now
undergoing the process of enlargement and exten-
sion, and when that is completed they will have a
fairly octopus-like grip of South Sea geography,
and be in a position to assist every amateur and
professional photographer under the Cross in the
development of his individual happiness.*

Recent gold-yields in many of the leading
Victorian mines have been considerably augmented
by the introduction of Wynne and Tregurtha’s
patent battery plates, which are being manufac-
tured by Messrs. J. Miller and Co., of I)ana-street,
Ballarat. These plates, by catching the fine gold
and pyrites, generally increase the yield by 10 per
cent. They are also invaluable in the treatment
of refractory ores.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
S.A. Agent-General Play ford will probably
resign before Christmas, and S.A. politicians al-
ready speculate about his successor. The two
most likely are Treas. Holder and Speaker Coles,
with odds in favor of the former.
One outside contractor, who could give
weighty evidence re departmental blundering, was
approached by Varney Parkes re the Carter-
Gummow Commission ; but he didn’t bite. The
amount involved was about <£ll,ooo.
The N.S.W. Lands for Closer Settlement
Bill has apparently dropped out of sight. And
people have almost given up wondering whose
land was to be resumed behind the back of Parlia-
ment if that amazing measure got through.
Jock MTntyre, who is vaguely understood
to lead the Vic. Opposition, has only one fault to
find with the Govt, just now. It gets too much
fulsome praise to please Jock—who, by the way,
was never fulsomely praised himself when he was
a Minister.
The public mind of Australia is fairly ripe
now for the consideration of a Bill to abolish all
State gratuities except those conferred on the
f round of poverty. Free passes on railways, and
tate deadheadism of every kind, should be
brought under vote.
‘ ‘ Frank Cotton the first to impress the
Single-tax on the Australian mind ! ” Why, in
1878 Prof. Pearson wrote in Melb. Age a series of
articles in favor of taxing the unearned increment.
The Georges, the Cottons, and others would profit
by reading those articles.
Rockhampton politicians, not content with
soliciting Max O’Rell’s assistance to boom Separa-
tion, recently interviewed Governor Lamington
thereon. Outside Rockhampton the Separation
barometer is down to zero. In the North (which
fears Townsville domination) and in Townsville
itself the movement is, apparently, dead as Caesar.
In Centralia the western towns make a laughing-
stock of the Autonomy question ; and, even in
Mt. Morgan, next door to Rockhampton, the
people smile a weird and weariful smile of half
contemptuous amusement when the matter is
mentioned.
