Graham Berry’s pension is supported by
the Tories, opposed by the Rads. The whirligig
of time ! In one decade, Berry the Dictator, the
Revolutionary, the accursed of all men in black
clothes ! Another decade, Graham Berry, Speaker,
at the Melbourne Club ball!
One of David Syme’s forms of revenge is to
sulk in his paper. John Hancock, Labor-member,
has been prominent in championing Syme’s
enemy, Speight. Syme’s opportunity came when
Hancock spoke on the Address in Parliament.
His speech was reported at a quarter of a column’s
length in the Tory Argus, but the self-styled
liberal Age dismissed it in two lines, thus :
Mr. Hancock made some remarks on Socialism and other
subjects.
This is Hancock’s punishment for presuming to
say a word in favor of Syme’s foe.
The elected of the Sydney Parliamentary
constituency of Gipps and the rejected of that
constituency now sit as equally-paid members of
N.S.W. Public Works Committee, and as members
of the Legislature, with practically equal political
powers. Which shows what an idiot the Public is
when it calls its sacrosanct ballot-box the Will of
the People.
Hughes, M.P., forecast, the other night, a
coalition between Premier Reid and Opposition-
leader Lyne. And why the incredulity excited.
Has not Australia already produced a Griffith-
M.‘ll wraith, a Berry-Service, and a Parkes-Robert-
son coalition ? And, only a few months ago, did
not a Dibbs-Parkes coalition brazenly uplift its
countenance ?
British House of Lords has passed by 29
majority the second reading of the Deceased
Wife’s Sister Marriage Act long-murdered by the
bishops. Canada has thrown out a Ministry
which endeavored to cram denominational educa-
tion down the throat of Manitoba —the lay
Catholics of Quebec assisting ! Everywhere
ecclesiasticism is weakening.
The branding of Chinese-made furniture,
as proposed by the N.S.W. Shops and Factories
Bill, can be defended plausibly enough by Protec-
tionism, but it is not Freetrade. In fact, almost
everything the Freetrade Reid Govt, does, or pro-
poses to do, provokes the howl from its ruck,
“This is agen our Freetrade principles.” The
Reid opportunism is not Freetrade.
It is an insult to civilisation for anyone
who took part in or approved of the methods of the
infamous Mount Rennie trial to offer ‘ ‘ mercy ” to
the surviving prisoners. They were citizens, and
as such were surely entitled to_ as‘deliberate and
dispassionate a trial as is ordinarily given in a
promissory-note dispute. Instead of which they
got a trial which would have disgraced Judge
Lynch.
The terrible catastrophe at Moscow, where-
by 8000 hungry citizens were killed or injured,
seems to have ended in a terribly funny joke for
the cablegrammer. “ During the course of the
inquiry- evidence was given implicating several
high officials of the empire in the disaster. The
Czar thereupon stopped the inquiry, and a verdict
was returned that the catastrophe was the act oi
God. ” The high officials were acting for the A 1
mighty, as usual.
Melbourne Age in effect issues orders that
Premier Turner is not to levy extra taxation to
make up the deficit, but trusts things righting
themselves next year. On this point it is time for
Turner and Syme to part company. Finance is
the weak point in all the Australian Governments,
and there are always too many of them trusting
that things will “come right.” The business of
the Treasurer is to seize things with both hands
and put them right.
In Queensland premises used for “ church
purposes” are exempt from taxation, and this
system has been brought to such a state of perfec-
tion that if services are habitually held on a vacant
allotment it thereby dodges the tax. Therefore,
FOREIGNERS.—(A Melbourne Sketch from Life.)
Fat Man encourages public worship on his corner
block to keep down the expenses till a buyer comes
along. In all the Australias the church dodges
taxation more or less —which is the old endowment
business in a mean, underhand, objectionable
form. If the aggregate church property of a pro-
vince escapes, by this process of exemption, .£5OOO
per annum of local and general taxation, then the
rest of the community must pay it. Therefore,
the churches are compulsorily endowed to that
extent by a community which thinks it has abol-
ished State aid to religion.
Although the N.S.W. Opposition declined
to help the N.S.W. Labor-members to torpedo
the Reid Government over the iniquitous Tooth
Brewery business, the same Opposition expected
the same Labor-members to help it to torpedo the
same Government over one of the minor expletives
of the Dean Curse.
Sydney Daily Telegraph clamorously
backs up a plebiscite as the best means of ascer-
taining the public will on all questions—except
Freetrade. That, says the D.T., has been settled
by “as distinct an expression of public opinion
as a plebiscite could bring forth.” Of course.
That is the D.T.’s fiscal doxy.

MRS. FEATHERWEIGHT : “ You’re sure you won't rum away
with me?”
CABBY: " No, mem; I’ve been married, this twinty year!"
Q. Parliamentarians are open to the charge
of having reduced payment of members from =2300
to £l5O a year, to prove that the pruning-kmfe
applied by the hand of depression was no respec-
ter of persons, and of now reconstructing them-
selves to <£3oo, “ from the return of the writ,
leaving the retrenched and exiled, mostly the
socially and politically friendless, in the cold,
wintry “soup.”
The formation of the S.A. Liberal Associa-
tion, with a programme almost on all-fours with
the announced Govt, policy, has completely taken
the wind out of the Ass party’s sails. b.A. pro-
ducers, prejudiced against some of the Labor-
party’s proposals, cordially detest lat Man s
proposed abolition of members’payment and free
education, and the new association just strikes the
happy mean. The Ass press, in its anger, de-
scribes the new league as a “ Labor move !
Melb. Argus celebrated the jubilee of Free-
trade, last week, with -a sub-leader that warmed
the very cockles of Fat Man’s soul. Thus : It
was on June 25, 1846, that the repeal of the Corn
Laws was finally passed by the House of Lords,
and on the following day the touch of the Royal
sceptre swept away those and. a hundred other
burdensome duties.’’ Welike “ touch of the Royal
sceptre ” well enough, but why not “ wave of the
monarchial wand ” ?
A series of articles on “ old age pensions,”
by a well-known Australian writer, have been
appearing in the Argus. He seemed to have a
free hand in the treatment, and the first two
articles were worthy of the Democratic press,
although their trend was skilfully repudiated m
the leading-columns. But after the second ap-
peared, and a lot of people had furiously enquired
why this Socialistic stuff was appearing, the
articles tailed off into the usual Argus style of
thing.
How does solicitor X. feel when sending a
brief to Premier Y. ? Are there not many little
State services which Premier Y. may do for
solicitor X. ; and when X. persuades a client
that Y. is just the man to have influence with the
court, is it possible he is wholly unmindful of
these possibilities ? Again, is it quite the irre-
proachable thing for Premier Y. to plead before
District Court Judges whose elevation to the
Supreme Court Bench is largely conditional upon
Y. ’s favor ?
Chief Secretary Brunker’s probably earnest
assertion that “ everything possible in the way of
home comforts” —pause and consider, individual
reader, what you would mean by “home com-
forts” —is supplied “without stint” to the in-
mates of N.S.W. Asylums, is an instance of the
gullibility of red-tapeism. Brunker thinks so,
and Brunker-sent reporters say so, because when-
ever Brunker or a Brunker envoy visits an asylum,
the whole caboose and its administration are
readied-up. No inspectorial eyes are ever let see
behind the scenes in any “ Guv’ment institoo-
shun.” If the Public Service Board wants to
know the truth about matters, let them connive at
a dummy pauper going through all the rigors, of
“ the system.” Or let the Government examine
“ cantankerous ” paupers, guaranteeing them pro-
tection from subsequent official retaliation.
Ex-Chief Justice Lilley on those silken
bonds “ Why should we [Australians] depend
for our peace on the Sultan’s pleasure, or a frown
of a Fatima or Haidee, or on a lapse by Ayesha,
or on the Czar’s liver or surfeit of cabbage-soup,
or on the divine German Wilhelm’s disgust with
das Enylischer blut in his Imperial veins ?”
Gov. Brassey’s speech reports that the loss
of traffic occasioned by the failure of the harvest
has caused a serious deficiency in the income
derived from Yic. railways during the current
financial year. Among the railway hands it is
roundly declared that the bad harvest was cpiite
as much as the rolling-stock could carry, anyhow.
The N.S.W. lawyer-bossed
Ministry which, in the interest
of the legal trades-union, has
steadfastly blocked all discussion
concerning the merits of the
brand of justice dispensed by
Mr. Justice Windeyer, cannot
escape putting that official in
the Parliamentary pillory should
it desire to have the province
pay a portion of his Privy
Council salary. But the ques-
tion can hardly arise. That the
rest of the Australias should be
willing to let a man with Mr.
Windeyer’s judicial record re-
present this country on any
tribunal is absolutely incon-
ceivable. There are at least
several dozen members of N. S.
Wales Assembly who would
vote for Mr. Justice Windeyer’s
dismissal from the bench were
the matter fully debated —and
there are not, The Bulletin
believes, 20 men in the House
who would vote him a shilling,
except for the purpose of get-
ting permanently rid of him.
The Lord Justices of the Privy
Council get =BOOOO a year salary
—and just think of the Aus-
tralia which makes such a mouth
over paying just about half the
money to a Railway Commis-
sioner who gives big value for
his salary, shelling out sixty
hundred pounds every year to
enable Lord Justice Windeyer
of Tomago to wear silk stockings
and flash his Dover at London
dinners !
Sundry Chinese lottery-
runners, prosecuted by Vic.
Crown Law Department at
Smellboume District Court, the other day, had
put their defence in the hands of Premier Turner’s
firm. The moral of this is quite clear. No solicitor
ought to be connected with the Government.
N.S.W. “ Lands ” Minister Carruthers will
either have to “ take a pull ” or get a little Par-
liament of his own specially to pass Land Bills.
He has still a lot to learn on the land-question,
and had much better stop tinkering and sit down
and think hard for a couple of years. Also read
Kipling’s little story, “ Tod’s Amendment,” and
let the moral soak in well. The Minister means
well, but his leg is too pullable and his methods
show a shocking lack of repose.
As a practical effect of the working of pay-
ment by results in education. A week or two ago
(writes a Bulletin correspondent) a messenger
from the headmaster of a Melb. district State
school interviewed several parents with the object
of inducing them to keep certai n of their children
at home for a month. The reason assigned for the
peculiar request was that the said children were
above the proper class age, and that their presence
would seriously affect the class results. On one
parent saying that to do so would bring him under
the provisions of the compulsory clauses of the
Act, he was informed that he need have no fear on
that score, as the head-master would see that he
was not proceeded against !
It is proposed that a conference of
Premiers be held to nominate an Australian Judge
to sit on the judicial committee of the British
Privy Council. A much more urgent need is a
conference to consider the hideous foolishness of
sending an Australian Judge 16,000 miles to hear
Australian cases, and making the cases go 16,000
miles after him and 16,000 miles home again, when
the same Judge might as well sit as a court of
appeal here and hear the same cases on the spot.
When Premier Reid wants his boots soled he
doesn’t send the shoemaker to England, and then
send the boots after him, and afterwards have
them sent back again ; he has them soled here.
Why not have his law soled here also ?
Item from Melbourne, last Thursday : —-
Mr. Finlayson, the Crown Prosecutor, applied to-day to
Mr. Justice A’Becket for the issue of a warrant for Geake’s
arrest. The application was granted.
Solicitor Geake was convicted of some very large
frauds and released, pending appeal, on A2OO bail,
which was practically a case of tying an alligator
to a straw to prevent it getting away. Geake got
away quite easily, the police apparently taking no
interest in his movements, and two weeks after
his departure is publicly known the Crown rises
up in haste and gets a warrant for his capture !
The awful velocity with which it only waits two
weeks before beginning to move in the matter
seems to show that the Crown is in a good state
of trainingand fit to run for its life in a stationary
manner, so to speak.

Personal Items.
Henry Lawson, who emphatically believes
that far-off fields are green, is on his way to Wes-
tralia.
He has arrived at last. S.M. Herald says
that Mr. Modest Maryanski, an American mineral-
ogist bound for Coolgardie, was a passenger per
the Warrimoo.
Ex-Speaker O’Rorke (M.L.) says that the
Seddon Government is giving the Maoriland
Upper House “ rope enough to dig its own
grave ” ! O’Rorke is a bhoy from Galway.
Many Sydneyites will be sorry to hear
that, when the mail left London, “Charley” Lett
was laid up, suffering from cerebral hemorrhage.
The doctors, however, expected him to pull round.
F. B. Suttor, politically quiescent at pre-
sent, has held office in N.S.W. Ministries for a
longer period, adding the occasions together, than
any other now alive. The late Parkes used to
“ top ” him.
Overheard at Melb. Law Courts the other
day : “ Hello, what case are you in ? ” “ They’re
appealing from a judgment in our favor.” “ itow
will it go ? ” “ Oh, we’re sure to lose—it’s one of
the Chief’s decisions.”
A Melb. solicitor who appeared for two
smellful justices in a recent inquiry, avers that
he has up his sleeve a card wherewith to work the
confusion of the teetotal beak primarily respon-
sible for his clients’ undoing.
During June no less than eight of Vic.
Chief Justice Madden’s judgments have been
appealed against, and of these the whole six
already reviewed have been reversed by the Full
Court. Even Griffith smiles.
When N.S.W. Speaker Abbott has ’em all
on—when he puts on the medals, ribbons, and
other insignia of his numerous offices in societies,
lodges, companies, and so forth, his capacious bust
is covered all but three sq. in.
The new N.S.W. Sheriff, Mr. Maybury, is
son-in-law of the late “Bob” Wisdom, Q.C.,
erstwhile N.S.W. Attorney-General, who went
within one vote of election as Speaker when
Wigram Allen was first chosen.
Miss Cowper, sister of the ex-Sheriff, and
daughter of an ex-N.S.W. Premier, is the only
lady who has a free pass over the N.S.W. State
railways—“because her father took part in the
introduction of railways into the colony. ”
Pious Brentnall, M.L.C., elected the
other day to the chair of Q. Leg. Council, was,
when at the Wesleyan Conference in Sydney 18
months ago, the sole articulate apologist for the
presence of the Kanaka in Queensland towns.
H : “ I was present when boomster G. W.
Taylor, at Dr. Bevan’s Melb. church, made his
offer of <£50,000 to the Congregational denomina-
tion. He carefully unfolded it, bit by bit, and
the yells of delight went nigh to lift the roof. ”
Journalist: “The mention of ex-beak
Baxter as a Tas. pensioner reminds me of a case
I once reported. The pensioned ex-Postmaster-
General of one of the Australian provinces was
fined <£2 for writing inside a posted newspaper.”
The much-injured and historical Mrs.
George Dean, and her mother, Mrs. Seymour, are
still running the little Surry Hills grocery. Busi-
ness isn’t brisk, but the gossips who come in for a
penn’orth of what they don’t want help to pay the
rent.

riety, starts on a lecturing-tour through the
N.S.W. country districts early in August; but
before leaving Sydney he promises to get a sensa-
tional speech off his chest to the electors of Philip
division.

proudly proclaimed himself the only Roman Catho-

Echuca bridge, flaunts his red guernsey, and sells

''rickett: “ No, sir ; I’m saved now. Are you ? ”

The Vic. Leg. Councillors on Gov.
Frassey’s speech were brilliant, as usual. C. J.
Ham, for instance, said that “ The hope expressed
in the speech that Queensland and Western Aus-
tralia would this year pass Federal Enabling
Bills was a consummation devoutly to be wished. ”
Convict Butler’s condemnation as weak-
minded of all persons with hair parted in the
middle, tends to confirm conjectures of the im-
becility of a certain Judge, whose hair has a six-
inch median parting. It also drops heavily on
H. B. Higgins, M.L.A., with his eight-inch part-
ing.
J. Stonham, assistant-teacher at Croydon
Park (N.S.W.) public school, standing for M.A.
degree at Sydney University a few weeks ago,
took as one of his themes the unusual subject of
ancient French, and passed triumphantly in it.
He is the first in N.S. Wales to achieve that dis-
tinction.
Looking for a house to rent around Melb.,
one notices the name “Davies, land and estate
agent,” in every window and on every allotment,
and it’s a different Davies every time. There are
in Melbourne of that name three financial agents,
four general ditto, 17 land and estate ditto—and
10 solicitors.
Lex. : What is the matter with Salomons,
Q. 0., that he should not be talked of as a possible
Australian representative on the Privy Council ?
Australia possesses no acuter intellect; and,
having held the N.S.W. Chief Justiceship, he is
technically qualified for the position under the
Imperial Act.
Prophecy is always risky. In March, ’BB
The Bulletin predicted that the late John
Ballance, of Maoriland, would be forgotten as a
politician, and remembered as the man who bred
the racer Fishhook. And now Ballance is poli-
tically immortal (moderately immortal, that is),
and only one man in five thousand, perhaps, re-
members he ever owned Fishhook.
A Vic. inventor (an ex-State school teacher)
some years ago devised an automatic mechanical
target, out of which local military authorities
reckoned he would clear at least a quarter of a
million. A Melb. syndicate sent him with his
invention to the land of J. Bull, and after five
years negotiating he has utterly failed, his chief
asset being his now unappreciated and valueless
idea. Five years ago he refused <£25,000 cash for
the thing.

When the proprietor of
the recently-deceased Evening
(Melb.) News was paying the
comps, their final wages, one of

was killed by extravagance, and you, Mr. Levi,

Astley Cooper, Bart—no relation of the
Coopers, of Sydney, by the way—has taken the
Cooper mansion at Point Piper for six months.
Residence by a real English baronet will no doubt
exorcise any plebeian association that may hang
round the edifice, and there is no telling what may
happen at the expiry of the baronet’s tenancy.
The place may even be bought as a residence for
the Governor !
The smoking parsons of Burwood and
Sydney must go the way of the lamentably-athletic
Hopper Joplin. The Rey. Hopper put his ankle
out in N. Queensland, twisted his wrist in Ballarat,
and hurt his nose in various parts of Australia,
endeavoring to be hail-fellow-well-met, for religious
ends, with the athlete worldlings about him. But
he cried at last “Vanity,” and bolted “home.”
To be anything to attain a different thing is to
play a part, and the religious play-actor must
always go.
Chevalier Antoine de Kontski, just now
giving a series of pianoforte recitals at Melb.
Bijou, may be accepted as the most remarkable
musical identity on earth. At the old age of 80
he travels on his merits as a soulful executant, not
less than on his ability to play from memory all
the finest works of the famous composers, who
knew him personally in the long, long ago. He is
himself a composer of 300 sonatas, symphonies,
oratorios, masses and operas. Had he died in
1829 he would have received the obituary notice
due to an infant prodigy—and he still lives. In
childhood, De Kontski was the petted pupil of
Beethoven ; in youth, he played marbles and duets
with Chopin ; and, since he arrived at man’s estate,
he has given more performances in public, and
kissed a greater number of royal hands, than any
other classical musician that ever was. M. de
Kontski, who is now Court Pianist to Kaiser Wil-
liam, dates his style of Chevalier from 1850, when
the King of Prussia gave him his decoration, along
with the Art and Science medal which he wears
upon his iron chest to-day.
Explorer Kennedy, the remains of whose
vehicle a Cardwell (Q.) man says he has found up
Townsville way, was of a more religious turn of
mind than modern explorers seem to be. In his
intercourse with the blacks his chief agencies of
influence were religion and rations, but mostly re-
ligion. The news of his death —the aborigines
killed him—provoked sermons in several of the
Sydney churches of the day, and St. James’s
Church even commemorates his virtues on a
tablet.
The big picture of Vic. Leg. Assembly
painted by Gordon Coutts must be a great disap-
pointment to Mr. Pursey Chirnside. That
elaborate young person is painted with his back
turned to the spectator and with his head screwed
round to show his face; but the artist gives no
glimpse of Pursey’s most prominent and precious
characteristics—his necktie and his colored waist-
coat. The artist who takes away from Pursey the
colored waistcoat takes that which not enriches
him and makes Pursey poor indeed.
That new importation from England, the
Rev. W. I. Carr Smith, of St. James’s, Sydney,
has devised a novel way of making the Church
“gee.” He has introduced smoke-concerts with
cards thrown in, being of opinion that the male
members require some “innocent diversion.” To
see the parson with his abnormally long “church-
warden ” with a back-ground of jet-black beard as
big as a patriarch’s one would fancy oneself in the
presence of a Mussulman at home with his hookah.
Mr. Smith’s mission is “to minimise as much as
possible the inordinate use of violent expletives so
characteristic of colonial society. ”

Had Gordon Coutts been a more careful
Bulletin reader he would have avoided at least
one mistake in his painting of Yic. Leg. Assem-
bly. He represents Premier Turner as delivering
an important speech—evidently a Budget—to a
full House in a smart, new, well-fitting, lavender-
colored suit. Turner simply couldn’t do such a
thing. A lavender suit may have been necessary
to the scheme of color, but as a matter of historical
veracity, the clothes ought to have been brown
old, and sacky. The Premier of the picture is a
tailor-made man, and Turner is certainly not that.
Anarchist Andrews, of Melbourne, who
appeals for funds through the Champion, is a
queer-looking personage. He takes no pride in
His attire, his usual garb consisting of a much-worn
suit far too tight and short; this, added to his
thin and straggling beard and his erratic manner
of speaking, brings him more ridicule tHan he de-
serves—for he is distinctly a man of rare and keen
intellect and of blameless honesty. The average
city man remembers when Andrews was one of
the best-dressed civil-servants in Victoria. Hap-
pening to differ from his chief (Cc-l. Templeton),
he called him a liar—which procured his dismissal
from the service.
Marcus Clarke’s retirement from the sub-
librarianship of Melb. Public Library was hastened
by his many wordy wars with the magnificent Mr.
Justice Barry, great Mogul of the establishment—
which, indeed, owes the Judge’s memory a heavy
debt of gratitude. Coming through the turnstiles
they met one day, the old man dressed impres-
sively in the heavy English fashion of sixty years
ago ; Clarke in the frailest of frail garments—his
pyjama suit. The old man stopped short. “ That
costume is unseemly,” said he. “The liberty of
the subject must be protected, sir, to dress as he
pleases, ” returned Clarke, as he slipped past.
Hon. J. G. WARD.
Photo, by Wrigglesworth and Binns.
Mrs. Johnston, relict of the late Com-
mander Johnston, R.N., died at Sydney last
week, aged 85. She was a native of England, and
came to N.S.W. in 1826 with her father, who
emigrated for the sake of the health of his two
sons. She was married in 1831 or ’32 to Lieut.
Johnston, a son of the Colonel who deposed Bligh.
Mrs. Johnston was, with a sister of* the late Sir
William Macleay, one of the last ladies in the
province to receive a grant of land upon her mar-
riage. Owing to the death of his father and elder
brother, Lieut. Johnston retired from the navy to
manage the family properties. Mrs. Johnston
never left N.S.W., and up to the departure of
Gov. and Lady Denison, she was a leader of
society. After this time she retired wholly into
private life.
A Parkes story told by Lord Jersey.
About the first public appearance the ex-Gov.
made was at the unveiling of the statue of Dr.
Lang, in Wynyard-square, Sydney. On that
occasion, Parkes made a speech—the first after his
severe cab-accident. It was a hot day, and Mayor
Burdekin, chairman of the gathering, considerately
offered the Premier a small glass of water. The
veteran ceased speaking, and motioned as though
he was going to drink it, but he only put it near
his lips, handed it back with “Hi don’t want
hany,” and resumed his address. Lord Jersey
happened to mention the incident to the old man
some time afterwards, when Parkes, with a broad
smile, said that, as it was such a small glass, he
thought it might be gin and water, but when he
got it near his nose he knew there was no gin
there.
Herewith appears the photo, of ex-Treas.
J. G. Ward, of Maoriland, whose luckless Farmers’
Association has necessitated his withdrawal, in a
badly-wrecked state, from politics. Ward started
earning his living as a message-boy in the tele-
graph-service in Southern M.L. He learnt in
spare moments to do the “ tick ” business, and got
a place at the operating board. After a while ho
deemed himself entitled to promotion and asked
therefor, but was refused on account of his youth.
He said a few hair-raising words to the boss, and
left the service ; to return to it 20 years later as
Postmaster-General, Commissioner of Telegraphs,
and the ruler of the man who refused him his
rise ! Ward has been roundly abused by the Tory
press both in Australia and at ’Ome, but he
has been probably the ablest, Treasurer M.L.
has known. His fall is another proof that
“Ye cannot serve State and Mammon.”
Maoriland Parliament has supplied several other
examples to the same effect. Stout’s legal and
trust business in Dunedin went absolutely to pot
when he was a Minister, and it took years after
his political downfall to build it up again. Now
that he is once more into the turmoil of public
life, he has no less than three partners to look after
his business in his absence. Ballance had to give
up his journalistic life in Wanganui, though he
dearly loved it, when he took the Premiership.
Pat Buckley had two partners—but office to
him was always made a holiday affair. On the
other hand, Premier Seddon has no business con-
nections to interfere with politics ; Lands Minister
M'Kenzie is an independent farmer, and his
family looks after his place down South ; Railways
Cadman is pretty free ; Public Works Hall-Jones
is absolutely untied; so also are Carroll, Thomp-
son, and Montgomery, W. P. Reeves, and Hori
Grey ; likewise Russell, Rolleston, and most of
the Tory leaders. As a business spec., politics
spell 9d. in the £.

The latest “ corner ” wheeze. Two well-known touring
N.S.W. actor-managers proceeding home through Hyde
Park, the other night, were bailed up and robbed—one of
a diamond ring, the other of £47 in cash. As it is told :
“Fortunately the thieves did not discover the firms
cheque-book!” That the story is pure invention doesn t
detract from its humor.
The Mecca of “ Jimmy” M‘Mahon’s London pilgrimage
is said to be the Cimeotograph.
The “ furniture and effects ” of a notorious Sydney
house were auctioned one day last week. Various “ lots
consisted of autographed portraits of well-known actors,
but bidding for the mummers was far from brisk—from
6d. to a few “ bob.” Patrons of the sale, however, found
plenty of amusement in comparing notes.
Pro. : Why does the private-life joke of the male mum-
mer always concern the way he dodged his board-bill ?
This is a conundrum that has always vexed me. Is the
actor more dishonest than any other man, and does he
pride himself on the fact ? Surely not.

Beerbohm Tree, writes The Bulletin’s London corre-
spondent, will shortly begin building his new London
theatre on the site of the old Haymarket Opera House.
“ Trilby ” has proved a veritable gold-mine to him and his
backers. The takings even now approach £lOOO a week,
and the profits have only been equalled by those of
“Charley’s Aunt.”
In Gilbert and Sullivan’s new opera, “ The Grand Duke,”
one of the characters, a theatrical manager, tells the
audience that if a man can rule a theatrical company he
can rule anything .
“ Both A and B rehearsal slight;
They say they’ll be ‘ all right at night ’
(They’ve both to go to school yet).
O in each act must change her dress;
D will attempt to ‘ square the press ’;
E won’t play Romeo unless
His grandmother plays Juliet;
F claims all hoydens as her rights
(She’s played them thirty seasons);
And G must show herself in tights
For two convincing' reasons—
Two very well-shaped reasons !
Oh, the man who can drive a theatrical team,
With wheelers and leaders in order supreme,
Can govern and rule, with a wave of his fin,
All Europe—with Ireland thrown in 1 ”
Victoria has several promising young baritones who
may have a future before them, but none of the “ rising”
tenors heard of late seem likely to rise much. The tenor
voice is more scarce everywhere than it used to be, and
this meat-eating country is not specially favorable to its
cultivation, one supposes.
Not equal to Walker’s—the Laird’s “ Scotch.”
A question which is exercising- the habitues of certain
musical warehouses just now has, through the pertinacity
of a certain misanthropic individual, attained consider-
able prominence. The aforesaid misanthrope declares
that Sunny Australia has produced no prominent vocal
male artistes. It is asserted that Newbury and Cunning-
ham are the possessors of good voices—especially the
former —but that, in the opinion of competent musicians,
these gentlemen are not genuine artistes. It is urged
that female vocalists such as Melba, Saville, Crossley, and
Sherwin are artistes, and it is added that Australia is as
far off as ever in evolving a son of the soil who could be
hall-marked “ Artist.” The reason assigned is that this
continent breeds meat-eating creatures whose daily food
quickly exterminates the “ soul-germ ” implanted occa-
sionally by Nature. It is said that Continental centres,
with less meat-eating propensities and opportunities,
have succeeded in giving to the world great singers, and
Italy is instanced as a locale where, with little meat but a
more pronounced vegetarian diet, “ soul ” microbes are
successfully cultivated. It is argued, as regards the
female portion of Australia, that the feminine vanity of
preserving the complexion is responsible for comparative
abstemiousness, combined with medical injunctions which
enjoin little meat-eating in young girls.
The easiest method of building up a theatrical fund has
been repeatedly suggested by the press. A tax of Id. in
the £ upon all salaries exceeding 15s. per week could be
levied by show-managers without any trouble at all, and
a fortnightly or monthly payment of this tax, by post or
otherwise, to the treasurer of the fund would be easy
to arrange. Nor would it be a difficult thing to enforce.
The objection that small managers are frequently dead-
broke doesn’t apply, because the broker pays no salaries,
and is consequently under no obligation to forward tax
moneys. Also, the objection that individual salaries
would be disclosed by this system is all gammon and
spinach. The managerial tax-paper would not need to
furnish any particulars.

and an enormous appetite.

Charles Morton, of the Palace Theatre, is the first
London manager to legislate against the big-hat nuisance.
He published a request that ladies attending his show
would either refrain from head-gear of the “ objection-
able ” class, or put it under the seat during the perform-
ance.

Shifter: “ The Lethbridge-Weir incident, at Melb.
Princess’s, deserves expansion. Mary Weir rehearsed
the Serpentine for the ‘ Forty Thieves,’ and every-
one could see it must be a hit. Alice Lethbridge
interfered, and Williamson said it shouldn’t be done.
Nevertheless, at night, Mary rushed on and did it, and
J.C.W. pretended to be angry. Of course, when the lithe
and wiry one was gone, the substantial cut-and-come-
again woman had her show.”

H. : “ As a tonic to Bellew’s powerful libel on Marat,
the Carlyle horse-leech fancy, read what he really was in
last Encyclopedia Britannica : 1 A courtly kind of
physician, a doctor for ladies and gentlemen, practising
a while in Edinburgh, then becoming attached to the
semi-regal household of the Count d’Artois.’ In short, he
began as an aristocrat, with a passionate affection for the
poor and oppressed, and was turned into a wild beast by
the persecution that drove him to live in the sewers.”
Chevalier Kontski, the Rip-Van-Winklish piano-puncher,
now in Melb., is a man whose soul is in heaven with his
old cronies Palestrina, Bach, Handel, Mozart, and
Beethoven, while his body keeps marching on
here below. He only calls himself a trifle over 80,
knowing the Australian penchant for youth, with its de-
termination of seeing Irving and Patti in the twentieth
century. Nobody can date him, any more than nut-
cracker Sam. Thompson, who has been on Flemington
training-ground, turnip in hand, every dawn since the
year one.

’Ouse is present to lend several pairs of hands.
Hilda Spong, accompanied by her mother, has left for
London.
A dramatic author bemoaning (in the Cornhill Maga-
zine) the failure of the average “ leading man ” to grasp
the playwright’s idea, says : “In that common case, all
you can do is to let him go his own road. Don’t insist on
his coming yours, for then you will get no result what-
ever —no approximation even to anything human. You
will not be able to force him into your characterisation,
and you will have destroyed such individuality as he may
possess of his own. Many a play has been ruined by an
author insisting on the certain, definite reading of a part
which the actor couldn’t give because it didn’t suit his
personality, and he was too little of an artist to change
or adopt it.” Moral, pick your co. whilst writing your
play, if the manager be agreeable. Generally speaking,
the manager—for excellent business reasons—would see
you darned first. In which common case each important
character must be made sufficiently flexible to fit many
actors. The most lucrative plays are those that admit of
successful playing by any competent band of mummers,
at ’Ome or abroad.

hagen. One, disguised as a waiter, attended on her at

hotel. A third got on as a stage-hand. A fourth
measured her for a shoe.

Dear Bulletin,— The Unitarian Church,
Eastern Hill (Melb.) is not a mere shell, and
Gyles Turner is not its only supporter and attend-
ant. The church, thanks to the Rev. A. Hender-
son, is in a more prosperous state than it has been
for many a long day. I could give you the names
of a dozen good men who, with their families, are
constant attendants and good at giving. A fact
not generally known is that many Spiritualists
have, since the collapse of the Sunday evening
gatherings in the Lyceum, gone over to the Uni-
tarian Church—acknowledged on all sides to be
one of the prettiest and most comfortable edifices
in Melbourne. The Unity . Club, which meets
fortnightly, boasts an average attendance of 150.
—M.
The usual girl with a tale of woe to tell
about a dreadful man has been on the loose at
Albury (N.S.W.). In the first place this bright
young child of nature alleged that she had been
“assaulted.” A shearer was arrested on the
charge, and being shown to her at the local gaol,
she promptly and emphatically declared that he
was not the wanted man. She afterwards con-
firmed the statement on oath and he was released.
Then a few hours later she declared that he was
the man, and that she had recognised him at first
sight, but had lied from some wholly unaccount-
able motive. Thereupon the shearer was re-
arrested, and after that the girl swore to him
steadily. Finally, after two months’ gaol, his
trial came and he proved an alibi and was acquit-
ted, and the girl was left still swearing. The
police are now looking for some other man to
convict on her oath, and as she is probably im-
proving in the art of identification, the present
writer wouldn’t take his chance as the next suspect
for a good-sized gold-mine.
“Dobson, Way, and Windeyer,” remarks
an Adelaide lawyer to The Bulletin, “ are
bracketed as eligible candidates for the proposed
Australian vacancy on the law bench of the Privy
Council, but on no sufficiently clear grounds.
Several other Judges fall within the sphere of
eligibility described by the Colonial Office. What,
for instance, except political conviction, stands
against the name of Charles Lilley ? He is inde-
pendent of ‘ salary ’; he was on the bench of Q.
Supreme Court when Windeyer was playing
minor politics, and his conduct on the Bench was
certainly as irreproachable as that of his much-
canvassed compeers. The unceremonious exclu-
sion of Lilley as a possible candidate for
the office raises the question: ‘ Would not
Higinbotham have been shut out on similar
grounds? 5 If this be so, then can an office,
the door of which is slammed in the faces
of Judges like Lilley and Higinbotham, and
which appears to stand invitingly open for persons
like Windeyer and Way, be seriously discussed I v
Australians at large ? ”

A newly-married, laborer out of employ-
nent cut his throat in Melb. the other day. His
bride, who had elected to sleep in the parlor, leav-
ing' him a monopoly of the nuptial cxmch, was
awakened by the milkman’s knock. He didn t
arise and collect the milk, as a loving husband
should, so the lady went to enquire into the pause :
of his backwardness, and lo ! she was a widow. I
She had been a widow once before. *

A correspondent writes :
At Whittlesea (Vic.) the village settlers have to go
round ten miles from the railway-station to reach their
land, whereas if thev were allowed right-of-way through
an adjacent squatter’s land the distance would be only a
little over a mile. When the settlers have exhausted the
Govt, grant (t'4o each) they leave the land partly cleared
and fenced, and the squatter thereafter finds it handy to
run his cattle over. No village settlement can prosper
under the conditions which exist at Whittlesea, ,\et it it
doesn't prosper despite these hopeless drawbacks it will
be held as a proof of the impossibility of village settle
meht in general. The Government seems to be always
working into Fatma-n’s hands.

Bone-grafting extraordinary. A London
doctor, the other day, had occasion to scoop out
all the interior of the heehbone (os calcis) of a
patient, owing to tuberculosis, leaving only a thin
shell. Now, a foot without a heel-bone is like
Hamlet without the Prince. So the resourceful
doctor took the collar-bone of a lamb (part of a
shoulder served as dinner to the doctors ot the
hospital), decalcified it in hydrochloric acid,
ground it into a paste, and filled the yawning
cavity in the bone therewith. In a fortnight the
healing was complete, and the patient has a new
heel quite equal to the natural human article.
This is the eighth time the same doctor has suc-
cessfully used this bone-packing process.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
The death of Professor Loisette, which
was recently reported in some of the American
papers, will not affect the business carried on by
the branch established in Sydney for the Aus-
tralasian provinces for imparting his memory-
culture system. When the Professor was lecturing
here last year he made full provision for
all contingencies, placing in charge of his branch
specially qualified assistants, who, as he certified,
are as capable of imparting his system as he was
himself. The system is such that it will live after
him. The fact that it has been successfully taught
in all parts of the civilised world for a quarter of
a century is a guarantee of its efficacy.*
The New Mutual Insurance.
We now have life insurance, accident insurance;
insurance against fire, theft, and damage. You
can insure your life, your property ; and you can
insure against damage by fire, water or the ele-
ments. But, still more practical and important
than all these, is that you can now insure against
ailments and ills which are prevalent around you.
You can insure against headaches, against pain
after meals, against all symptoms of liver disease;
you can also insure against pain in back, oppres-
sive or difficult breathing, lassitude, nausea, and
loss of flesh —in fact, all the symptoms following
kidney weakness—by a prompt recourse to War-
ner’s Safe Cure. Do not wait until you are so
queer and bad that you will be refused a policy of
life insurance, but whenever the symptoms of the
liver or kidney ailment appear, go to your nearest
medicine vendor, procure a bottle of this medicine,
and try it. Unless your kidneys and liver are
differently made to others, it will have the same
prompt curative effect it has had in thousands of
cases which have been cured by the use of Warner’s
Safe Cure. Surely you do not need a philosopher
to explain a clear, practical point of this kind.
Every day common sense will allow vou to reason
it out carefully for yourself, and if guided by such
a reasoning, you will be as satisfied with the result
as was Mr. Thos. Nash, furrier, Geelong, who
says: “ During a severe attack of liver disease,
accompanied by yellow jaundice, and after follow-
ing medical advice without receiving any benefit,
I was recommended to take Warner’s Safe Cure.
I am happy to say that two bottles completely
cured me, and have had no trouble of the kind
since.”*

A cageful of men had an exciting experi-
ence in the New Victoria United, Ballarat, the
other day. Something had gone wrong, and the
pumps had not been throwing the usual volume,
so that there were seven or eight feet of water in
the shaft “unbeknownst.” Into this cage and
men were lowered, and they might have drowned
had not one cool customer fought his way through
the top, and then hauled his companions up after
him. The last man was half-way to heaven when
the doctors got to work on him.
Among the sheaves of English obituaries
on Parkes the Saturday Review’s brief notice
stands out as about the truest:—
Undoubtedly a man of considerable talents, he rose to
place and power by his gift of vigorous, rugged, and pic-
turesquely ungrammatical oratory ; for a time he was so
idolised by a section of the Antipodean public that he
took himself quite seriously as a heaven-sent statesman.
He regarded Australia as a Greater Britain, and himself
as the greatest of Australians. A colossal egotist, he
believed that what he could not do could be done by no
one else in Australia. The fact that Australia has not
come nearer to Federation than she is now is due in a
large measure to his unbounded jealousy of other men’s
efforts. His inability to pay his debts seems to have con-
vinced his admirers that he was an Australian Pitt.
There is but one error—Parkes was not ungram-
matical.
There was little ceremony about the dis-
missals from N.S.W. Civil Service. For instance,
Mr. Archibald Fraser, Under-secretary for Justice,
after 42 years able and honorable service, obtained
the first intimation of his dismissal from the
Gazette, which said that he would retire from
the service at the end of the month. A day
or two later Minister Gag Gould looked in and
curtly told him that the new Under-secretary was
coming to-morrow, and that he needn’t attend the
office after then —he would be paid up to the end
of the month. And this after 42 years service !
The Bulletin sincerely deplores the exit of Mr.
Fraser from N.S.W. Civil Service —more especially
as he has been succeeded by an official whose mind,
as far as it has been sampled, evidently revolves in
the same circle as the alleged intellect of a poli-
tician who now, by a strange irony, presides over
the N.S.W. Justice department. For Mr. Fraser
was a man conspicuously fitted alike by his in-
stincts and his experience to act as an intermediary
between convicted men and the Minister who
could dispense or withhold mercy. It is under-
stood that Mr. Fraser intends to enter upon the
practice of his profession as a solicitor in Sydney.
The inquiry into the alleged very bad
mismanagement of the electrical and engineering
branches of the Vic. postal department ran up
against the same snag which so constantly met
the Lormer inquiry at its early stages. Hamilton,
M.L.A., propounded a series of ugly charges
against the management. A board was appointed
to investigate, and the Government put up bar-
rister Maxwell to conduct the case for the prose-
cution, and barrister Mackay to act for the defence.
Mackay apparently received his brief in good time,
and, when the inquiry commenced, he was ready
to show there and then that the department was
as pure as snow and as brainy as Solomon. But
the Government only briefed Maxwell the night
before the opening of the inquiry, and he had to
apply for a week’s adjournment while he read up
the case.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
An Australian abroad to The Bulletin by last
mail: —“ Every person in England who has been in Aus-
tralia, no matter for how short a time, writes to Manager
Musgrove for a season-ticket to admit to all matches, and
on previous occasions has had them. Musgrove, however,
objects to this wholesale free-pass system, and there is
much complaining.
“ Clem Hill’s faultless 73 against Essex, the
second match of the tour, deserves much praise. The
Australian wickets were going down like ninepins, and he
stopped a rot which might have meant defeat, and thus
affected the whole tour.
“The Australian cricketers travel round Eng-
land in two first-class carriages. The Midland Railway
Co. has contracted for the whole tour.
“ The wealth-line is strongly drawn on English
cricket-grounds. The general admission to see a match
against the Australians is Is., the grand stands are ss.
and 10s. 6d.
“Manager Musgrove was the only notable in
frock coat and top hat at the Sheffield Park match. Eight
suits and straw hats were the rule, but a number, includ-
ing “ Tummy,” wore the ordinary “ bowler.”
“ The Australians were determined not to lose
their first match, and played for a draw failing a win.
This made the game very slow, for everything high to the
off was allowed to pass, and, the ground being hard, ball
after ball was never struck at.
“ The Australians can go into any theatre in
England on presenting their cards. They used to take
their friends in with them, but Musgrove shut down on
this in his own sweet way.
“ George Bull (late of Sydney) travels with the
cricketers on behalf of the London Daily Mail, the
Harmsworths’ new venture. He has not married and
says he never intended to marry Madge Rossell-—who
dances as happily as ever in ‘ The Shop Girl ’ at the Gaiety.
“Thecricketers, when playing at Sheffield Park,
have the pick of the best rooms in the Hotel Metropole,
the finest hotel in Brighton. They dine privately on the
choicest of viands and drink only the best brands of fizz.
And the noble Earl stands the whole racket. It is said
that on this occasion his Lordship felt hurt that the wine-
bill was so small.
“ Last season Little Johnny Briggs took a team
up to Liverpool to play a match against 18 of Liverpool
and district. Among his eleven was Crossland, the Lan-
cashire quick bowler, who was disqualified for the county
for throwing. Bowling one of his swiftest it hit the
opposing batsman a severe blow on the leg, and the man
walked away from the wicket as Crossland appealed un-
successfully for leg-before. ‘ Not out! Not out! ’ they
shouted after the retreating man. But he turned round
and remarked, ‘ Noa —but Om domned well going.’
“ Gregory’s 154 against the XI. of England at
the Crystal Palace was a fine exhibition of all-round
batting ; and for the first time since he came to England
he showed his true form.
“ The London press are not in love with Eady’s
stj'le, remarking that he has not shown himself a bats-
man, neither does he shape like one. It is funny to see
little Gregory put one to the boundary without apparent
effort, while the Tasmanian giant stands up, opens his
shoulders, and cannot get the ball half the distance.
“ Giffen’s 7 for 15 in the match against De
Trafford’s English XI. was a triumph for the South Aus-
tralian, and he made the most of it. When the innings
was all over he stood near the wickets deep in conversa-
tion until every other player was in the pavilion. He
then made his entry alone amid the cheers of the crowd
which had assembled to see him pass. It was a well-con-
sidered theatrical effect.
“ Jones’s performance in the first innings against
the English XI., at the Crystal Palace, should not be lost
sight of in the light of Giffen’s more sensational effort in
their second innings. Eight wickets for 39 runs is not an
everyday average.”
‘ Trumble,” writes another Bulletin corres-
pondent from England, “proved the folly of his exclusion
from the Sheffield Park match by taking 10 Essex wickets
for 108 runs. M'Kibbin’s solitary scalp has cost 105 runs,
and up to now he and Eady are the failures of the team.
The Tasmanian fields like an over-grown, loose-boned colt
with a draught-horse’s shoes on, and bats in the style of a
Yarrabakbloks ‘Yob.’ His hands are feet, and his feet
over-loaded mud-barges. It may be ‘ big-man’s ’ nervous-
ness, but it is none the less disastrous and distressing.
/‘A wet or fiery wicket is necessary to supply
sting to the bowling of Kortright, who gathers up steam
in a 70-feet preliminary run. Mead—a clever bowler of
the Peel type—should be the more successful bowler.
“ Hill has batted like a wary seasoned veteran
in the three matches played—32, 23, 73, and 41, are his
steadily-earned scores. The Adelaide boy can hit two
‘ fourers ’ in succession, and then play the remainder of
the over as if he were batting for a ‘ not out,’ and that is
the secret of century-scoring. An old, steady head on
young, lively shoulders.
“ De Trafford’s team ‘ turned it up ’ as soon as
their slogging captain went for 2 (a ‘ blob’ in the second)
and never made an attempt to stiffen it down again.
Giffen, who had been having bad luck, clean bowled five
of them, and got 7 for 15. It looked as simple as smash-
ing eggs with an axe. The English papers have since dis-
covered the England XI. was ‘no class.’ They are also
questioning Jones’s delivery, and complaining that
Gregory hits too hard, and Hill inconsiderately puts the
ball to ‘ leg ’ when all the fieldsmen are on the ‘ off.’
“ At Crystal Palace a successful member of the
team gave a striking illustration of the answer to a vulgar
old conundrum (‘ What is it the poor man throws away
and the rich man puts in his pocket ?’) He showed the
poor man’s independence and made his fellow-country-
men’s stomachs squirm. King Kruger thus acts adroitly,
and he stiffened Chamberlain ; then why not King
who stiffened Grace ?
“The opportunities afforded A. Trott and
Harry (both employed by the M.C.C.) to show their
calibre have not disclosed any special performance. The
latter v. Leicester scored 8 and 26 (thrown out), and
Albert has secured 4 wickets in each of several innings for
about 25 runs per wicket.
“ Trotty (’Joe’) was not at ease until Thoms
and West ‘ passed ’ Jones’s delivery at Sheffield Park. The
delivery of nearly all the fast bowlers here is doubtful,
more or less.
* * * *
All the Australian cricketers are “Mistered”
by London Times. In the Sheffield Park match all the
Englishmen, save Jackson and Fry, were excepted from
the honor—even Prince Ran., &c., having to be satisfied
with mere initials.
Mofussil : “ Ranjitsinghi, the cricketer, is a
pupil of Chester Macnaghten, a remarkable man, who
died in India a few weeks ago. In 1870 Gov. Lord Mayo
appointed him chief of the Kathiawar College, where he
remained ever since. This has exercised incalculable in-
fluence in the education of native aristocrats. Macnaghten
was a veritable nurse of rulers, for a hundred of the most
influential rajahs of India at present were moulded by
him.”
» * * *
All England, in their second innings at Lord’s,
scored 111 for the loss of four men, on a “ heavy ” wicket,
between showers. If the Australians had scored their
first innings of 53 under the same conditions, their press
barrackers would have assumed that the wicket was “ab-
solutely unplayable.”
That same innings of 53 in the test-match ought
to be accepted as “a smack in the eye” by every rabid
patriot who cried “ Unplayable wicket! ” when the M.C.C.
got rid of Our Boys for a paltry 18. Nobody save the
patriots could understand how the wicket came to be so
particularly bad during one particular hour of the day
when the Australians were batting on it. The M.C.C.
went in first and made a decent score. Then the Austra-
lians got out for 18, after which they followed on and
“ found the wicket improving”—inasmuch as they didn’t
all go down like ninepins this time.
Excessive caution, aggravated by nervousness,
was what the Australians suffered from in the first innings
of their first two matches at Lord’s. On each occasion
they saw their mistake, and played a bolder game in the
second innings, with vastly better results. In England
nowadays the slow and careful style of play doesn’t seem
to answer, unless, of course, the bowling is so much
weaker than the batting that it admits of any sort of
treatment. The county teams score rapidly, or get out
soon, and the same rule has generally applied to the Aus-
tralians in matches where they didn’t stand over their
opponents. Against a South of England eleven, for
example, they had scored 240 for three wickets at 5
o’clock in the afternoon, thanks to Giffen and Gregory,
who contributed over 100 apiece by fairly vigorous hitting.
“ Having done so well,” says the Morning Post report,
“it might reasonably have been expected they would
attempt to force the game. Strangely enough, however,
they adopted very cautious methods, and met with such
little success that in the last 45 minutes only 18 were
added and three wickets fell.” It is this confounded
caution that stops so many batsmen, at the last minute,
from getting the “coveted century.” In last week’s test-
match Abel began to “ play with great care as he neared
the hundred” and came to grief accordingly. Served
him right. The bolder game had been a good friend to
him, yet he meanly went back on it.
* * * *
When news of the Australians’ 53 in the test
match reached Kalgoorlie, the miners clubbed together
and cabled to Harry Musgrove—“For the honor of Aus-
tralia, knock off and bring them home.—Australia.”
* ¥ * *
At the Y.R.C. “Grand National” meeting,
which opens next Saturday, the Millers and the Manifolds
will, more likely than not, keep the big prizes from slip-
ping into less decent hands. Dungan is favorite for the
G.N. Hurdle Race at time of writing, and the brilliant
steeplechaser, who can’t very well come to grief at a
hurdle,' is fast enough to beat nearly all of them, even
with the steadier of list. 71b. on his back. That the
Messrs. Miller mean business is certain. Redleap was
entered more for the purpose of keeping the weights
down, than with the hope of seeing the old ’un win
again. Artist, who has been expected to prove the
stable’s champion on the day, didn’t get very kind treat-
ment from the handicapper (list. 91b.); nevertheless,
this dark horse will carry The Bulletin’s five shillings
should the owners recommend his chance, in which case
the betting-market will give punters a good straight tip.
If Artist wins his trial he ought to go out at a short price
and win the race, bar accidents.
One feature of the weights for the big Cups is
the treatment of the rising three-year-olds. Handicappers
are attaining a higher opinion of what youngsters are
capable of doing over distances as compared with pre-
vious years, and juveniles seem to be improving. Penal-
ised Waterfall’s fourth last .year, behind a three-year-old
filly, tends to very much confirm the view. All the same,
Newhaven’s 8"2 in Caulfield Cup, and 713 in Flemington,
do not look very alluring even if he is a stayer, which the
writer doubts.
* * * *
Wallace’s lOst. in Melb. Cup ought to effectually
stop him, if something else doesn’t. It is quite likely that
one of the stable three-year-olds will be the St. Alban’s
pick on the day.
*"* * *
If he can stay, Vanitas is a likely C. Cup candi-
date. He is bred that way, and is a particularly smart
sprinter.
* * * *
Now current talk that one Sydney select crowd
who have been waiting with pleasurable patience for a
big cup, got a stiff knock-out when they saw the weights.
People who desire to be thought clever, fall badly when
a handicapper sets ’em down at their own value, so to
speak.
* * * *
The Ascot Gate-Money Racing Club, Melb.,
gives £2OO for a day’s racing in July, £3OO for one in
October, and £5OO for one in Nov., including the Ascot
Gold Cup, £250. Obviously the club skrimps up to rival
Caulfield. The cards are shuffling.
* * * *
If Moorefield Handicap run was The Captain’s
true form, he isn’t the best to have hanging round a
stable. Favorite, and carrying only 7.10, he finished like
a non-stayer. The Newcastle people got in on city punters
with Helmsman, at a cost to the latter of much hard cash
and many inverted commas.
* * * *
The inferior 13 saddled for Moorefield juvenile
race gave one an idea of what a young giant could do
among ’em. “ Consistency ”is a lost term, “ turn-about ”
seeming to be the thing. And when one doesn’t win
when it’s his day out, it’s simply due to bad luck.
* * * *
The expected return of Broken Hill to his Aus.
Cup form in the Spring is causing the laying of live wagers
for Melb. two miles. His late running doesn’t altogether
warrant the bookified assumption. Broken Hill —broken
still.
* * * *
Strong Adelaide tips concerning local horses for
the Cups, Derby, handicaps—everything—are now in cir-
culation. Auraria showed the winning way last year, and
the holy city intends preparing for a big spring horse
boom this year.
* ♦ * *
Saturday’s racing-coincidences :—At Moorefield
(N.S.W.) and Williamstown (Vic.), four double-named
horses won races ; two pulled off doubles, one race of each
double being the same, distance done in the same time ;
in each of the others exactly 11 horses ran, the favorites
in both starting at 5 to 2.
» * » »
More people attended the S.A.J.O.’s last one-
day meeting and more money went through the “tote”
than has ever been the case before. The six winning horses
were all well-backed at an average price of 10 to 1. Sig-
nificant !
**; * *
A little-thought-of tote-advantage is the fact
that should no money be on the winner of a race all
backers get back their cash minus 10 percent. Thus Man
of Honor won Mentone (Vic.) Flying Handicap, last week,
unbacked with the machine operating. The man who had
£5O on Sinking Fund, the second horse, would have got
£45 instead of leaving it in Mo’s bag.
aft * * ♦
Davies’s horse, The Gondolier, sire of G.N.
mare Emmalea, and the oldest standing stallion in Vic-
toria, is rising 30 years.
» * * *
Two leading Australian racing clubs will shortly
decide whether jumping races will be vetoed during De-
cember, January, and February. About time something
was done.
* * » *
Under cross-examination a Victorian judge and
steward has admitted having had a bet on the third horse,
which eventually got the money, the winner being wiped
out, and the second disqualified. “ Follow the stewards ! ”
* * • •
Saturday wheel-sandwiched football-spells have
enabled bikist Meadham to show improving form. Two
wins since the innovation, and a heat record—l‘3 3-sths
for half-a-mile—last week, is up to top style. He would
have annexed the final but for the old, familiar “ picket-
ing.”
* * • •
Ex-viceregal private - secretary Wallington is
now about again after his recent cycling accident—but
with his arm in a sling. On the glad tidings being noised
abroad, a special meeting of the—holdfast!—Collingwood
Cycling Club was called, and a congratulatory resolution
passed and forwarded to Guvment’ouse on pink paper.
How are the mighty fallen ! Even good old Collingwood,
the historic home of “ the push,” infused with the snob-
bery of effete civilisation !
* * * *
Adelaide tried mounting its telegraph mes-
sengers on bikes, and now the postal department runs a
hospital for crippled bikes, and there isn’t an uninjured
bo3' in the service. The authorities are considering
traction engines as a substitute for bikes.
» * * *
It is averred that a new cycle with solid rubber
tyre and pneumatically-hung frame is going to knock
right out the expensive and comparatively-dangerous
machine of The retail price will be under £l2.
As Rugby progresses, improving weekly play
doesn’t seem to attract the Sydney public to the extent
expected. Saturday’s semi-final crowds were only fair to
ordinary. Randwicks walked over ’Varsity by 21 to 0,
but it was an exciting match, the points giving faint idea
of the rate of notching. Only three were secured in the
first half, after which the U’s were taken off their feet,
and couldn’t last the distance. They started too well, but
the premiers showed superior combination The Rand-
wick’s can’t well lose the final—unless the opposing backs
defend their line with bull-dogs and shot-guns.
Wallaroo and Rand wick run in and out in race-
track fashion. Saturday gave Wallaroo an Bto 3 supre-
macy. It was a mostly forward struggle, the heavies in
front being made to earn their tea. Consequently, the
rear division got work as casual laborers, the “feeding”
being scanty. There isn’t much difference between the
two teams : it all depends how the game is played.
* * * *
The Vic. Eootball Ass. attempted last Saturday
to revive interest in the game it has stifled by changing
the boundary-rule and declaring the ball in play after
leaving the umpire’s hands. This doubtful improvement
failed to raise any popular enthusiasm. The Association
will probably put its only other idea into execution, and
count behinds as well as goals. Anything rather than try
cleansing the big clubs of cronkness.
Leading Vic. football clubs still maintain their
relative positions behind Essendon, which went through
the hardening process for the premiership by defeating
Williamstown, 7 goals to 3 goals, on Saturday. Colling-
wood downed unlucky Geelong, and South beat Melb.,
whoimmediately left for their Tas. trip, which should effec-
tually burst them up. Of the middle division, St. Kilda,
though unfortunate, look promising, their Footscray vic-
tory placing them above Melb. and next to Fitzroy. As
predicted, Essendon, Collingwood, and South Melb. seem
certain to fill the premiership places this season.
* * ♦ *
The existence of the Vic. game of football de-
pends upon the local schools and colleges. Whatever
game the boy is used to, the man will play. The Vic.
game is rapidly losing ground amongst the schools, while
Rugby promises to be shortly adopted by many. When
the schools play Rugby the other game will be a thing of
the past.
* * * *
J. Greddon, a typical athlete, put up a sft. 7in.
standing high jump at aQ. sports fixture lately. He was
handicapped to stand, his opponents running. Which is
something new in the way of handicapping. Greddon’s
jump, if true, is a very tall performance.
* * * *
The Scientific American is booming a bicycle
built of wood by a Philadelphia lad who couldn’t afford
the steel article. Everything is wood but the driving-band,
which is a leather strap. The machine is sold for a few
shillings, and the 14-year-old inventor has made a fortune.
* * * *
Pugilist “Pluto,” like his namesake, appears to
have some knowledge of the wealth hidden in the bowels
of the earth. His Westralian experiences have brought
him back to Melb. possessed of com. He now drives the
nattiest of turnouts, has a nice residence at Middle Park,
is married to a pretty white girl, takes music-lessons from
a well-known professor, and has dropped prize-ring associ-
ations with a heavy thud.
* * * *
The coming Melbourne boom— the Harriers. So
far, the game is Respectable.
* * * *
Some few seasons back .all the Melb. senioi
football clubs ran a second 20 ; now it takes them all then
time to pay the first 20.
* * * *
Adelaide football clubs, in the endeavor to win
back the public support fast waning, have abolished
“ little marks,” the stupid system which resulted in the
incessant scrimmages and rough play. But it seems to be
too late to save the “ glorious Australian game.”
'** * *
Vic. league of wheelmen are “ coming it hot ”
on the color question. Fourteen singlet wearers in one
recent scratch race have been fined £1 each.
* ♦ * *
A fortune awaits the woman who shows great
form at billiards.
* * * *
An Auckland man offers to bet <£so he can pro-
duce an amateur who can beat the locally-made world’s
record of 60sec. for a quarter-mile over hurdles.
* * * *
Dick Hunt, old-time pugilist, hero of over 50
bare-knuckle battles, and at one period doorkeeper at
the old Vic. Theatre, Sydney, died last week.
* ♦ * *
Related of a certain bibulous suburban trainer
that, after returning from a net-fishing spree, he stayed
up all night trying to train two shrimps to dance a horn
pipe!
* * * *
When some owners experience a bad season or
two they generally change their trainer. A few even
change unlucky neddies’ names. Others change their
jocks. They would show more wisdom if they changed
their horses.
* * * *
Current motto : “He who bets, and runs away,
lives to welsh some other day.”
* * * «
“ Roaring” in horses is now often prevented by
the operation of tracheotomy and insertion of a tracheo-
tomy tube. In the Selling Race at the second Jan.
meeting at Manchester, Eng., the horses that finished
first, second, and fourth all had silver tubes in their wind-
pipes.
* * * *
Yet another case recorded of an owner making a
present of a prad to his trainer ! Gifts of this sort are, in
most cases, the trainers’ property all the time.

bottle this small quantity to take during the day, and to-
night I will be as right as pie when I have again to sing.

and coughs, and I have used nothing else for years.”

Sundry casual sports used to wonder where a
certain sepulchral-looking schoolmaster got his good in-
formation. Also, the parents of a young Ikey Mo couldn’t
understand why “ friends ” gave the boy so much silver.
The double mystery has been fathomed. Young Hooknose
was the master’s favorite class-boy, and, Hebrew-like, im-
parted all the breakfast and tea-table horsey chatter for a
small consideration.
» * * *
“Scotty the Wr inkier ” : —Not only in sport,
but in work, pacing is necessary to obtain records : e.g. in
post-hole sinking, trenching, lock-spitting for surveyors,
and notably in shearing. When M‘lnnes did the
Brookong tally he had a mate in the pen who took the
cobblers. I was not present at Howe’s record, but I know
that the pacing, running or racing done against Mick
Granlow at Conbogalong, on the Castlereagh, was a
matter of trying to kill him, instead of which he put up a
record, over 200, and not bad for a second year’s shearing.
He was only 18 years old, and the work was equal to a
score of mile sprints on a bike. I think one Provan has
the rabbit-skinning medal, something like 80 in half-an-
hour, though the country reeks with rabbiters who will
tell you they can beat that. To-day another record-
sinasher claims two acres ploughing in a day in April,
nine-inch furrow, five-inches deep, single furrow plough.
This makes him walk 21 miles, clean his mould board, lose
time at turns, and feed his horses, all between 7 a.m. and
5 p.m. He is a fair bishop among ploughmen, and was
probably a fisherman when he learned to estimate.
* * » *
Yankee files predict extensive use of one-
wheeled sulkies during coming trotting season. The
latest pneumatic sulky has a high seat, attached by steel
rods to shafts which don’t wobble, but help the horse to
keep an even, well-balanced gait. The horse’s hoofs keep
clear of the sulky, which seems as near perfection as
possible.
• * • •
The Salvation Army has adopted the bike.
“ We claim the cycle for God,” says the English War Cry,
chiefly, it appears, because it enables many an “ officer”
to put in Saturday night with his wife and still be at
“ knee-drill ”10 miles away next morning. This explana-
tion was necessary, or it might have been thought that
the bike was approved of because it enabled many an
“officer” to slither from his wife and spend Saturday
evening with his “best girl.”
* * * *
How many good horses are lost through that in-
sane belief that a broken leg cannot be repaired? Drs.
Hosking and Douglas, of Masterton, M.L., the other day
set a fractured leg in a plaster splint with excellent
results.
* * * *
The old and the new. How seldom the name of
“ J. B. Clark” now appears as a winning owner, or even
as nominator for future classics !
* * * *
The owners of a rather smart Sydney horse have
been waiting so long to make a haul, that they are now
almost frightened to win. *
Next tank-diver ? There’s always room at the
top of the staging, whatever there may be at the bottom
of the tank.
* * * *
A Northern paper : “ Where are the turf gems
of bygone days ? ” So far as The Bulletin is aware, they
are either in pawn or still glittering flashly on the fingers
and shirt-front of Ikey Mo.
* * » *
One Melb. pony-club is giving £5OO in stakes for
a one-day meeting.

Told of one generous pony-owner that he lately
paid a losing-mount jock, half-a-plug of tobacco and six-
pence to get his hair cut.
A FOOTBALL-PLAYER.
If I could paint you, friend, as you stand there,
Guard of the goal, defensive, open-eyed,
V/atching the tortured bladder slide and glide
Under the twinkling feet; arms bare, head bare,
The breeze a-tremble through crow-tufts of hair ;
Bed-browed in face, and ruddier, having spied
A wily foeman breaking from the side,
Aware of him—of all else unaware.
If I could limn you, as you leap and fling
Your weight against his passage, like a wall;
Clutch him and collar him, and rudely cling
For one brief moment till he falls —you fall;
My sketch would have what Art can never give—
Sinew and breath and body ; it would live.
EDWARD CRACROFT LEFROY.

INVALIDS
AND
THE AGED.
In Darlinghurst prisoners are allowed to
make pets of rats, with which it is said the gaol is
overrun.
Dear Bulletin, —Has the wetting of the
coal anything to do with the combustion of recent
Newcastle (N.S.W.) cargoes? Shipping linseed
and wheat loose was knocked off at Riga (Baltic)
in ’69 or ’7O on account of cargoes taking fire.
I was on board a brig, the Annie, of Riga, loaded
with linseed, loose, and her cargo took fire on
account of part of the linseed having been wet.
As luck happened, we were forced to put back to
the Bolderaa on account of ice in the Bay of Riga,
and were therefore in port when the fire was dis-
covered. And, later on, when employed at New-
castle, I noted that the small coal-heaps at the
Waratah Co’s old tunnel, and the coal-heaps at
the A.A. pit, all took fire from the ins ide. — Walter
Goodwin.
Melbourne Age and Argus had never a
nasty remark between them to pass upon the inci-
dent of Lawyer Geake’s absconding from his bail.
A very few days before the vanished swindler was
wanted at the Criminal Court the merits of his
case were argued before the Full Court. Where
was Geake at that time ? Didn’t he meet any of
the lawyers or barristers who appeared for him on
appeal? If not, didn’t they know that he had
bolted ? And if he was about when his conviction
was upheld by the Full Court, how came it that a
duly sentenced criminal could escape from justice
without the police troubling themselves in the
matter ? Geake, from that moment, should have
been shadowed by the myrmidons of the law, and
presuming they couldn’t then see him they ought
to have started looking for him right away. Why
thisprofound newspaper silence concerning Geake’s
escape under such unpardonable circumstances?
and is the Victorian Law a dog that it should be
kicked aside by the first attorney whose leg it has
chanced to grip ?
Now, sing' a song of lawyer Geake,
Whose crafty tongue is in his cheek ;
They “ wanted ” him the other week—
He stole what wasn’t his’n.
His Honor called the truant’s name,
The grinning crier did the same,
But lawyer Geake he never came
To put himself in prison.
Good Geake was held in high regard
Until, when proved a knavish card,
He got two years, with labor hard —
At least that was the sentence.
Meantime he wandered forth “ on bail ”
And went to Jainberoo by rail,
Or, possibly, preferred to sail
To Hingland for repentance.
He “ cut his stick,” which wasn’t odd;
For, though a common thieving clod
Must always take a trip to qi od,
The sinfullest attorney
Is different from one of such :
Conviction doesn’t hurt him much,
He breaks from Nemesis’s clutch
And makes a nicer journey.
So, sing a song, in joyful strain,
Of justice treated with disdain ;
The law may seek its Geake in ' ain
He got a chance of clearing.
Whilst judges grave confirmed h s cass
He went to find the missing mac. ,
Observing, “ Now’s the time and spacs
For easy disappearing.”

Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir,—ln the year of 1871 I was eight days,
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness in the joints, and have tried!
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney-
I had a great difficulty in walking, and went to.
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.
W. O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same coinplaint as
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On
Thursday before Good .Friday I took the first .of
your Pills; since then I have used 4 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.

Dear Bulletin, —It is not correct that
N.S.Wi State school headmasters have heretofore
been paid according to the certificate held. Many
l ’2A ’’'teachers in charge of seventh-class schools
receive A 2171 a year, while scores of men holding
“2B ” Certific ites (a grade lower) are in charge of
sixth-class schools at =£2os a year. The fact is,
that teachers have heretofore been paid according
to the class of school held. Each class of school
had a certain salary attached to it, and the teacher
in charge got that salary no matter what certificate
he held.— Cave.
Concerning N.S.W. Govt. Astronomer
Russell’s 19-year cycle theory in explanation of
droughts, three of which he can make out and
trace more or less distinctly, the inland squatter
has long since cut the years up into sixes, with an
occasional seven thrown in—which fits in with the
scientist’s plans. On this theory a good many
tree squatters almost cleared their holdings two
years ago, and began stocking again early this
year. Except in the far west of N.S.W. (where
there is not in scores of holdings enough grass to
muster on for shearing) these buyers can already
see about 100 per cent, on their purchases.
A man can live without fire, without books,
and, if he be a good bushman, without cooks ; but
no man can live without a good knife. So it is
not surprising that the name of Mr. W. Jno.
Baker, cutler, 3 Hunter-street, Sydney, is known
far and wide, and that his unequalled stock brings
testimonials and recognitions from all parts of
Australia. He has the knowledge that can be
/ained only at the great Sheffield manufactories,
and, besides, he has the long Australian experience
which makes him right up to date in supplying
cutlery to every section of the community. He
has three registered designs, and one patented
design of his own; he has farmers’, boundary-
riders’ knives ; veterinary knives and instruments
in new and special form; butchers’, cooks’, sports-
mans’, hunters’ knives ; sawbread knives, carving
knives and forks ; table cutlery ; and the grandest
Usplay of pocket-knives in Sydney. He has a
sj lend id stock of razors ; beautiful strops, made of
Yustralian leather by Australian workmen ; and
an endless variety of scissors. Any man or woman
>n need of cutlery should call or write for price-list
and illustrated catalogue. Mr. W. Jno. Baker,
in order to encourage country and intercolonial
trade, does not charge for postage on cutlery to
any address in Australia, New Zealand, and
Tasmania. *

ABRIDGED PROSPECTUS.
TIIHIS company is projected for the purpose of acquiring from the Perth Brick Syndicate Limited, as
-L a going concern their brick-making business on the Canning River, Perth, West Australia, includ-
ing about 26 acres of freehold land, plant, &c., and for the purpose of purchasing and erecting the
most approved brick-making machinery in order to extend the present business.
The property has been thoroughly examined by Mr. G. C. Inskip, F.R.1.8.A., the well-known
Melbourne architect, who was sent to Perth especially for the purpose. Copy of his report can be
seen at the offices of the brokers.
The freehold, consisting of about 26 acres of land, contains a valuable clay deposit most suitable
for the manufacture of bricks of good quality. It is situate on the Canning River, about 10 miles
from Perth, and a similar distance from Fremantle.
Special attention is called to the following matters : ,
1. The building trade in West Australia is very brisk, and there is every indication that it will
continue so for some years.
2. The demand for bricks of good quality is greater than can at present be locally supplied.
3. Bricks are now being imported from the eastern colonies at a cost of 30s. per 1000 for carriage,
to which has to be added losses for breakage in handling.
3. This company will be the first in Perth to possess suitable machinery for the manufacture of
bricks on a large scale, and it will take over a sound business as a going concern, including the
very large existing contracts.
It will probably hereafter be deemed advisable to extend the operations of the company to other
centres, such as Coolgardie, Kalgoorlie, Menzies, etc.
Melbourne, June 12, 1896.
