TO MINING COMPANIES, BATTERY OWNERS
4 AND OTHERS.
Increase your yields by Saving the Fine Gold
and Pyrites by the use of
Wynne and Tregurtha’s
Patent Battery Plates,
This Invention, lately patented, revolutionises the ordi-
nary battery treatment of ores, for by its use copper-
plates, quicksilver, and chemicals are entirely dispensed
with. The Wynne and Tregurtha Patent Battery Plates
are made of iron, cast with peculiarly-shaped ribs. They
are fitted in a wooden frame, and take the place of the
ordinary copper-plates and blanket tables. The tailings
on leaving the stamper boxes pass over the plates which,
from their construction, catch the fine gold and pyrites,
allowing the sand and water to flow away. When the
plates are fully charged with the concentrates they are
then tilted backwards by means of a lever, throwing the
contents into a trough underneath. In practical use
much better results have been obtain'd by these plates
than by' the ordinary copper ones, the yield of gold being
increased by fully 10 per cent. Excellent results have
been achieved in the treatment of refractory ores, where
the gold is associated with some foreign mineral, whiph
has proved deleterious to the mercury and causing the
loss of the gold. The plates are strong, simple in cqn
struction, and easily handled, and can be obtained from

THE ALEXANDRA DEEP LEADS COM-
PANY is projected for the purpose of
acquiring all the right, title, and interest of in and
to applications for Four Mining Leases, compris-
ing an aggregate area of 169 acres, known as the
U7T. Flat, situate on theU. T. Creek, Alexandra,
and for which Leases are now being issued ; and
also to provide capital for developing And working
the alluvial ground contained therein.
These Leases are at the foot of the gullies con-
taining the well-known and rich mining claims
known as the Homeward Bound and Lucky Leads,
from which enormous yields of gold have been
obtained. The said Leads run directly into the
Flat proposed to be acquired and worked by this
Company, and are distant about one hundred
yards from the boundary pegs of the Leases.
The U. T. Flat has been tested by boring, and
by sinking a trial shaft to a depth of 130 feet, when
bottom was reached, the result of which wiL be
seen in the statements of Mr. W. Hartley and Mr.
W. Hewett, subjoined. Subsequently, a full-sized
.haft was sunk to a depth of 128 feet, and timbered
'.lie whole distance, but had to be abandoned m
xmsequence of the Tangye pumps then used prov-
ing unsuitable for the work by becoming choked
with the sand and graved encountered when getting
into the wash, and the capital being utterly insuf-
ficient to persevere with the work. This shaft is
on the land comprised in the present Leases, and
may prove of great value hereafter, when drained,
for air or timber purposes.
The following, taken from the official pamphlet,
entitled “ The Neglected Goldfields of Victoria,”
and the Alexandra Times, published in 1875,
shows the gold obtained, and dividends paid, by
some of the mines on these leads :
Mine. Gold obtained. Pa^ n “ s divi ‘
Lucky No. 2 ... 27,0000z5. <£47,500

(1873-4) ... 7,0000z5. .£25,000
Mysterious ... 3,4150z5.
Lucky No. 4 ... 1,9160z5. .£6,000
Lucky No. 5 ... 4,1000z5. <£lo,ooo
(From 1000 tons.)
The sinking is easy, and can be let on contract
for from 20s. to 30s. per foot to a depth of 150 feet.
Sawn timber can be obtained at 10s. per 100 feet
delivered.
It is proposed to obtain much more suitable and
powerful machinery than was used before, and to
procure tho advice and assistance of thoroughly
qualified experts m selecting machinery and sites
tor commencing operations.
The Reports of Capt. W. J. Begelhole, Jewell-
s' 10 ''' 11 mining expert; Mr. Samuel K. Vickery
Surveyor-General of Victoria; and Messrs!
Haitley and Hewett,. prove conclusively the bona-
fide nature of the undertaking, with very reason-
able prospects of yielding handsome returns upon
the capital invested, and the Provisional Directors
have every confidence in recommending it to the
attention of the investing public.
Over 8000 shares have been subscribed for in
Alexandra, and 8000 are reserved for Walhalla
and the balance will be allotted strictly according
to priority of application. 6
Applications for shares may be made to the
Brokers,, or to the Manager pro. tern., from whom
any further information may be obtained.
Report by Capt. W. J. Begelhole.
In accordance with your instructions I have
made a careful inspection of the Alexandra Deep
.Leads Company s leases, consisting of IG9 acres
situated on the U.T. Creek, Alexandra.
T consider the property well worth developing.
I am quite prepared to join, and will advise my
friends to do likewise, in providing funds for that
purpose.
There is no doubt in my mind-as to the exist-
ence of a deep alluvial lead in that portion of the
valley secured by you.
Taking into consideration the large amount of
gold won from the leads in the various gullies, and
that the trend of all these leads is in the direction
of the land secured by you, I am of opinion that
when the main lead, which, must run through your
property , is properly opened up, it will prove to be
ot a highly payable character, and will handsomely
repay all wno take part in the venture.
The reefs in the adjacent rising ground, which
' lav ® yielded such large returns in the past, also
trend directly into your leases, and the general
indications lead me to form the conclusion that
the several lines of reef will junction at a depth in
your property.
I should estimate that about J 23000, judiciously
expended, will prove the property successfully.
I am, Gentlemen, yours faithfully,
W. J. BEGKLHOLE.
Mulbournk, 6th June, 1896.

Dear Bulletin, —Why does the press
always print it “ swagsman ” ? Neither in bush,
nor in town, have I ever heard a man, in ordinary
talk, say “swagsman.” It was always, whenever
that intended word teas used (which is seldom
now), “ swagman ”or “ swaggy.” Who invented
“ swagsman ”? And why ?—Dipso.
Dear Bulletin, — Woman rarely cuts her
throat; neither does she shoot herself, and
very rarely hangs herself. Can any of your
readers say why ?—or why, also — re the woman
who threatened to suicide because her husband
remained undisturbed by the fleas which were de-
vouring ha —do women fuss so much more than
men about fleas ? Is it more accessible garments,
a thinner skin, a livelier imagination or, do
women taste better than men ? —“ A.”

A Westralian outlines for The Bulletin
his experiences of Japanese Bridgets :
A few experiences of Jap. house servants under assign-
ment will be interesting to the uninitiated. When the
assigned boy arrives you find he cannot speak much Eng-
lish-knows very little about cooking—is very polite and
willing and exceedingly handy—provided you have the
patience to act as instructor After three months you
find a boy who is all cunning, can speak enough English
to make himself understood, and has been well drilled by
other experienced house boys. Then the trouble begins
boy becomes sulky, will kick your feet at every oppor-
tunity, throw slops out of the window, burn the clothes
when ironing, sing all day long in a painful Jap. mono-
tone, and when reprimanded tell you it’s nice singing.
He will leave the kitchen dirty and do every possible
thing to annoy you. Should his friends arrive he will
“shout” for them at your expense, should liquor be
handv, and calmly tell you he has done so. You patiently
bear'it all—for he is a two-year assigned servant—until
you at last ask him what he means by it all. Then, in
broken English, he will explain : “Me not get enough
wages—only 10s. per week—other boy get 25s.—me got
no clothes —me want you lend me £5 ” —. You
distinctly decline-and off to bed goes your boy. No
more work for you till you accede —he s sick. One
lady imprisoned her boy for refusing duty She
called upon me after the trouble, and I ordered
my boy to bring two cups of tea As the boy was
rather long I rang again, and in he marched with one
eye shut, his face all chalk-lined, and carrying a
Jap. sandal on a tray—no tea—and impudently offered
my friend the sandal, bowing to the ground and retiring
backwards from the room. They had boycotted her.
Once they get sulky you must either grant their demands
or dismiss them, for they are then useless and an annoy-
ance. Once I threatened to imprison my boy, and he
coolly replied, “ Gaol more better.” You must allow then-
ample time every afternoon to bath in warm water ; about
this they are very particular. As soon as all is cleared up
at night, out goes the boy till daylight next morning, and,
should you want supper, you must provide it for yourself.
If you leave the plates for the boy to wash up, you’ll cer-
tainly hear about it next morning in anything but choice
language. When their friends call, your work is sus-
pended, and you endure their noisy merriment Visitors
must be allowed. This is the conduct of the assigned
boy gathered from many experiences. But, when the
Jap is free, all this changes, and he becomes a very good,
clean obedient servant. While he is assigned on small
wages you “cop out.” [Not astonishing Assignment
evidently means slavery.— Ed. B.]

Wliat nine months “ solitary ” means in
Nj.S. W. When sentenced to three years or up-
wards the prisoner is kept in the local gaol (where
convicted) until a batch is ready, when he, with
others, is transferred to Darlinghurst, which is a
“ distributing ” gaol, The journey is made in
irons. In Darlinghurst he is kept in what is
called “ transit ” until he has passed the doctor as
fit for separate treatment (i.e., solitary confine-
ment). When the Comptroller - General deter-
mines to what gaol he shall be sent for his
nine months’ solitary, he, with others, with
rivetted irons on his ankles, is sent by rail to
the chosen spot. For 23 hours out of the 24
lie is confined in a silent cell, on a limited diet,
hearing no voice. One hour in the 24 he has for
(solitary and silent) exercise. At the end of six
months, he gets an extra hour per day for (solitary
and silent) exercise, and is allowed 2oz. of coarse
sugar daily. During the nine months he neither
receives nor sends letters. If his wife or children
die he hears nothing of it. At the end of nine
riionths he mingles with other prisoners, and is
often a fit subject for a lunatic asylum.

Seen and Unseen, a Brisbane spiritualistic
paper, conducted by Theodore Wood, lately
printed “a 20-minutes’ address by Jesus of Naza-
reth, specially delivered for this paper, and taken
down in shorthand from the medium’s lips.”
Ordinary journalism has evidently no show with
the Mellon or pumpkin variety. Singularly
enough, however, the sentiments ascribed to Seen
and Unseen’s interviewer were precisely similar
to the mediumistic hogwash which any ghost
of any Smith can twaddle by the hour. The
signature may be. the signature of Esau, but
the voice is a. ways the voice of Cissy.

SYDNEY CABBIES!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In 1879, the year of Sydney’s International
Exhibition, the city’s cabs increased from 500 to
700, and with prosperous years the number rose
till ’B9 saw the maximum—l32o. “ The depres-
sion ” has knocked off the odd 320. Eorty years
ago there were only three two-wheelers. The
driver of one of them, Bannister, still on the stand
at the Exchange, is a cabman with a history. He
was a Eureka stockade, and escaped from the
Govt, troopers with his two horses and dray; and
the morning after the Dunbar wreck he helped
to hold the spar from which Johnson’s rescuer
was let down the precipitous face of “the Gap.”
Johnson was drawn up “shivering, in blue shirt
and blue trousers ; he would have drunk off the
whole of a bottle of brandy, but was only allowed
a sip.”
* * * *
A first-class cab costs over <£100; a good
horse, £l5. Expenses of two horses, cab repair, &c.,
mean up to 355. weekly. A cab-license costs per
year 305.; a driver’s license, 7s. 6d. Three-fourths
of the cabmen own
and drive; many
mortgage their cabs
and horses. One-
fourth rent cabs from
owners of yards about
the city; the rate is
6s. 6d. per day for a
cab and one horse,
10s. for a cab and two
horses.
* * *
Cab - horses which
have no stable-mate
often do 365 days’
work a year. Three-
fourths work Sun-
days ; daily hours are
ten or twelve. The
oldest nag is a 24-
year-old grey, with
18 years’ cab-service.
“ He’s a good ’un yet,” says his driver affec-
tionately. There are several ex - steeplechasers
“on the ranks.”
* * * *
Here is a typical cabman’s story : “ One
pourin’ night, when only ‘ Mad Micky ’ and me
was on the stand, two rather swell blokes kiddin’
drunk rolls up out of the rain and mist, and one on
’em sez, ‘Drive us to Coogee,’ namin’ ‘ the Castle.’
So up I gets and drives ’em. Well, when we gets
near the big place they pulls me up and gets
out and I see they’re both sober. One bloke gives
me a bob. ‘ What’s this for ? ’ I sez, feelin’ mad
enough to get off and pull their tongues out. ‘ It’s
all you’ll get,’ he sez, ‘ and if yer don’t like it and
make a row we’ll deal it out to yer.’ In a moment
I’m down and stoushing him on the point. He
falls and the other bloke bolts. I holds my man
till he comes to and I sez, ‘ Yer’ve got the biggest
cheek of any man wot ever wore clothes ; how am
I to make a livin’ drivin’ you out ’ere for a bob ? ’
I knowed then he didn’t belong to the big place,
so I makes ’im get into the cab and he tells me
where to drive. Passin’ a paddock he outs —but
I’m with ’im. He knowed just where to drop on
the holes i n the palin’s every time—but I was with
’im. He comes to a lane, slips down it, then turns
into another and like a wmk he’s over a back
gate. Just as he lands I grabs his ’at. ‘ I’ll keep
this,’ I sez, ‘in case yer want to give me brusher.’
He swarms up a back balcony-post, swings from
the balcony on to a shed roof, runs along it, and
disappears in another paddock. I goes back to
the cab with the ’at; I have that ’at still.”
* * * *
A cabman’s commentary on the Law :—“ It
pays to have a s’licitor, because a man always
feels he’s summoned unjust until he’s beat, and a
s’licitor often fogs the beak—’specially if the beak
has been in a perlitical depitition or convival
evenin’ the night afore. Once when two on us
were joggin’ down Pitt-street we was brought up.
The other bloke had a lawyer to defend him and
got fined 30 bob; I defended meself and got
two quid. The beak sez, ‘ I alwus find the wurst
men defend theirselves—two quid,’ he sez. Oh,
the beaks and lawyers wink the other eye at each
other all right; I ken prove it. One on us blokes
were loiterin’ in King-street one day and was
nabbed and brought up and charged with loiterin’
in King-street. *Oh ! ’ sez our bloke’s s’licitor,
‘ the charge don’t say wot King-street—Newtown,
or Melbourne, or ’Evin or Herth.’ ‘Do you press
that point ? ’ asks the beak, just as simple as if it
were the fusst time he had heerd of it. ‘Of course
I do,’ sez the lawyer. ‘Case discharged,’ sez the
beak. Lor, didn’t our bloke stare ! He didn’t
know where ’e are for a month after.”
* * * »
Sometimes a cabman wins :—“I was drivin’ a
fare down one on ’em Bondi ’ills when a copper
yells out to me like he was struck ratty. ‘ Wot
are yer barkin’ at,’ I sez. ‘ Stop !’ he sez. I stop.
‘ I’ll show yer,’ ’e sez, ‘ wot I’m barkin’ hat; wot’s
yer name and address? ’ ‘ There’s my number,’ I
sez, ‘ that’s all yer want. ‘ Where’s yer ’ome ? ’
he sez. ‘My ’ome is wherehever hi am when the
nightfalls on me,’ I sez. ‘Ho!’ he sez, "ow
funny you’are.’ ‘’Opes yer like it, ole feller,’ I
sez. So he takes my number, and soon enough
brings me up afore his own fancy magistrate—the
bloke wot fines cabmen <£2 for what the other
beaks fine ’em 2s. 6d. for—an’ he swears agin me
for furious drivin’ —nearly running over people—
a very strong tale. ‘ ’Ow fast was he goin’ ? ’ asks
the magistrate. ‘Tour miles an hour, yer wur-
ship,’ he sez. Blue me, that’s where the funny
part come in, and it made even the beak laugh. I
was discharged that time.”
* * * *
The cabman doesn’t like the “sham toff ” who
“ comes up and sez, ‘ cabby ! Railway Station !
drive like the wind ! ’ You cut out a twenty-five
minute drive in a quarter of an hour. He gets
out; knocks his cigar-ash off on the wheel; sez
‘ Fine turn-out you’ve got, cabby ! deuced fine
turn-out! ’ and, blue me, if he don’t give you a
shilling. ‘ Well,’l says, ‘yer ’ort to keep a cab
of yer own!’ “No doosid imperdence, cabby,’
he sez, “ no doosid imperdence, or I’ll give you in
charge ! ’ Whenever you see a cabman with his
hat on one side and scratchin’ his head, wond’rin’-
like, it’s because he’s just had a fare like that.”
* * * *
Men tell cabby to “go like h—1”; women first
say, “Is your horse quiet, driver?” and next,
“ Please drive steadily. ” There are twenty male
fares for every woman; the clergy don’t cab it.
In the rosy days of 10 or 15 years ago, an old
Exchange-stand cabman sometimes fluked =£lo a
day, often £2O a week, and always averaged £G
per week the year through. Now, despite his
regular customers, he is in luck’s way to make a
weekly =£4. Fifteen years ago a certain Queens-
land squatter, who used to stay at the Exchange
Hotel, would for three months together give his
cabman never less per week than £l2. On leaving
Sydney, when driven to the wharf, he would pre-
sent cabby with a parting “fiver.” When the
banks smashed in ’93, the excited depositors rush-
ing hither and thither made good business ; but,
when the hot hubbub died away into cold depres-
sion, the cabmen suffered terribly. Many stood in
the ranks for two days of 12 hours each without
earning a shilling. The best-known averaged but
255. a week for many weeks. Things have im-
proved a little, yet Christmas last was to many
cabmen as poor as midwinter. Country visitors to
town have become quite as careful with their silver
as town people.
S. A. MILLS.
An English curiosity is a policeman whose
heart beats so loud (mitral murmur) as to be aud-
ible through his clothes at a distance of several
feet.
A farmer at Tooradin, Yic., lately found a
hive of bees, in full working order, inside the
dried-up carcase of a dead bullock. The find
panned out a kerosene-tinful of honey. Another
testimony to Biblical truth ! See Judges, xiv.,
where Samson found a hive of bees in a lion’s
carcase, and bet the Philistines 30 sheets and 30
change of garments that they couldn’t guess his
riddle : “ Out of the eater came forth meat, and
out of the strong came forth sweetness.” Where-
upon the Philistines stewed their brains over the
puzzle for a week, and finally got so mad
that they went to Samson’s wife and threatened
to burn her father’s house down if she didn’t
screw the answer out of Sam. Accordingly she
worried the poor devil till he told her, “ because
she lay sore upon him,” and Sam lost his bet—
which he paid by going down to Ashkelon, with
“the Spirit of the Lord upon him,” and slaying
30 men whose clothes he collared. “If ye had not
plowed with my .heifer,” he told the Philistine
bookies, “ ye had not found out my riddle”—and
“ Samson’s wife was given to his companion whom
he had used as his friend,” which was a nice,
friendly thing to do.
Vie. railway department has posted a notice
“ trespassers will be prosecuted” at the
an unfenced cutting, 70ft. deep, on the Outrun
line. The first trespasser was dead when he
reached the bottom, so proceedings weren’t taken
in his case; but two others who went over the
department’s man-trap in the dark are slowly
recovering in the hospital, and it is understood
that, when they are convalescent, what remains
of them will be pursued with the utmost rigor of
the law.
Strenuous efforts are being made by moral
Melbourne shopkeepers to wipe out the itinerant
flower - sellers, particularly the females. The
Mayor told a deputation “ he was quite aware of
the danger of moral contamination to which the
she] flower-sellers were exposed.” Hem !
Stout, K.C.M.G. (Maoriland) : “ There is
less freedom in the N.Z. Parliament than in any
other Parliament in the civilised world.” Stout is
evidently going downhill mentally. When a man
begins to lose his powers of expression and his
sense of proportion, and has to emphasise himself
by saying that the small thing he objects to is the
worst or the biggest or the ugliest thing in all the
woild, he is obviously decaying. When his mental
decay gets very bad ne loses his hold of his super-
latives, and then he tries to emphasise himself by
putting big capital letters all over his communica-
tions. That is another stage of the complaint.
After that he begins to write all in capitals, and
to say that everything is the most dreadful thing
on earth, and next he drops out of sight altogether.
Sunshine and Fvesh Air.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The natives of New Guinea are clothed principally in
sunshine and fresh air.—Rev. William Watson.
There’s a charming little land
Where the skies are soft and bland, '
Where the folks are mostly gay and black and
bare ;
Where you court your summer girl
With her fluffy hair a-curl
In a costume sweet of —sunshine and fresh air.
Should she chance to be a swell,
Your entrancing Guinea belle,
She may haply sport a feather in her hair;
For the rest —from chubby toes
To her charming chubby nose
She’ll be muffled close—m sunshine and fresh air
“ Sweet ” (the singer sang), “ O sweet
Are the waves that wash her feet !”
What had he said had he but seen his fair
Pirouette in dainty pose,
On her dusky dainty toes,
In a charming garb of—sunshine and fresh air ?
Had he seen the dusky maid
In her simple charms arrayed,
Wed her chosen in a style both chaste and rare—
Forty bridesmen (comme il faut),
Forty bridesmaids in a row,
Smiling sweetly —dressed in sunshine and fresh
air 1
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
A meek-eyed flower-girl was she
In rather seedy socks
With blooms, revived ingeniously,
All scattered in a box.
Alas ! we know her story well:
Poor soul, she tempted and she fell.

Two Cockies .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Their selections adjoined. Some work had
been done; slab houses and bark sheds had been
built, and a few acres on the ridge cleared and
fenced ; but the initial energy had waned and the
joy of grubbing and splitting on their own land
departed. Any reason was now good enough for
adjourning the planned improvements.
* * * *
It was quite a month since Steve started to
grub the big ironbark behind the shed. This day
ho began with a brave heart; got down among the
roots and into the hole as large as a tank; dug
tediously at the red clay that covered the black-
snake roots, jabbed at the stones that blunted his
tools, the lace of fibres that barred his way, till at
last he was able to use the axe effectively to sever
one more of the giant’s claws. But the sound of
the axe aroused Abe, who in the next paddock had
been regarding a broken fence with an uneasy
feeling that he ought to mend that “someday.”
With his hands in the top of his pants, he strolled
over to where Steve was working, climbed the
fence, squatted on the top rail, pulled out a plug
of tobacco and a knife, and began to fill his pipe.
This made it necessary for Steve to lay down the
mattock and fill his pipe.
“ I wouldn’t bust myself if I were you, Steve, to
iut that tap. Put a rope in her, and we’ll pull her
down.” -
“So 1 was thinking, Abe; but I ain’t got a
lope, and you ain’t got a rope; so what’s the use
talking?”
“ Then let her rip till a ‘southerly ’ comes and
locks her out.”
The two men surveyed the tree, the hole, the
heap of dirt, in silent, smoking contemplation.
■Steve’s muscles relaxed their tension ; his sense of
obligation to “see that tree down before dark”
weakened. Abe expectorated at the hole at the
tap-root and missed. Steve, spitting in social
lompetition, also missed ; but spoken words could
not have better expressed their opinion of the job
before them.
“ What say, Steve, if we go and have a look at
that blaokbutt near the high rock ? I'll bet a
crown there’s a bees’ nest in the third limb.”
“ You don’t say! Why, I’ve looked that tree
over and over scores of times.”
“ It’s there right enough. One of my kids found
it going to school. You take your cross-cut, and
1 11 bring an axe and bucket.”
“ 1 don’t care if I do, Abe ; it’s too hot for hard
graft anyway.”
Day after day this pretence of work was gone
through ; when it was not a bees -nest to chop out
it was a wallaby-hunt, or an unbranded calf, or
a cow near calving that required running in. No
crops were planted worth speaking of ; no money
earned that could be calculated. How food of the
very roughest was provided remained a secret
known only to their wives and the trusting store-
keeper.
* * * *
It chanced that a bullock had died in the
swamp, and the two men going down to skin it
returned with the hide on their shoulders, mightily
pleased, for it promised a few shillings of ready
money. Drawing near home they could hear c in-
tending cries, the shrill, voices of their wives
quarrelling.
“ I tell you my dad was a real gentleman ; kept
a hotel; and we two servants —yes, two ser-
vants.”
“I’m sure ! An old man who hung round the
stables, and an orphan child who ran away because
you did not give her enough to eat. Hotel, indeed !
Say grog-shanty ! ”
“Grog-shanty yourself ! It’s like your impu-
dence. Who are you, I should like to know? Why,
your father was only a poor cocky, and you never
had a shoe to your foot till you wore your old
man’s bluchers ! ”
The men, coming up, tried to pacify them ; but
only to be assailed in their turn as weak-spirited
creatures.
This was not the first outbreak on the part of
the two wives, but, so far, it was the most serious ;
and the men’s soothing voices were drowned in the
screams of the women as, half-led, half-dragged,
they were taken back to their doors.
* * * *
So the feud began. That day the women
reiterated to their husbands all the wrongs they
had suffered ; the utter worthlessness of the other
family ; how articles had been borrowed and never
returned ; how “she,” that woman they had done
so much for, had said this, had said that; how the
husband was a lazy good-for-nothing, who idled
his own time and other people’s—till at last the
men, from being peace-makers, were drawn into
the strife and for days after avoided each other.
One day the pent fire burst forth. Abe was on
one side of the fence ; Steve on the other.
“ Where’s that swingle-tree I lent you, Blake ? ”
“ I don’t know, and I don’t care. Where’s the
money you owe me for half the dividing-fence ?”
“Pay for half that fence ! That dry-rotted,
white-ant-eaten fraud ! Why, it would drop to
bits if a cow looked at it. It never was worth a
curse, and if you had any pride you’d burn it.
Pay for it, indeed ! You just ante-up that swingle-
tree ! ”
“Now, none of your pointing, Pye; just you
pay for that fence, or I’ll make you. ”
For hours, with wearying iteration, the discus-
sion continued, the voices rising higher and
higher. The sins they had done, the sins they
had not done—all were raked up in long impreca-
tion. In a fury of mind, and perhaps to dare his
adversary, Abe harnessed up his horses to the
plough, using the swingletree so much spoken of.
Up and down the land the horses toiled, Steve
standing by raging and impotent.
“ You call that ploughing ? If I couldn’t turn a
better furrow than that I’d drown myself.”
Abe plodded on regardless of criticism ; and
Steve, fire in his eye and shaking with excitement,
also harnessed up his horses. Presently the two
men were both ploughing, each in his own orchard.
All day long they toiled, with only the necessary
pauses to rest the horses, each waiting for the
other to “ knock off.” Dead to fatigue, regardless
of blistering sunshine, they labored till darkness
sent them indoors.
Next morning the sun had not risen when they
recommenced their task ; each covertly surveying
the other’s work and condemning it as altogether
bad; each resolved to show his superiority, both
in quantity and quality, as a laborer.
A week later Abe'commenced cutting a Scotch
drain at an angle across his orchard. Its outlet
was at the dividing-line of the lower paddocks.
“The scoundrel ! ” cried Steve to his wife ; “ he
wants to run all his drainage on to my grass. I’ll
be level with him.” Thereupon Steve cut a drain
to meet Abe’s, and no damage resulted to either of
them, but much benefit to their fruit-trees.
Abe’s oranges had the scale; he rigged up a
spraying-machine and cleaned them. Steve, after
scouting at the new-fangled foolery, followed the
example. For the competition in work had
developed into a competition in orchards ; early
and late the men were pruning, harrowing, mulch-
ing, chipping. At evening, design or accident
always brought them to the dividing-fence.
“ Where’s that swingletree I lent you, Blake?”
“ Where’s that money you owe me for half the
fence ? ”
‘ ‘ Call yourself a man ? ”
“ Not if you are one, you loafer.”
So they scolded and railed night after night.
* * * *
Month after month, year after year the
feud lasted ; the rage of the men seeming to feed
on their nightly brawl. And financially they
prospered ; no two orchards like theirs were to be
seen for miles around ; bigger and bigger cheques
came from Sydney agents ; and if Steve was the
first to open a bank-account, Abe led the way in
buying a sulky. Then Steve built a brick house,
and Abe went one better with a stone one. Steve’s
boy went to boarding-school; Abe replied by send-
ing two girls to college. The same pride was
shown in their live-stock. When Abe bought
Clydesdales, Steve bought Suffolk Punches; Ayr-
shire cows in one paddock stared at Jerseys in the
other ; and the Langshan rooster crowing in one
yard was promptly challenged by the neighboring
Leghorn.
But neither time nor prosperity blunted the
animosity of the men. Long ago the wives would
have made it up; but the husbands would hear no
good of each other or the other family, and
after their nightly brawl retired to their suppers
with unabated wrath, heaping imprecations on
“ that blanked scoundrel Blake!” “that worth-
less loafer Pye ! ”
One summer, after a short sickness, Abe Blake
died, and the widow, selling out to advantage, left
the district. Steve took no pains to conceal his
satisfaction.
“That’s one good riddance, Missus. I believe a
bad neighbor is the greatest curse in God’s world.
We’ve had no peace now for seven years.”
Steve welcomed the new owner with effusion ;
but his wife noticed that he now slackened greatly
in his work. He often wandered aimlessly about
—up and down the track by the dividing fence by
preference —muttering to himself. When he came
in, the wife would inquire what he was saying.
“ What a blessing it is, Missus ! What a bless-
ing it is to have a good neighbor at last ! ”
* * * *
The orchard was neglected ; weeds and scale,
aphis and white lice bred unchecked. Steve did
not seem to notice or care about that, or anything
else. Mrs. Pye looked on in despair at her home
and orchard going' to rack and ruin ; nothing
roused the once-energetic Steve from his nerveless
apathy. And so the family" might have drifted
into the back-wash .of disaster from which they
had emerged, but. for the lucky chance that in-
duced Steve to join a lodge. Of course, his wife
opposed the idea ; she knew what he was when he
got into company ; and her forebodings were not
belied, for had it not been for the sobriety .of the
horse the master would hardly have reached home
that night. The wife, sitting up anxiously, heard
the gate open, and, waiting for the expected foot-
fall, heard instead her husband’s voice in loud
altercation, and in the words she could catch there
sounded to her something familiar. Opening the
door, she crept out on to the verandah. It was
bright moonlight, and there, over by the dividing-
fence, was Steve gesticulating with waving arms
and stamping feet.
“ Where’s that swingletree I lent you, you
scoundrel ? . . Want me to pay for half your
rotten old fence, do you ? . . I’ll see you fur-
ther first, and your fence, too ! . . Call your-
self a man ? ” and so on. ’Twas the old, old story
once again.
Steve came into the house at last, hot with his
old rage and scorn, and the next day and for many
days lie slaved with old-time vigor to overtake the
arrears of work. But gradually he slowed off ;
the impetus given by his outburst slackened ; and
his former lethargy returned— only to be dissipated
later on by another fit of drunkenness and an-
other verbal war with the shade of Abe Blake over
the dividing-fence. So that, in time, Steve’s wife
came to regard his periodic visits to the lodge as a
wise dispensation of Providence, and, waiting
anxiously in her bed at night for his return, she
listened with sleepless eyes till the old familiar
words rang out on the night.
“Where’s that swingletree I lent you, you
scoundrel ? Want me to pay for half your rotten
old fence ? ”
Only then she turned to rest, closing her eyes on
the pillow with satisfied content, and murmuring
to herself : “ Thank God ! Steve’s drunk again ! ’

Dear Bulletin, — Vic. Govt, is preparing
to spend more thousands on wire-netting to cope
with the rabbits. I lay big odds on bunny. After
trapping thousands of him I thought I knew
something about his capabilities, but two of him
surprised me the other day—up near Darraweit
Guim, on Deep Creek. A farmer’s son and I were
out shooting a few, when the dogs chased a couple
into a hollow stump, as upright as they make ’em.
The dogs entered after them but couldn’t reach
either. I stooped down and looked up, and found
the stump hollow all the way up. I stand six feet,
the farmer’s son about sft. Bin, He got on my
shoulders, raised himself to the top and looked
down. He said he could see one bunny but
couldn’t reach him. We got some bark and dead
wood and lit a .fire in the stump, the inside of
which, being ant-eaten and dry, blazed up fiercely,
For a few seconds there was tremendous squealing
inside, then one bunny fell down into the fire, and,
though badly singed, made off, and the other
scrambled, out of the top , and proceeded to make
himself scarce likewise. The dogs caught both,
By the way, rabbits round here have splendid
layers of fat round the kidneys—while the farmers
are felling sheoak trees to keep the cattle alive till
the spring. This isn-’t a lie, and it may surprise
those who don’t know bunny.—F.C.
